http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Guas%C3%BA_Front
<p>The <b>Guasú Front</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Left-wing_politics" title="Left-wing politics">left-wing</a> <a href="/wiki/Electoral_alliance" title="Electoral alliance">electoral alliance</a> in <a href="/wiki/Paraguay" title="Paraguay">Paraguay</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Paraguayan_general_election,_2013" title="Paraguayan general election, 2013">general election in 2013</a>. It was formed in 2010 by a merger of the centre-left <a href="/wiki/Patriotic_Alliance_for_Change" title="Patriotic Alliance for Change">Patriotic Alliance for Change</a> and the far-left <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Unitary_Space_%E2%80%93_People%27s_Congress&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Unitary Space – People's Congress">Unitary Space – People's Congress</a>. It consists of eleven parties, including the <a href="/wiki/Party_for_a_Country_of_Solidarity" title="Party for a Country of Solidarity">Party for a Country of Solidarity</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Paraguayan_Communist_Party" title="Paraguayan Communist Party">Paraguayan Communist Party</a>. It is led by impeached former president <a href="/wiki/Fernando_Lugo" title="Fernando Lugo">Fernando Lugo</a>, who also ran as a senatorial candidate. Its presidential candidate was <a href="/wiki/An%C3%ADbal_Carrillo" title="Aníbal Carrillo">Aníbal Carrillo</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Left-wing_politics
<p>In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Left-right_politics" title="Left-right politics">left-right politics</a>, <b>left-wing politics</b> are political positions or activities that accept or support <a href="/wiki/Social_equality" title="Social equality">social equality</a>, often in opposition to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Social_hierarchy" title="Social hierarchy">social hierarchy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Social_inequality" title="Social inequality">social inequality</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">2</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lukes_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lukes-3">3</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">4</a></sup> It typically involves a concern for those in society who are perceived as disadvantaged relative to others and an assumption that there are unjustified inequalities that need to be reduced or abolished.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lukes_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lukes-3">3</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Left-right_politics
<p>The <b>left-right</b> <a href="/wiki/Political_spectrum" title="Political spectrum">political spectrum</a> is a system of classifying political positions, <a href="/wiki/Ideology" title="Ideology">ideologies</a> and <a href="/wiki/Political_party" title="Political party">parties</a>. <a href="/wiki/Left-wing_politics" title="Left-wing politics">Left-wing politics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Right-wing_politics" title="Right-wing politics">right-wing politics</a> are often presented as opposed, although a particular individual or group may take a left-wing stance on one matter and a right-wing stance on another. In France, where the terms originated,  has been called "the party of movement" and  "the party of order."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">2</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">3</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">4</a></sup> The intermediate stance is called <a href="/wiki/Centrism" title="Centrism">centrism</a> and a person with such a position is a <b>moderate</b>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Political_spectrum
<p>A <b>political spectrum</b> is a system of classifying different <a href="/wiki/Politics" title="Politics">political</a> positions upon one or more <a href="/wiki/Geometry" title="Geometry">geometric</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coordinate_axis" title="Coordinate axis">axes</a> that symbolize independent political dimensions.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Politics
<p><b>Politics</b> is the practice and theory of influencing other people on a civic or individual level. More narrowly, it refers to achieving and exercising positions of <a href="/wiki/Governance" title="Governance">governance</a> — organized control over a human community, particularly a <a href="/wiki/State_(polity)" title="State">state</a>. A variety of methods are employed in politics, which include promoting one's own political views among people, <a href="/wiki/Negotiation" title="Negotiation">negotiation</a> with other political subjects, making <a href="/wiki/Law" title="Law">laws</a>, and exercising <a href="/wiki/Force_(law)" title="Force">force</a>, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Warfare" title="Warfare">warfare</a> against adversaries. Politics is exercised on a wide range of social levels, from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clans" title="Clans">clans</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tribes" title="Tribes">tribes</a> of traditional societies, through modern <a href="/wiki/Local_government" title="Local government">local governments</a>, <a href="/wiki/Company" title="Company">companies</a> and institutions up to <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_state" title="Sovereign state">sovereign states</a>, to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_politics" title="International politics">international level</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Governance
<p><b>Governance</b> refers to "all processes of governing, whether undertaken by a government, market, or network, whether over a family, tribe, formal or informal organization, or territory, and whether through laws, norms, power, or language."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup> It relates to processes and decisions that seek to define actions, grant power, and verify performance.</p>
<p>In general terms, governance occurs in three broad ways:<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[]</sup></p>
<p>To distinguish the term  from govern: "governance" is the concrete activity that reproduces a formal or informal organization. If the organization is a formal one, governance is primarily about what the relevant "governing body" does. If the organization is an informal one, such as a market, governance is primarily about the rules and norms that guide the relevant activity. Whether the organization is a geo-political entity, a corporate entity, a socio-political entity, or an informal one, its governance is the way the rules and actions are produced, sustained, and regulated.</p>
<p></p>
<p></p>
<p>The word  derives from the Greek verb κυβερνάω [] which means  and was used for the first time in a metaphorical sense by Plato. It then passed on to <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> and then on to many languages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">2</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Latin
<p><b>Latin</b> is an <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">ancient</a> <a href="/wiki/Italic_languages" title="Italic languages">Italic language</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">2</a></sup> originally spoken by the <a href="/wiki/List_of_ancient_Italic_peoples" title="List of ancient Italic peoples">Italic</a> <a href="/wiki/Latins_(Italic_tribe)" title="Latins">Latins</a> in <a href="/wiki/Latium" title="Latium">Latium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Ancient Rome</a>. Along with most <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Europe" title="Languages of Europe">European languages</a>, it is a descendant of the ancient <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_language" title="Proto-Indo-European language">Proto-Indo-European language</a>. Influenced by the <a href="/wiki/Etruscan_language" title="Etruscan language">Etruscan language</a> and using the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_Alphabet" title="Greek Alphabet">Greek Alphabet</a> as a basis, it took form as what is recognizable as Latin in the <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italian peninsula</a>. Modern <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">Romance languages</a> are continuations of dialectal forms of the language. Additionally many students, scholars, and some members of the <a href="/wiki/Minister_(Christianity)" title="Minister">Christian clergy</a> speak it fluently, and it is still taught in some primary, secondary and post-secondary educational institutions around the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">3</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">4</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Classical_antiquity
<p><b>Classical antiquity</b> is a broad term for a long period of cultural <a href="/wiki/History" title="History">history</a> centered on the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a>, comprising the interlocking civilizations of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">ancient Greece</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">ancient Rome</a>, collectively known as the <a href="/wiki/Greco-Roman_world" title="Greco-Roman world">Greco-Roman world</a>. It is the period in which Greek and Roman society flourished and wielded great influence throughout Europe, North Africa and the Middle East.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History
<p><b>History</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JosephJanda_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-JosephJanda-2">2</a></sup> is the study of the past, specifically how it relates to humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">3</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">4</a></sup> It is an <a href="/wiki/Umbrella_term" title="Umbrella term">umbrella term</a> that relates to past events as well as the discovery, collection, organization, and presentation of <a href="/wiki/Information" title="Information">information</a> about these events. The term includes <a href="/wiki/Universe#History_of_the_Universe" title="Universe">cosmic</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geological_history_of_the_Earth" title="Geological history of the Earth">geologic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Organism#History_of_life" title="Organism">organic history</a>, but is often generically implied to mean <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_world" title="History of the world">human history</a>. Scholars who write about history are called <a href="/wiki/Historian" title="Historian">historians</a>. Events occurring prior to written record are considered <a href="/wiki/Prehistory" title="Prehistory">prehistory</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Umbrella_term
<p>An <b>umbrella term</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Terminology" title="Terminology">term</a> used to cover a broad number of functions or items that all fall under a single common category.  is also called a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hypernym" title="Hypernym">hypernym</a>. For example,  is an umbrella term that encompasses <a href="/wiki/Cryptography" title="Cryptography">cryptography</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cryptanalysis" title="Cryptanalysis">cryptanalysis</a>, among other fields. Similarly, an <a href="/wiki/Umbrella_organization" title="Umbrella organization">umbrella organization</a> is a central and coordinating body representing a number of smaller, separate bodies.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Terminology
<p><b>Terminology</b> is the study of terms and their use. <b>Terms</b> are <a href="/wiki/Word" title="Word">words</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Compound_word" title="Compound word">compound words</a> that in specific contexts are given specific meanings, meanings that may deviate from the meaning the same words have in other contexts and in everyday language. The discipline Terminology studies among other things how such terms of art come to be and their interrelationships within a culture. Terminology differs from <a href="/wiki/Lexicography" title="Lexicography">lexicography</a> in studying concepts, conceptual systems, and their labels, whereas lexicography study words and their meanings.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Word
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Linguistics" title="Linguistics">linguistics</a>, a <b>word</b> is the smallest element that may be uttered in isolation with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semantic" title="Semantic">semantic</a> or <a href="/wiki/Pragmatics" title="Pragmatics">pragmatic</a> content" title="Meaning">meaning</a>). This contrasts with a <a href="/wiki/Morpheme" title="Morpheme">morpheme</a>, which is the smallest unit of meaning but will not necessarily stand on its own. A word may consist of a single morpheme, or several, whereas a morpheme may not be able to stand on its own as a word. A complex word will typically include a <a href="/wiki/Root_(linguistics)" title="Root">root</a> and one or more <a href="/wiki/Affix" title="Affix">affixes</a>, or more than one root in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Compound_word" title="Compound word">compound</a>. Words can be put together to build larger elements of <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">language</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Phrase" title="Phrase">phrases</a>, <a href="/wiki/Clause" title="Clause">clauses</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sentence_(linguistics)" title="Sentence">sentences</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Linguistics
<p><b>Linguistics</b> is the <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">scientific</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup> study of <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">language</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">2</a></sup> There are broadly three aspects to the study, which include language form, language meaning, and language in context.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">3</a></sup> The earliest known activities in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Descriptive_linguistics" title="Descriptive linguistics">description of language</a> have been attributed to <a href="/wiki/P%C4%81%E1%B9%87ini" title="Pāṇini">Pāṇini</a> around 500 BCE, with his analysis of <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> in .<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">4</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Science
<p><b>Science</b> is a systematic enterprise that builds and organizes <a href="/wiki/Knowledge" title="Knowledge">knowledge</a> in the form of testable explanations and predictions about the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wilson_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-wilson-2">2</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">3</a></sup> In an older and closely related meaning, "science" also refers to a body of knowledge itself, of the type that can be rationally explained and reliably applied. A practitioner of science is known as a <a href="/wiki/Scientist" title="Scientist">scientist</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Knowledge
<p><b>Knowledge</b> is a familiarity with someone or something, which can include <a href="/wiki/Fact" title="Fact">facts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Information" title="Information">information</a>, <a href="/wiki/Description" title="Description">descriptions</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Skills" title="Skills">skills</a> acquired through <a href="/wiki/Experience" title="Experience">experience</a> or <a href="/wiki/Education" title="Education">education</a>. It can refer to the theoretical or practical understanding of a subject. It can be implicit or explicit; it can be more or less formal or systematic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>, the study of knowledge is called <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">epistemology</a>; the philosopher <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> famously defined knowledge as "<a href="/wiki/Justified_true_belief" title="Justified true belief">justified true belief</a>." However, no single agreed upon definition of knowledge exists, though there are numerous theories to explain it.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fact
<p>A <b>fact</b> is something that has really occurred or is actually the case. The usual test for a statement of fact is verifiability, that is whether it can be <a href="/wiki/Proof_(truth)" title="Proof">proven</a> to correspond to <a href="/wiki/Experience" title="Experience">experience</a>. Standard reference works are often used to check facts. <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">Scientific</a> facts are verified by repeatable <a href="/wiki/Experiment" title="Experiment">experiments</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proof_(truth)
<p>A <b>proof</b> is <a href="/wiki/Necessity_and_sufficiency" title="Necessity and sufficiency">sufficient</a> <a href="/wiki/Evidence" title="Evidence">evidence</a> or an <a href="/wiki/Argument" title="Argument">argument</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Truth" title="Truth">truth</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Proposition" title="Proposition">proposition</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">2</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">3</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">4</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Necessity_and_sufficiency
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>, <b>necessity</b> and <b>sufficiency</b> are implicational relationships between <a href="/wiki/Statement_(logic)" title="Statement">statements</a>.<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[]</sup> The assertion that one statement is a  <a href="/wiki/Condition_(philosophy)" title="Condition">condition</a> of another means that the former statement is true <a href="/wiki/If_and_only_if" title="If and only if">if and only if</a> the latter is true. That is, the two statements must be either simultaneously true or simultaneously false. In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ordinary_English" title="Ordinary English">ordinary English</a>, ‘necessary’ and ‘sufficient’ indicate relations between <a href="/wiki/Condition_(philosophy)" title="Condition">conditions</a> or states of affairs, not statements. Being male sibling is a necessary and sufficient condition for being a brother. Fred’s being a male sibling is necessary and sufficient for the truth of the statement that Fred is a brother.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Logic
<p><b>Logic</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-argumentative_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-argumentative-1">1</a></sup> has two meanings: first, it describes the use of valid <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reasoning" title="Reasoning">reasoning</a> in some activity; second, it names the <a href="/wiki/Normative" title="Normative">normative</a> study of reasoning or a branch thereof.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PopkinStroll1993_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-PopkinStroll1993-2">2</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jacquette2002_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-jacquette2002-3">3</a></sup> In the latter sense, it features most prominently in the subjects of <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">computer science</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reasoning
<p><b>Reason</b> is the capacity for <a href="/wiki/Consciousness" title="Consciousness">consciously</a> making sense of things, applying <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>, for establishing and verifying <a href="/wiki/Fact" title="Fact">facts</a>, and changing or justifying practices, institutions, and <a href="/wiki/Belief" title="Belief">beliefs</a> based on new or existing information.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup> It is closely associated with such characteristically <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">human</a> activities as <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a>, <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">language</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Art" title="Art">art</a>, and is normally considered to be a definitive characteristic of <a href="/wiki/Human_nature" title="Human nature">human nature</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">2</a></sup> The concept of reason is sometimes referred to as <a href="/wiki/Rationality" title="Rationality">rationality</a> and sometimes as <b>discursive reason</b>, in opposition to <a href="/wiki/Intuition_(psychology)" title="Intuition">intuitive reason</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">3</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Consciousness
<p><b>Consciousness</b> is the <a href="/wiki/Quality_(philosophy)" title="Quality">quality</a> or state of being aware of an external object or something within oneself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-van_Gulick2004_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-van_Gulick2004-2">2</a></sup> It has been defined as: <a href="/wiki/Sentience" title="Sentience">sentience</a>, <a href="/wiki/Awareness" title="Awareness">awareness</a>, <a href="/wiki/Subjectivity" title="Subjectivity">subjectivity</a>, the ability to <a href="/wiki/Experience" title="Experience">experience</a> or to <a href="/wiki/Feeling" title="Feeling">feel</a>, wakefulness, having a sense of <a href="/wiki/Self" title="Self">selfhood</a>, and the executive control system of the mind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Farthing1992Psychology_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Farthing1992Psychology-3">3</a></sup> Despite the difficulty in definition, many philosophers believe that there is a broadly shared underlying intuition about what consciousness is.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">4</a></sup> As <a href="/wiki/Max_Velmans" title="Max Velmans">Max Velmans</a> and Susan Schneider wrote in : "Anything that we are aware of at a given moment forms part of our consciousness, making conscious experience at once the most familiar and most mysterious aspect of our lives."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">5</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quality_(philosophy)
<p>A <b>quality</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup> is an attribute or a <a href="/wiki/Property_(philosophy)" title="Property">property</a>. Attributes are ascribable, by a subject, whereas properties are possessible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cargile.2C_1995_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cargile.2C_1995-2">2</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[]</sup> In contemporary <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a> the idea of qualities, and especially how to distinguish certain kinds of qualities from one another, remains controversial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cargile.2C_1995_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cargile.2C_1995-2">2</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Property_(philosophy)
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Modern_philosophy" title="Modern philosophy">modern philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>, a <b>property</b> is an attribute of an <a href="/wiki/Object_(philosophy)" title="Object">object</a>; a red object is said to have the property of redness. The property may be considered a form of object in its own right, able to possess other properties. A property however differs from individual objects in that it may be instantiated, and often in more than one thing. It differs from the logical/mathematical concept of <a href="/wiki/Class_(set_theory)" title="Class">class</a> by not having any concept of <a href="/wiki/Extensionality" title="Extensionality">extensionality</a>, and from the philosophical concept of <a href="/wiki/Class_(philosophy)" title="Class">class</a> in that a property is considered to be distinct from the objects which possess it. Understanding how different individual entities can in some sense have some of the same properties is the basis of the <a href="/wiki/Problem_of_universals" title="Problem of universals">problem of universals</a>. The terms <b>attribute</b> and <b>quality</b> have similar meanings.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Modern_philosophy
<p><b>Modern philosophy</b> is a category of <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a> that originated in <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western Europe</a> in the 17th century, and is now common worldwide. It is not a specific doctrine or school, although there are certain assumptions common to much of it, which helps to distinguish it from earlier <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Philosophy

http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Global_Underground
<p><b>Global Underground</b> is a music label founded in 1996 by <a href="/wiki/Andy_Horsfield" title="Andy Horsfield">Andy Horsfield</a> and James Todd. The label symbolized the international explosion of dance music during the 1990s and first manifesto for high-end DJs such as <a href="/wiki/Sasha_(DJ)" title="Sasha">Sasha</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paul_Oakenfold" title="Paul Oakenfold">Paul Oakenfold</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Digweed" title="John Digweed">John Digweed</a>, <a href="/wiki/Danny_Tenaglia" title="Danny Tenaglia">Danny Tenaglia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nick_Warren" title="Nick Warren">Nick Warren</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dave_Seaman" title="Dave Seaman">Dave Seaman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Darren_Emerson" title="Darren Emerson">Darren Emerson</a>, and <a href="/wiki/James_Lavelle" title="James Lavelle">James Lavelle</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Andy_Horsfield
<p><b>Andy Horsfield</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">British</a> entrepreneur best known for founding <a href="/wiki/Global_Underground" title="Global Underground">Global Underground</a> in 1996. In 2007 the record label was partially acquired by <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Sound" title="Ministry of Sound">Ministry of Sound</a> and Horsfield remained as head of the company. In 2008 he partnered with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_Palumbo" title="James Palumbo">James Palumbo</a> to start MOSHK America. Horsfield became the president of Ministry of Sound and <a href="/wiki/Hed_Kandi" title="Hed Kandi">Hed Kandi</a> for the US, Canada, and Mexico. In 2008 Ministry of Sound America released "<a href="/wiki/Let_Me_Think_About_It" title="Let Me Think About It">Let Me Think About It</a>", the longest running track on the Top 50 Dance Airplay chart and one of Horsfield's first achievements as President of Ministry of Sound America. He also owns Ruthless Entertainment, an Entertainment and New Tech company based in Singapore.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">2</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">3</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_Britain
<p><b>Great Britain</b>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">note 1</a></sup> also known as <b>Britain</b>, is an <a href="/wiki/Island" title="Island">island</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic Ocean</a> off the north-western coast of <a href="/wiki/Continental_Europe" title="Continental Europe">continental Europe</a>. It is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_islands_by_area" title="List of islands by area">ninth-largest island in the world</a> and the largest island in <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>. With a population of about 61 million people in 2011, it is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_islands_by_population" title="List of islands by population">third-most populous island</a> in the world, after <a href="/wiki/Java" title="Java">Java</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Honsh%C5%AB" title="Honshū">Honshū</a>. It is surrounded by over 1,000 smaller <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islands" title="Islands">islands</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islets" title="Islets">islets</a>. Politically,  refers to the island together with a number of surrounding islands, which constitute the territory of <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a>. The island of <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a> lies to its west.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">8</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">9</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ons_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-ons-11">10</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">11</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">12</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Island
<p>An <b>island</b>  or <b>isle</b>  is any piece of <a href="/wiki/Continent" title="Continent">sub-continental</a> land that is surrounded by <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a>. Very small islands such as emergent land features on <a href="/wiki/Atoll" title="Atoll">atolls</a> can be called <a href="/wiki/Islet" title="Islet">islets</a>, <a href="/wiki/Skerry" title="Skerry">skerries</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cay" title="Cay">cays</a> or keys. An <a href="/wiki/River_island" title="River island">island in a river</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Lake_island" title="Lake island">lake island</a> may be called an <a href="/wiki/Ait" title="Ait">eyot or ait</a>, or a <a href="/wiki/Holm_(island)" title="Holm">holm</a>. A grouping of geographically or geologically related islands is called an <a href="/wiki/Archipelago" title="Archipelago">archipelago</a>, e.g. the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Continent
<p>A <b>continent</b> is one of several very large <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Landmass" title="Landmass">landmasses</a> on <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>. They are generally identified by <a href="/wiki/Convention_(norm)" title="Convention">convention</a> rather than any strict criteria, with up to seven regions commonly regarded as continents. These are—from largest in size to smallest: <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a>, <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Australia_(continent)" title="Australia">Australia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NatlGeo_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-NatlGeo-1">1</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Landmass
<p><b>Land</b>, sometimes referred to as <b>dry land</b>, is the solid surface of the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>, that is not covered by <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup> The division between land and <a href="/wiki/Ocean" title="Ocean">ocean</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sea" title="Sea">sea</a>, or other bodies of water, is one of the most fundamental separations on the planet. The vast majority of human activity has historically occurred, and continues to occur, on land. For this reason, land, and particularly those areas of land that support <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hunting" title="Hunting">hunting</a>, and other human activities, may be invested with great emotional and cultural value. Furthermore, the life forms that exist primarily on land, including <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_plant" title="Terrestrial plant">terrestrial plants</a> and <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_animal" title="Terrestrial animal">terrestrial animals</a>, differ from those that exist primarily in large bodies of water. The demarcation between land and water varies. In some places, it is sharply defined by solid rock <a href="/wiki/Landform" title="Landform">landforms</a> coming directly to the edge of the water. In other places, land may slowly give way to marshes or swamps, with no clear point at which the land ends and a body of water has begun. In such places, the demarcation can further vary due to tides and weather, with the area where land and sea interact being called the "coastal zone".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">2</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth
<p><b>Earth</b> is the third <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planet</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>. It is the <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">densest</a> and fifth-largest of the eight planets in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>. It is also the largest of the Solar System's four <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_planet" title="Terrestrial planet">terrestrial planets</a>. It is sometimes referred to as the <a href="/wiki/World" title="World">world</a> or the Blue Planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-blueplanet_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-blueplanet-28">23</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet
<p>A <b>planet</b> is an <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_object" title="Astronomical object">astronomical object</a> <a href="/wiki/Orbit" title="Orbit">orbiting</a> a <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">star</a> or <a href="/wiki/Stellar_evolution#Stellar_remnants" title="Stellar evolution">stellar remnant</a> that is massive enough to be rounded by its own <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravity</a>, is not massive enough to cause <a href="/wiki/Thermonuclear_fusion" title="Thermonuclear fusion">thermonuclear fusion</a>, and has <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cleared_the_neighbourhood" title="Cleared the neighbourhood">cleared its neighbouring region</a> of <a href="/wiki/Planetesimal" title="Planetesimal">planetesimals</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-footnoteA_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-footnoteA-1">a</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAU_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-IAU-2">1</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WSGESP_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-WSGESP-3">2</a></sup> The term  is ancient, with ties to <a href="/wiki/History" title="History">history</a>, <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mythology" title="Mythology">mythology</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">religion</a>. The planets were originally seen by many early cultures as divine, or as emissaries of <a href="/wiki/Deity" title="Deity">deities</a>. As scientific knowledge advanced, human perception of the planets changed, incorporating a number of disparate objects. In 2006, the <a href="/wiki/International_Astronomical_Union" title="International Astronomical Union">International Astronomical Union</a> officially adopted a resolution <a href="/wiki/IAU_definition_of_planet" title="IAU definition of planet">defining planets</a> within the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>. This definition has been both praised and criticized and remains disputed by some scientists because it excludes many objects of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planemo" title="Planemo">planetary mass</a> based on where or what they orbit. Although eight of the planetary bodies discovered before 1950 remain "planets" under the modern definition, some celestial bodies, such as <a href="/wiki/Ceres_(dwarf_planet)" title="Ceres">Ceres</a>, <a href="/wiki/2_Pallas" title="2 Pallas">Pallas</a>, <a href="/wiki/3_Juno" title="3 Juno">Juno</a>, <a href="/wiki/4_Vesta" title="4 Vesta">Vesta</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a>, that were once considered planets by the scientific community are no longer viewed as such.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Astronomical_object
<p><b>Astronomical objects</b> or <b>celestial objects</b> are naturally occurring <a href="/wiki/Entity" title="Entity">physical entities</a>, associations or structures that current <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a> has demonstrated to exist in the <a href="/wiki/Observable_universe" title="Observable universe">observable universe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">2</a></sup> The term  is sometimes used interchangeably with . Typically, an astronomical body refers to a single, cohesive structure that is bound together by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravity</a>. Examples include the <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroids</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moons" title="Moons">moons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planets</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a>. Astronomical objects are gravitationally bound structures that are associated with a position in space, but may consist of multiple independent astronomical bodies or objects. These objects range from single planets to <a href="/wiki/Star_cluster" title="Star cluster">star clusters</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nebula" title="Nebula">nebulae</a> or entire galaxies. A <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">comet</a> may be described as a body, in reference to the frozen nucleus of ice and dust, or as an object, when describing the nucleus with its diffuse coma and tail.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Entity
<p>An <b>entity</b> is something that exists by itself, although it need not be of material existence. In particular, <a href="/wiki/Abstraction" title="Abstraction">abstractions</a> and <a href="/wiki/Legal_fiction" title="Legal fiction">legal fictions</a> are usually regarded as entities. In general, there is also no presumption that an entity is <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">animate</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abstraction
<p><b>Abstraction</b> is a process by which concepts are derived from the usage and classification of literal concepts, <a href="/wiki/First_principle" title="First principle">first principles</a>, or other methods. "An abstraction" is the product of this process—a concept that acts as a super-categorical noun for all subordinate concepts, and connects any related concepts as a  or .<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Langer_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Langer-1">1</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/First_principle
<p><b>In <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a></b>, first principles are referred to as <a href="/wiki/Axiom" title="Axiom">axioms</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Postulate" title="Postulate">postulates</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics
<p><b>Mathematics</b> is the abstract study of topics such as <a href="/wiki/Quantity" title="Quantity">quantity</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-OED-2">2</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Structure" title="Structure">structure</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kneebone_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kneebone-3">3</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Space" title="Space">space</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-OED-2">2</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Calculus" title="Calculus">change</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LaTorre_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-LaTorre-4">4</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ramana_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ramana-5">5</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ziegler_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ziegler-6">6</a></sup> There is a range of views among mathematicians and philosophers as to the exact scope and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Definition_of_mathematics" title="Definition of mathematics">definition of mathematics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mura_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mura-7">7</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Runge_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Runge-8">8</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantity
<p><b>Quantity</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Property_(philosophy)" title="Property">property</a> that can exist as a <a href="/wiki/Magnitude_(mathematics)" title="Magnitude">magnitude</a> or <a href="/wiki/Counting" title="Counting">multitude</a>. Quantities can be compared in terms of "more", "less" or "equal", or by assigning a numerical value in terms of a unit of measurement. Quantity is among the basic <a href="/wiki/Class_(philosophy)" title="Class">classes</a> of things along with <a href="/wiki/Quality_(philosophy)" title="Quality">quality</a>, <a href="/wiki/Substance_theory" title="Substance theory">substance</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Change_(mathematics)" title="Change">change</a>, and relation. Being a fundamental term, quantity is used to refer to any type of quantitative properties or attributes of things. Some quantities are such by their inner nature, while others are functioning as states of things such as heavy and light, long and short, broad and narrow, small and great, or much and little. A small quantity is sometimes referred to as a <b>quantulum</b>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Property_(philosophy)
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Modern_philosophy" title="Modern philosophy">modern philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>, a <b>property</b> is an attribute of an <a href="/wiki/Object_(philosophy)" title="Object">object</a>; a red object is said to have the property of redness. The property may be considered a form of object in its own right, able to possess other properties. A property however differs from individual objects in that it may be instantiated, and often in more than one thing. It differs from the logical/mathematical concept of <a href="/wiki/Class_(set_theory)" title="Class">class</a> by not having any concept of <a href="/wiki/Extensionality" title="Extensionality">extensionality</a>, and from the philosophical concept of <a href="/wiki/Class_(philosophy)" title="Class">class</a> in that a property is considered to be distinct from the objects which possess it. Understanding how different individual entities can in some sense have some of the same properties is the basis of the <a href="/wiki/Problem_of_universals" title="Problem of universals">problem of universals</a>. The terms <b>attribute</b> and <b>quality</b> have similar meanings.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Modern_philosophy
<p><b>Modern philosophy</b> is a category of <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a> that originated in <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western Europe</a> in the 17th century, and is now common worldwide. It is not a specific doctrine or school, although there are certain assumptions common to much of it, which helps to distinguish it from earlier <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Philosophy

http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tuy_Ph%C6%B0%E1%BB%9Bc_District
<p><b>Tuy Phước</b> is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Districts_of_Vietnam" title="Districts of Vietnam">rural district</a> of <a href="/wiki/B%C3%ACnh_%C4%90%E1%BB%8Bnh_Province" title="Bình Định Province">Bình Định Province</a> in the <a href="/wiki/South_Central_Coast" title="South Central Coast">South Central Coast</a> region of Vietnam. The district capital lies at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tuy_Ph%C6%B0%E1%BB%9Bc" title="Tuy Phước">Tuy Phước</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Statoids_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Statoids-2">2</a></sup> The district has 13 administrative units including the towns <a href="/wiki/Di%C3%AAu_Tr%C3%AC" title="Diêu Trì">Diêu Trì</a>, Tuy Phước and the communes Phước Thắng, Phước Hưng, Phước Hoà, Phước Quang, Phước Sơn, Phước Hiệp, Phước Lộc, Phước Thuận, Phước Nghĩa, Phước An và Phước Thành..</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Districts_of_Vietnam
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Provinces_of_Vietnam" title="Provinces of Vietnam">provinces</a> of <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a> are divided into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/District_(Vietnam)" title="District">districts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Provincial_city_(Vietnam)" title="Provincial city">provincial cities</a>, and <a href="/wiki/District-level_town_(Vietnam)" title="District-level town">district-level towns</a>. The administrative unit of the <a href="/wiki/Huy%E1%BB%87n" title="Huyện">huyện</a> dates from the 15th Century.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Provinces_of_Vietnam
<p><a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a> is divided into <b>58 <a href="/wiki/Province" title="Province">provinces</a></b> and <b>5 <a href="/wiki/Municipalities_of_Vietnam" title="Municipalities of Vietnam">municipalities</a></b> existing at the same level as provinces.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vietnam
<p><b>Vietnam</b>, officially the <b>Socialist Republic of Vietnam</b>, is the easternmost country on the <a href="/wiki/Indochina" title="Indochina">Indochina Peninsula</a> in <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a>. With an estimated 90.3 million inhabitants as of 2012, it is the world's <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_population" title="List of countries by population">13th-most-populous country</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Asian_countries_by_population" title="List of Asian countries by population">eighth-most-populous Asian country</a>. <a href="/wiki/Names_of_Vietnam" title="Names of Vietnam">The name Vietnam</a> translates as "Southern Viet", and was first officially adopted in 1802 by Emperor <a href="/wiki/Gia_Long" title="Gia Long">Gia Long</a> and again in 1945 with the founding of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_Vietnam" title="Democratic Republic of Vietnam">Democratic Republic of Vietnam</a> under <a href="/wiki/Ho_Chi_Minh" title="Ho Chi Minh">Ho Chi Minh</a>. The country is bordered by China to the north, <a href="/wiki/Laos" title="Laos">Laos</a> to the northwest, <a href="/wiki/Cambodia" title="Cambodia">Cambodia</a> to the southwest, and the <a href="/wiki/South_China_Sea" title="South China Sea">South China Sea</a> to the east.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">8</a></sup> Its capital city has been <a href="/wiki/Hanoi" title="Hanoi">Hanoi</a> since the reunification of <a href="/wiki/North_Vietnam" title="North Vietnam">North</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_Vietnam" title="South Vietnam">South Vietnam</a> in 1976.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indochina
<p><b>Indochina</b> or <b>Indo-China</b> is a peninsula in <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HMDOG_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-HMDOG-1">1</a></sup> lying roughly southwest of <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, and east of <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>. The name has its origins in the French  as a combination of the names of "India" and "China", referring to the location of the territory between those two countries, though the majority of people in the region are neither Chinese nor Indian. The term may also be used in <a href="/wiki/Biogeography" title="Biogeography">biogeography</a> for the "Indochinese Region", a major biogeographical region within the <a href="/wiki/Indomalaya_ecozone" title="Indomalaya ecozone">Indomalaya ecozone</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Southeast_Asia
<p><b>Southeast Asia</b> or <b>Southeastern Asia</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Subregion" title="Subregion">subregion</a> of <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a>, consisting of the countries that are geographically south of <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, east of <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, west of <a href="/wiki/New_Guinea" title="New Guinea">New Guinea</a> and north of <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup> The region lies on the intersection of <a href="/wiki/Plate_tectonics" title="Plate tectonics">geological plates</a>, with heavy seismic and volcanic activity. Southeast Asia consists of two geographic regions: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mainland_Southeast_Asia" title="Mainland Southeast Asia">Mainland Southeast Asia</a>, also known as <a href="/wiki/Indochina" title="Indochina">Indochina</a>, comprising <a href="/wiki/Cambodia" title="Cambodia">Cambodia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Laos" title="Laos">Laos</a>, Myanmar, <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Maritime_Southeast_Asia" title="Maritime Southeast Asia">Maritime Southeast Asia</a>, comprising <a href="/wiki/Brunei" title="Brunei">Brunei</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a>, <a href="/wiki/East_Timor" title="East Timor">East Timor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unitednations1_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-unitednations1-2">2</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template"></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Subregion
<p>A <b>subregion</b> is a part of a larger <a href="/wiki/Region" title="Region">region</a> or <a href="/wiki/Continent" title="Continent">continent</a> and is usually based on location. <a href="/wiki/Cardinal_direction" title="Cardinal direction">Cardinal directions</a>, such as south or southern, are commonly used to define a subregion.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Region
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Geography" title="Geography">geography</a>, <b>regions</b> are areas broadly divided by physical characteristics, human-impact characteristics, and the interaction of humanity and the environment. Geographic regions and sub regions are mostly described by their imprecisely defined, and sometimes transitory boundaries, except in human geography, where <a href="/wiki/Jurisdiction_(area)" title="Jurisdiction">jurisdiction</a> areas such as national borders are clearly defined in law.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geography
<p><b>Geography</b> is the branch of <a href="/wiki/Knowledge" title="Knowledge">knowledge</a> that studies the lands, the features, the inhabitants, and the phenomena of the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">2</a></sup> A literal translation would be "to describe or write about the Earth". The first person to use the word "geography" was <a href="/wiki/Eratosthenes" title="Eratosthenes">Eratosthenes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">3</a></sup> Four historical traditions in geographical research are <a href="/wiki/Spatial_analysis" title="Spatial analysis">spatial analysis</a> of the natural and the human phenomena, <a href="/wiki/Area_studies" title="Area studies">area studies</a>, study of the man-land relationship, and research in the <a href="/wiki/Earth_science" title="Earth science">Earth sciences</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">4</a></sup> Nonetheless, modern geography is an all-encompassing discipline that foremost seeks to understand the Earth and all of its human and natural complexities - not merely where objects are, but how they have changed and come to be. Geography has been called "the world discipline" and "the bridge between the human and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physical_science" title="Physical science">physical science</a>". Geography is divided into two main branches: <a href="/wiki/Human_geography" title="Human geography">human geography</a> and <a href="/wiki/Physical_geography" title="Physical geography">physical geography</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">5</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">6</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">7</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Knowledge
<p><b>Knowledge</b> is a familiarity with someone or something, which can include <a href="/wiki/Fact" title="Fact">facts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Information" title="Information">information</a>, <a href="/wiki/Description" title="Description">descriptions</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Skills" title="Skills">skills</a> acquired through <a href="/wiki/Experience" title="Experience">experience</a> or <a href="/wiki/Education" title="Education">education</a>. It can refer to the theoretical or practical understanding of a subject. It can be implicit or explicit; it can be more or less formal or systematic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>, the study of knowledge is called <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">epistemology</a>; the philosopher <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> famously defined knowledge as "<a href="/wiki/Justified_true_belief" title="Justified true belief">justified true belief</a>." However, no single agreed upon definition of knowledge exists, though there are numerous theories to explain it.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fact
<p>A <b>fact</b> is something that has really occurred or is actually the case. The usual test for a statement of fact is verifiability, that is whether it can be <a href="/wiki/Proof_(truth)" title="Proof">proven</a> to correspond to <a href="/wiki/Experience" title="Experience">experience</a>. Standard reference works are often used to check facts. <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">Scientific</a> facts are verified by repeatable <a href="/wiki/Experiment" title="Experiment">experiments</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proof_(truth)
<p>A <b>proof</b> is <a href="/wiki/Necessity_and_sufficiency" title="Necessity and sufficiency">sufficient</a> <a href="/wiki/Evidence" title="Evidence">evidence</a> or an <a href="/wiki/Argument" title="Argument">argument</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Truth" title="Truth">truth</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Proposition" title="Proposition">proposition</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">2</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">3</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">4</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Necessity_and_sufficiency
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>, <b>necessity</b> and <b>sufficiency</b> are implicational relationships between <a href="/wiki/Statement_(logic)" title="Statement">statements</a>.<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[]</sup> The assertion that one statement is a  <a href="/wiki/Condition_(philosophy)" title="Condition">condition</a> of another means that the former statement is true <a href="/wiki/If_and_only_if" title="If and only if">if and only if</a> the latter is true. That is, the two statements must be either simultaneously true or simultaneously false. In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ordinary_English" title="Ordinary English">ordinary English</a>, ‘necessary’ and ‘sufficient’ indicate relations between <a href="/wiki/Condition_(philosophy)" title="Condition">conditions</a> or states of affairs, not statements. Being male sibling is a necessary and sufficient condition for being a brother. Fred’s being a male sibling is necessary and sufficient for the truth of the statement that Fred is a brother.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Logic
<p><b>Logic</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-argumentative_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-argumentative-1">1</a></sup> has two meanings: first, it describes the use of valid <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reasoning" title="Reasoning">reasoning</a> in some activity; second, it names the <a href="/wiki/Normative" title="Normative">normative</a> study of reasoning or a branch thereof.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PopkinStroll1993_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-PopkinStroll1993-2">2</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jacquette2002_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-jacquette2002-3">3</a></sup> In the latter sense, it features most prominently in the subjects of <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">computer science</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reasoning
<p><b>Reason</b> is the capacity for <a href="/wiki/Consciousness" title="Consciousness">consciously</a> making sense of things, applying <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>, for establishing and verifying <a href="/wiki/Fact" title="Fact">facts</a>, and changing or justifying practices, institutions, and <a href="/wiki/Belief" title="Belief">beliefs</a> based on new or existing information.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup> It is closely associated with such characteristically <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">human</a> activities as <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a>, <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">language</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Art" title="Art">art</a>, and is normally considered to be a definitive characteristic of <a href="/wiki/Human_nature" title="Human nature">human nature</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">2</a></sup> The concept of reason is sometimes referred to as <a href="/wiki/Rationality" title="Rationality">rationality</a> and sometimes as <b>discursive reason</b>, in opposition to <a href="/wiki/Intuition_(psychology)" title="Intuition">intuitive reason</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">3</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Consciousness
<p><b>Consciousness</b> is the <a href="/wiki/Quality_(philosophy)" title="Quality">quality</a> or state of being aware of an external object or something within oneself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-van_Gulick2004_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-van_Gulick2004-2">2</a></sup> It has been defined as: <a href="/wiki/Sentience" title="Sentience">sentience</a>, <a href="/wiki/Awareness" title="Awareness">awareness</a>, <a href="/wiki/Subjectivity" title="Subjectivity">subjectivity</a>, the ability to <a href="/wiki/Experience" title="Experience">experience</a> or to <a href="/wiki/Feeling" title="Feeling">feel</a>, wakefulness, having a sense of <a href="/wiki/Self" title="Self">selfhood</a>, and the executive control system of the mind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Farthing1992Psychology_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Farthing1992Psychology-3">3</a></sup> Despite the difficulty in definition, many philosophers believe that there is a broadly shared underlying intuition about what consciousness is.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">4</a></sup> As <a href="/wiki/Max_Velmans" title="Max Velmans">Max Velmans</a> and Susan Schneider wrote in : "Anything that we are aware of at a given moment forms part of our consciousness, making conscious experience at once the most familiar and most mysterious aspect of our lives."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">5</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quality_(philosophy)
<p>A <b>quality</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup> is an attribute or a <a href="/wiki/Property_(philosophy)" title="Property">property</a>. Attributes are ascribable, by a subject, whereas properties are possessible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cargile.2C_1995_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cargile.2C_1995-2">2</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[]</sup> In contemporary <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a> the idea of qualities, and especially how to distinguish certain kinds of qualities from one another, remains controversial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cargile.2C_1995_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cargile.2C_1995-2">2</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Property_(philosophy)
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Modern_philosophy" title="Modern philosophy">modern philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>, a <b>property</b> is an attribute of an <a href="/wiki/Object_(philosophy)" title="Object">object</a>; a red object is said to have the property of redness. The property may be considered a form of object in its own right, able to possess other properties. A property however differs from individual objects in that it may be instantiated, and often in more than one thing. It differs from the logical/mathematical concept of <a href="/wiki/Class_(set_theory)" title="Class">class</a> by not having any concept of <a href="/wiki/Extensionality" title="Extensionality">extensionality</a>, and from the philosophical concept of <a href="/wiki/Class_(philosophy)" title="Class">class</a> in that a property is considered to be distinct from the objects which possess it. Understanding how different individual entities can in some sense have some of the same properties is the basis of the <a href="/wiki/Problem_of_universals" title="Problem of universals">problem of universals</a>. The terms <b>attribute</b> and <b>quality</b> have similar meanings.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Modern_philosophy
<p><b>Modern philosophy</b> is a category of <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a> that originated in <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western Europe</a> in the 17th century, and is now common worldwide. It is not a specific doctrine or school, although there are certain assumptions common to much of it, which helps to distinguish it from earlier <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup></p>
