http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arnold_Naimark
<p><b>Arnold Naimark</b>,  is a <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canadian</a> physician, academic, and former President of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Manitoba" title="University of Manitoba">University of Manitoba</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Canada
<p><b>Canada</b>  is a country in North America consisting of <a href="/wiki/Provinces_and_territories_of_Canada" title="Provinces and territories of Canada">10 provinces and 3 territories</a>. Located in the northern part of the continent, it extends from the Atlantic to the Pacific and northward into the Arctic Ocean. At 9.98 million square kilometres in total, Canada is the world's <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_and_dependencies_by_area" title="List of countries and dependencies by area">second-largest country by total area</a>, and its <a href="/wiki/Canada%E2%80%93United_States_border" title="Canada–United States border">common border</a> with the United States is the world's longest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Land_border" title="Land border">land border</a> shared by the same two countries.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Provinces_and_territories_of_Canada
<p>The <b>provinces and territories of Canada</b> combine to make up the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_and_dependencies_by_area" title="List of countries and dependencies by area">world's second-largest country</a> by area. Originally three provinces of <a href="/wiki/British_North_America" title="British North America">British North America</a>, New Brunswick, Nova Scotia and the <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Canada" title="Province of Canada">Province of Canada</a> united to form the new nation. Since then, Canada's external borders have changed several times, and has grown from four initial provinces to ten provinces and three territories as of 1999. The ten provinces are <a href="/wiki/Alberta" title="Alberta">Alberta</a>, <a href="/wiki/British_Columbia" title="British Columbia">British Columbia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manitoba" title="Manitoba">Manitoba</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Brunswick" title="New Brunswick">New Brunswick</a>, <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_and_Labrador" title="Newfoundland and Labrador">Newfoundland and Labrador</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nova_Scotia" title="Nova Scotia">Nova Scotia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ontario" title="Ontario">Ontario</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prince_Edward_Island" title="Prince Edward Island">Prince Edward Island</a>, <a href="/wiki/Quebec" title="Quebec">Quebec</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Saskatchewan" title="Saskatchewan">Saskatchewan</a>. The three territories are <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Territories" title="Northwest Territories">Northwest Territories</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nunavut" title="Nunavut">Nunavut</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Yukon" title="Yukon">Yukon</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_countries_and_dependencies_by_area
<p>This is a list of the world's <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_state" title="Sovereign state">sovereign states</a> and their <a href="/wiki/Dependent_territory" title="Dependent territory">dependent territories</a> by area, ranked by total area.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sovereign_state
<p>A <b>sovereign state</b> is a nonphysical juridical entity of the international legal system that is represented by a <a href="/wiki/Centralized_government" title="Centralized government">centralized government</a> that has supreme independent authority over a geographic area. International law defines sovereign states as having a permanent population, a <a href="/wiki/Government" title="Government">government</a>, and the capacity to enter into <a href="/wiki/International_relations" title="International relations">relations</a> with other sovereign states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup> It is also normally understood to be a state which is neither dependent on nor subject to any other power or state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">2</a></sup> The existence or disappearance of a state is a <a href="/wiki/Question_of_fact" title="Question of fact">question of fact</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lalonde2002_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lalonde2002-3">3</a></sup> While according to the declarative theory of state recognition a sovereign state can exist without being <a href="/wiki/Diplomatic_recognition" title="Diplomatic recognition">recognised</a> by other sovereign states, unrecognised states will often find it hard to exercise full treaty-making powers and engage in <a href="/wiki/Diplomacy" title="Diplomacy">diplomatic</a> relations with other sovereign states.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Centralized_government
<p>A <b>centralized government</b> is one in which <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Political_power" title="Political power">power</a> or <a href="/wiki/Rational-legal_authority" title="Rational-legal authority">legal authority</a> is exerted or coordinated by a <a href="/wiki/De_facto" title="De facto">de facto</a> political executive to which <a href="/wiki/Federated_state" title="Federated state">federal states</a>, <a href="/wiki/Local_government" title="Local government">local authorities</a>, and smaller units are considered subject. In a national context, centralization occurs in the transfer of power to a typically <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_state" title="Sovereign state">sovereign</a> <a href="/wiki/Nation_state" title="Nation state">nation state</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Political_power
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Social_science" title="Social science">social science</a> and <a href="/wiki/Politics" title="Politics">politics</a>, <b>power</b> is the ability to <a href="/wiki/Social_influence" title="Social influence">influence</a> the behavior of people. The term <a href="/wiki/Authority" title="Authority">authority</a> is often used for power perceived as legitimate by the <a href="/wiki/Social_structure" title="Social structure">social structure</a>. Power can be seen as evil or <a href="/wiki/Justice" title="Justice">unjust</a>, but the exercise of power is accepted as endemic to humans as social beings. In the corporate environment, power is often expressed as upward or downward. With downward power, a company's superior influences subordinates. When a company exerts upward power, it is the subordinates who influence the decisions of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leader" title="Leader">leader</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Social_science
<p><b>Social science</b> is an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Academic_discipline" title="Academic discipline">academic discipline</a> concerned with society and the relationships among individuals within a society. It includes <a href="/wiki/Anthropology" title="Anthropology">anthropology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Economics" title="Economics">economics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Political_science" title="Political science">political science</a>, <a href="/wiki/Psychology" title="Psychology">psychology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sociology" title="Sociology">sociology</a>. In a wider sense, it may often include some fields in the <a href="/wiki/Humanities" title="Humanities">humanities</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oed_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-oed-1">1</a></sup> such as <a href="/wiki/Archaeology" title="Archaeology">archaeology</a>, <a href="/wiki/History" title="History">history</a>, <a href="/wiki/Law" title="Law">law</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Linguistics" title="Linguistics">linguistics</a>. The term may however be used in the specific context of referring to the original , established in 19th century, sociology. <a href="/wiki/%C3%89mile_Durkheim" title="Émile Durkheim">Émile Durkheim</a>, <a href="/wiki/Karl_Marx" title="Karl Marx">Karl Marx</a> and <a href="/wiki/Max_Weber" title="Max Weber">Max Weber</a> are typically cited as the principal architects of modern social science by this definition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">2</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Academic_discipline
<p>A <b>discipline</b> is <a href="/wiki/Knowledge" title="Knowledge">knowledge</a> or a concentration in one academic field of study or profession. A discipline incorporates expertise, people, projects, communities, challenges, studies, inquiry, and research areas that are strongly associated with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_academic_disciplines" title="List of academic disciplines">academic areas of study</a> or areas of <a href="/wiki/Profession" title="Profession">professional practice</a>. For example, the branches of science are commonly referred to as the scientific disciplines. <a href="/wiki/Gravitation" title="Gravitation">Gravitation</a> is strongly associated with the discipline of <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a>, and is considered to be part of that disciplinary knowledge.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Knowledge
<p><b>Knowledge</b> is a familiarity with someone or something, which can include <a href="/wiki/Fact" title="Fact">facts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Information" title="Information">information</a>, <a href="/wiki/Description" title="Description">descriptions</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Skills" title="Skills">skills</a> acquired through <a href="/wiki/Experience" title="Experience">experience</a> or <a href="/wiki/Education" title="Education">education</a>. It can refer to the theoretical or practical understanding of a subject. It can be implicit or explicit; it can be more or less formal or systematic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>, the study of knowledge is called <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">epistemology</a>; the philosopher <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> famously defined knowledge as "<a href="/wiki/Justified_true_belief" title="Justified true belief">justified true belief</a>." However, no single agreed upon definition of knowledge exists, though there are numerous theories to explain it.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fact
<p>A <b>fact</b> is something that has really occurred or is actually the case. The usual test for a statement of fact is verifiability, that is whether it can be <a href="/wiki/Proof_(truth)" title="Proof">proven</a> to correspond to <a href="/wiki/Experience" title="Experience">experience</a>. Standard reference works are often used to check facts. <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">Scientific</a> facts are verified by repeatable <a href="/wiki/Experiment" title="Experiment">experiments</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proof_(truth)
<p>A <b>proof</b> is <a href="/wiki/Necessity_and_sufficiency" title="Necessity and sufficiency">sufficient</a> <a href="/wiki/Evidence" title="Evidence">evidence</a> or an <a href="/wiki/Argument" title="Argument">argument</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Truth" title="Truth">truth</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Proposition" title="Proposition">proposition</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">2</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">3</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">4</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Necessity_and_sufficiency
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>, <b>necessity</b> and <b>sufficiency</b> are implicational relationships between <a href="/wiki/Statement_(logic)" title="Statement">statements</a>.<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[]</sup> The assertion that one statement is a  <a href="/wiki/Condition_(philosophy)" title="Condition">condition</a> of another means that the former statement is true <a href="/wiki/If_and_only_if" title="If and only if">if and only if</a> the latter is true. That is, the two statements must be either simultaneously true or simultaneously false. In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ordinary_English" title="Ordinary English">ordinary English</a>, ‘necessary’ and ‘sufficient’ indicate relations between <a href="/wiki/Condition_(philosophy)" title="Condition">conditions</a> or states of affairs, not statements. Being male sibling is a necessary and sufficient condition for being a brother. Fred’s being a male sibling is necessary and sufficient for the truth of the statement that Fred is a brother.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Logic
<p><b>Logic</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-argumentative_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-argumentative-1">1</a></sup> has two meanings: first, it describes the use of valid <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reasoning" title="Reasoning">reasoning</a> in some activity; second, it names the <a href="/wiki/Normative" title="Normative">normative</a> study of reasoning or a branch thereof.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PopkinStroll1993_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-PopkinStroll1993-2">2</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jacquette2002_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-jacquette2002-3">3</a></sup> In the latter sense, it features most prominently in the subjects of <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">computer science</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reasoning
<p><b>Reason</b> is the capacity for <a href="/wiki/Consciousness" title="Consciousness">consciously</a> making sense of things, applying <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>, for establishing and verifying <a href="/wiki/Fact" title="Fact">facts</a>, and changing or justifying practices, institutions, and <a href="/wiki/Belief" title="Belief">beliefs</a> based on new or existing information.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup> It is closely associated with such characteristically <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">human</a> activities as <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a>, <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">language</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Art" title="Art">art</a>, and is normally considered to be a definitive characteristic of <a href="/wiki/Human_nature" title="Human nature">human nature</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">2</a></sup> The concept of reason is sometimes referred to as <a href="/wiki/Rationality" title="Rationality">rationality</a> and sometimes as <b>discursive reason</b>, in opposition to <a href="/wiki/Intuition_(psychology)" title="Intuition">intuitive reason</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">3</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Consciousness
<p><b>Consciousness</b> is the <a href="/wiki/Quality_(philosophy)" title="Quality">quality</a> or state of being aware of an external object or something within oneself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-van_Gulick2004_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-van_Gulick2004-2">2</a></sup> It has been defined as: <a href="/wiki/Sentience" title="Sentience">sentience</a>, <a href="/wiki/Awareness" title="Awareness">awareness</a>, <a href="/wiki/Subjectivity" title="Subjectivity">subjectivity</a>, the ability to <a href="/wiki/Experience" title="Experience">experience</a> or to <a href="/wiki/Feeling" title="Feeling">feel</a>, wakefulness, having a sense of <a href="/wiki/Self" title="Self">selfhood</a>, and the executive control system of the mind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Farthing1992Psychology_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Farthing1992Psychology-3">3</a></sup> Despite the difficulty in definition, many philosophers believe that there is a broadly shared underlying intuition about what consciousness is.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">4</a></sup> As <a href="/wiki/Max_Velmans" title="Max Velmans">Max Velmans</a> and Susan Schneider wrote in : "Anything that we are aware of at a given moment forms part of our consciousness, making conscious experience at once the most familiar and most mysterious aspect of our lives."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">5</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quality_(philosophy)
<p>A <b>quality</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup> is an attribute or a <a href="/wiki/Property_(philosophy)" title="Property">property</a>. Attributes are ascribable, by a subject, whereas properties are possessible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cargile.2C_1995_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cargile.2C_1995-2">2</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[]</sup> In contemporary <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a> the idea of qualities, and especially how to distinguish certain kinds of qualities from one another, remains controversial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cargile.2C_1995_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cargile.2C_1995-2">2</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Property_(philosophy)
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Modern_philosophy" title="Modern philosophy">modern philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>, a <b>property</b> is an attribute of an <a href="/wiki/Object_(philosophy)" title="Object">object</a>; a red object is said to have the property of redness. The property may be considered a form of object in its own right, able to possess other properties. A property however differs from individual objects in that it may be instantiated, and often in more than one thing. It differs from the logical/mathematical concept of <a href="/wiki/Class_(set_theory)" title="Class">class</a> by not having any concept of <a href="/wiki/Extensionality" title="Extensionality">extensionality</a>, and from the philosophical concept of <a href="/wiki/Class_(philosophy)" title="Class">class</a> in that a property is considered to be distinct from the objects which possess it. Understanding how different individual entities can in some sense have some of the same properties is the basis of the <a href="/wiki/Problem_of_universals" title="Problem of universals">problem of universals</a>. The terms <b>attribute</b> and <b>quality</b> have similar meanings.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Modern_philosophy
<p><b>Modern philosophy</b> is a category of <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a> that originated in <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western Europe</a> in the 17th century, and is now common worldwide. It is not a specific doctrine or school, although there are certain assumptions common to much of it, which helps to distinguish it from earlier <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Philosophy
19 hops
Test 8
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Latvian_Braille
<p><b>Latvian Braille</b> is the <a href="/wiki/Braille" title="Braille">braille</a> alphabet of the <a href="/wiki/Latvian_language" title="Latvian language">Latvian language</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Braille
<p><b>Braille</b> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">a</a></sup> is a tactile <a href="/wiki/Writing_system" title="Writing system">writing system</a> used by the <a href="/wiki/Blindness" title="Blindness">blind</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Visual_impairment" title="Visual impairment">visually impaired</a>. It is traditionally written with embossed paper. Braille-users can read computer screens and other electronic supports thanks to <a href="/wiki/Refreshable_braille_display" title="Refreshable braille display">refreshable braille displays</a>. They can write braille with the original <a href="/wiki/Slate_and_stylus" title="Slate and stylus">slate and stylus</a> or type it on a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Braille_writer" title="Braille writer">braille writer</a>, such as a portable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Braille_note-taker" title="Braille note-taker">braille note-taker</a>, or on a computer that prints with a <a href="/wiki/Braille_embosser" title="Braille embosser">braille embosser</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Writing_system
<p>A <b>writing system</b> is an organized, regular method of <a href="/wiki/Information" title="Information">information</a> <a href="/wiki/Recording" title="Recording">storage</a> and <a href="/wiki/Information_transfer" title="Information transfer">transfer</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Communication" title="Communication">communication</a> of <a href="/wiki/Message" title="Message">messages</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">language</a> by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Visual" title="Visual">visually</a> <a href="/wiki/Code" title="Code">encoding and decoding</a>" title="Reading">reading</a>) with a set of <a href="/wiki/Sign_(semiotics)" title="Sign">signs</a> or <a href="/wiki/Symbolic_system" title="Symbolic system">symbols</a>, both known generally as <a href="/wiki/Character_(symbol)" title="Character">characters</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup> These characters, often including <a href="/wiki/Letter_(alphabet)" title="Letter">letters</a> and <a href="/wiki/Number" title="Number">numbers</a>, are usually recorded onto a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Storage_medium#Recording_medium" title="Storage medium">durable medium</a> such as paper or <a href="/wiki/Data_storage_device" title="Data storage device">electronic storage</a>/<a href="/wiki/Display_device" title="Display device">display</a>, although non-durable methods may also be used, such as writing in sand or <a href="/wiki/Skywriting" title="Skywriting">skywriting</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Information
<p><b>Information</b>, in its most restricted technical sense, is a <a href="/wiki/Sequence" title="Sequence">sequence</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Symbols" title="Symbols">symbols</a> that can be interpreted as a <a href="/wiki/Message" title="Message">message</a>. Information can be recorded as <a href="/wiki/Sign_(semiotics)" title="Sign">signs</a>, or transmitted as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Signal_(electronics)" title="Signal">signals</a>. Information is any kind of <a class="extiw" href="//en.wiktionary.org/wiki/event" title="wikt:event">event</a> that affects the <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamic_state" title="Thermodynamic state">state</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Dynamical_system" title="Dynamical system">dynamic system</a> that can interpret the information.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sequence
<p>A <b>sequence</b> is an ordered list. Like a <a href="/wiki/Set_(mathematics)" title="Set">set</a>, it contains <a href="/wiki/Element_(mathematics)" title="Element">members</a>. The number of ordered elements is called the  of the sequence. Unlike a set, order matters, and exactly the same elements can appear multiple times at different positions in the sequence. Most precisely, a sequence can be defined as a <a href="/wiki/Function_(mathematics)" title="Function">function</a> whose domain is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Countable" title="Countable">countable</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Totally_ordered" title="Totally ordered">totally ordered</a> set, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_numbers" title="Natural numbers">natural numbers</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Set_(mathematics)
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, a <b>set</b> is a collection of distinct objects, considered as an <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_object" title="Mathematical object">object</a> in its own right. For example, the numbers 2, 4, and 6 are distinct objects when considered separately, but when they are considered collectively they form a single set of size three, written {2,4,6}. Sets are one of the most fundamental concepts in mathematics. Developed at the end of the 19th century, set theory is now a ubiquitous part of mathematics, and can be used as a foundation from which nearly all of mathematics can be derived. In <a href="/wiki/Mathematics_education" title="Mathematics education">mathematics education</a>, elementary topics such as <a href="/wiki/Venn_diagram" title="Venn diagram">Venn diagrams</a> are taught at a young age, while more advanced concepts are taught as part of a university degree. The term itself was coined by Bolzano in his work <a href="/wiki/The_Paradoxes_of_the_Infinite" title="The Paradoxes of the Infinite">The Paradoxes of the Infinite</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics
<p><b>Mathematics</b> is the abstract study of topics such as <a href="/wiki/Quantity" title="Quantity">quantity</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-OED-2">2</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Structure" title="Structure">structure</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kneebone_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kneebone-3">3</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Space" title="Space">space</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-OED-2">2</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Calculus" title="Calculus">change</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LaTorre_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-LaTorre-4">4</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ramana_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ramana-5">5</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ziegler_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ziegler-6">6</a></sup> There is a range of views among mathematicians and philosophers as to the exact scope and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Definition_of_mathematics" title="Definition of mathematics">definition of mathematics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mura_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mura-7">7</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Runge_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Runge-8">8</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantity
<p><b>Quantity</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Property_(philosophy)" title="Property">property</a> that can exist as a <a href="/wiki/Magnitude_(mathematics)" title="Magnitude">magnitude</a> or <a href="/wiki/Counting" title="Counting">multitude</a>. Quantities can be compared in terms of "more", "less" or "equal", or by assigning a numerical value in terms of a unit of measurement. Quantity is among the basic <a href="/wiki/Class_(philosophy)" title="Class">classes</a> of things along with <a href="/wiki/Quality_(philosophy)" title="Quality">quality</a>, <a href="/wiki/Substance_theory" title="Substance theory">substance</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Change_(mathematics)" title="Change">change</a>, and relation. Being a fundamental term, quantity is used to refer to any type of quantitative properties or attributes of things. Some quantities are such by their inner nature, while others are functioning as states of things such as heavy and light, long and short, broad and narrow, small and great, or much and little. A small quantity is sometimes referred to as a <b>quantulum</b>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Property_(philosophy)
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Modern_philosophy" title="Modern philosophy">modern philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>, a <b>property</b> is an attribute of an <a href="/wiki/Object_(philosophy)" title="Object">object</a>; a red object is said to have the property of redness. The property may be considered a form of object in its own right, able to possess other properties. A property however differs from individual objects in that it may be instantiated, and often in more than one thing. It differs from the logical/mathematical concept of <a href="/wiki/Class_(set_theory)" title="Class">class</a> by not having any concept of <a href="/wiki/Extensionality" title="Extensionality">extensionality</a>, and from the philosophical concept of <a href="/wiki/Class_(philosophy)" title="Class">class</a> in that a property is considered to be distinct from the objects which possess it. Understanding how different individual entities can in some sense have some of the same properties is the basis of the <a href="/wiki/Problem_of_universals" title="Problem of universals">problem of universals</a>. The terms <b>attribute</b> and <b>quality</b> have similar meanings.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Modern_philosophy
<p><b>Modern philosophy</b> is a category of <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a> that originated in <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western Europe</a> in the 17th century, and is now common worldwide. It is not a specific doctrine or school, although there are certain assumptions common to much of it, which helps to distinguish it from earlier <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Philosophy

Test 9
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/7mm_Weatherby_Magnum
<p>The <b>7 mm Weatherby Magnum</b> is a powerful 7 mm <a href="/wiki/Rifle" title="Rifle">rifle</a> <a href="/wiki/Cartridge_(firearms)" title="Cartridge">cartridge</a> offered by the <a href="/wiki/Weatherby" title="Weatherby">Weatherby</a> firearms company in their <a href="/wiki/Weatherby_Mark_V" title="Weatherby Mark V">Mark V</a> <a href="/wiki/Rifle" title="Rifle">rifles</a>. The cartridge was one of the first cartridges offered by the Weatherby company.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rifle
<p>A <b>rifle</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Firearm" title="Firearm">firearm</a> designed to be fired from the shoulder, with a barrel that has a helical groove or pattern of grooves cut into the barrel walls. The raised areas of the rifling are called "lands," which make contact with the projectile, imparting spin around an axis corresponding to the orientation of the weapon. When the projectile leaves the barrel, this spin lends gyroscopic stability to the projectile and prevents tumbling, in the same way that a properly thrown <a href="/wiki/American_football" title="American football">American football</a> or <a href="/wiki/Rugby_football" title="Rugby football">rugby</a> ball behaves. This allows the use of aerodynamically-efficient pointed bullets and thus improves range and accuracy. The word "rifle" originally referred to the grooving, and a rifle was called a "rifled gun." Rifles are used in <a href="/wiki/War" title="War">warfare</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hunting" title="Hunting">hunting</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shooting_sports" title="Shooting sports">shooting sports</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Firearm
<p>A <b>firearm</b> is a portable <a href="/wiki/Gun" title="Gun">gun</a>, being a <a href="/wiki/Gun_barrel" title="Gun barrel">barreled</a> <a href="/wiki/Weapon" title="Weapon">weapon</a> that launches one or more <a href="/wiki/Projectile" title="Projectile">projectiles</a> often defined by the action of an explosive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">2</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">3</a></sup> The first primitive firearms were invented in 13th century <a href="/wiki/History_of_science_and_technology_in_China" title="History of science and technology in China">China</a> when the man portable <a href="/wiki/Fire_lance" title="Fire lance">fire lance</a> was combined with projectiles such as scrap metal, broken porcelain, or darts/arrows.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">4</a></sup> The technology gradually spread through the rest of <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asia</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South Asia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a> and then into <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>. In older firearms, the propellant was typically <a href="/wiki/Gunpowder" title="Gunpowder">black powder</a>, but modern firearms use <a href="/wiki/Smokeless_powder" title="Smokeless powder">smokeless powder</a> or other propellants. Most modern firearms have <a href="/wiki/Rifling" title="Rifling">rifled</a> barrels to impart spin to the <a href="/wiki/Projectile" title="Projectile">projectile</a> for improved flight stability.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gun
<p>A <b>gun</b> is a normally tubular <a href="/wiki/Weapon" title="Weapon">weapon</a> or other device designed to discharge <a href="/wiki/Projectile" title="Projectile">projectiles</a> or other material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup> The projectile may be solid, liquid, gas or energy and may be free, as with bullets and artillery shells, or captive as with <a href="/wiki/Taser" title="Taser">Taser</a> probes and <a href="/wiki/Harpoon#Explosive_harpoons" title="Harpoon">whaling harpoons</a>. The means of projection varies according to design but is usually effected by the action of gas pressure, either produced through the rapid combustion of a <a href="/wiki/Propellant" title="Propellant">propellant</a> or compressed and stored by mechanical means, operating on the projectile inside an open-ended tube in the fashion of a piston. The confined gas accelerates the movable projectile down the length of the tube imparting sufficient velocity to sustain the projectile's travel once the action of the gas ceases at the end of the tube or muzzle. Alternatively, acceleration via electromagnetic field generation may be employed in which case the tube may be dispensed with and a guide rail substituted.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Weapon
<p>A <b>weapon</b>, <b>arm</b>, or <b>armament</b> is any device used in order to inflict damage or harm to living beings, structures, or systems. Weapons are used to increase the efficacy and efficiency of activities such as <a href="/wiki/Hunting" title="Hunting">hunting</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crime" title="Crime">crime</a>, <a href="/wiki/Law_enforcement" title="Law enforcement">law enforcement</a>, <a href="/wiki/Self-defense" title="Self-defense">self-defense</a>, and <a href="/wiki/War" title="War">warfare</a>. In a broader context, weapons may be construed to include anything used to gain a strategic, material or mental advantage over an adversary.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hunting
<p><b>Hunting</b> is the practice of killing or trapping any living organism, or pursuing it with the intent of it. Hunting <a href="/wiki/Wildlife" title="Wildlife">wildlife</a> or feral animals is most commonly done by humans for food, <a href="/wiki/Recreation" title="Recreation">recreation</a>, or trade. Animals may also hunt other animal species, but this is usually called <a href="/wiki/Predation" title="Predation">predation</a>. In present-day use, lawful hunting is distinguished from <a href="/wiki/Poaching" title="Poaching">poaching</a>, which is the killing, trapping or capture of the hunted species contrary to applicable law. The species that are hunted are referred to as <a href="/wiki/Game_(food)" title="Game">game</a> and are usually <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bird_migration" title="Bird migration">migratory</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Resident_bird" title="Resident bird">non-migratory</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gamebird" title="Gamebird">gamebirds</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wildlife
<p><b>Wildlife</b> traditionally refers to non-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Domesticated" title="Domesticated">domesticated</a> <a href="/wiki/Animal" title="Animal">animal</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Species_(biology)" title="Species">species</a>, but has come to include all <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">plants</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fungi" title="Fungi">fungi</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">organisms</a> which grow or live wild in an area without being introduced by <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">humans</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup> Domesticating wild plant and animal species for human benefit has occurred many times all over the planet, and has a major impact on the environment, both positive and negative.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Domesticated
<p><b>Domestication</b>" title="Latin">Latin</a> ) is the process whereby a <a href="/wiki/Population" title="Population">population</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Living_organism" title="Living organism">living organisms</a> is changed at the <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">genetic</a> level, through generations of <a href="/wiki/Selective_breeding" title="Selective breeding">selective breeding</a>, to accentuate traits that ultimately benefit <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">humans</a>. A usual <a href="/wiki/By-product" title="By-product">by-product</a> of domestication is the creation of a dependency in the domesticated organisms, so that they lose their ability to live in the wild.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup> This differs from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taming" title="Taming">taming</a> in that a change in the <a href="/wiki/Phenotype" title="Phenotype">phenotypical</a> expression and <a href="/wiki/Genotype" title="Genotype">genotype</a> of the animal occurs, whereas  is simply an environmental socialization/behavioral trait; the process by which animals become accustomed to human presence. In the <a href="/wiki/Convention_on_Biological_Diversity" title="Convention on Biological Diversity">Convention on Biological Diversity</a>, a domesticated species is defined as a "species in which the evolutionary process has been influenced by humans to meet their needs."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">2</a></sup> Therefore, a defining characteristic of domestication is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artificial_selection" title="Artificial selection">artificial selection</a> by humans. Humans have brought these populations under their control and care for a wide range of reasons: to produce <a href="/wiki/Food" title="Food">food</a> or valuable <a href="/wiki/Commodity" title="Commodity">commodities</a> and for types of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Labour_(economics)" title="Labour">work</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scientific_research" title="Scientific research">scientific research</a>, or simply to enjoy as <a href="/wiki/Pet" title="Pet">companions</a> or <a href="/wiki/Ornamental_plant" title="Ornamental plant">ornaments</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Population
<p>A <b>population</b> is a summation of all the organisms of the same group or <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a>, who live in the same <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geographical_area" title="Geographical area">geographical area</a>, and have the capability of interbreeding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">2</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Species
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biology</a>, a <b>species</b> is one of the basic units of <a href="/wiki/Biological_classification" title="Biological classification">biological classification</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Taxonomic_rank" title="Taxonomic rank">taxonomic rank</a>. A species is often defined as the largest group of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organisms" title="Organisms">organisms</a> capable of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interbreeding" title="Interbreeding">interbreeding</a> and <a href="/wiki/Reproduction" title="Reproduction">producing</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fertile" title="Fertile">fertile</a> <a href="/wiki/Offspring" title="Offspring">offspring</a>. While in many cases this definition is adequate, the difficulty of defining species is known as the <a href="/wiki/Species_problem" title="Species problem">species problem</a>. Differing measures are often used, such as similarity of DNA, morphology, or ecological niche. Presence of specific locally adapted traits may further subdivide species into "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Infraspecific_taxa" title="Infraspecific taxa">infraspecific taxa</a>" such as <a href="/wiki/Subspecies" title="Subspecies">subspecies</a>" title="Variety">varieties</a>, subvarieties, and <a href="/wiki/Form_(botany)" title="Form">formae</a>).</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biology
<p><b>Biology</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Natural_science" title="Natural science">natural science</a> concerned with the study of <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">life</a> and <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">living organisms</a>, including their structure, function, growth, <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolution</a>, distribution, and <a href="/wiki/Taxonomy_(biology)" title="Taxonomy">taxonomy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aquarenagloss_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-aquarenagloss-1">1</a></sup> Modern biology is a vast and eclectic field, composed of many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_biology_disciplines#Branches_of_biology" title="List of biology disciplines">branches and subdisciplines</a>. However, despite the broad scope of biology, there are certain general and unifying concepts within it that govern all study and research, consolidating it into single, coherent field. In general, biology recognizes the <a href="/wiki/Cell_(biology)" title="Cell">cell</a> as the basic unit of life, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genes" title="Genes">genes</a> as the basic unit of <a href="/wiki/Heredity" title="Heredity">heredity</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolution</a> as the engine that propels the synthesis and creation of new <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a>. It is also understood today that all organisms survive by consuming and transforming <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a> and by <a href="/wiki/Homeostasis" title="Homeostasis">regulating</a> their internal environment to maintain a stable and vital condition.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Natural_science
<p>The <b>natural sciences</b> are the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sciences" title="Sciences">sciences</a> that seek to elucidate the rules that govern the natural world through scientific methods, the cornerstone of which is <a href="/wiki/Measurement" title="Measurement">measured</a> by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Numerical_data" title="Numerical data">quantitative</a> data.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELedoux200234_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELedoux200234-1">1</a></sup> Based on <a href="/wiki/Formal_science" title="Formal science">formal sciences</a>, they also attempt to provide mathematical <a href="/wiki/Scientific_modelling" title="Scientific modelling">models</a> of natural processes. The term "natural science" is used to distinguish the subject from the <a href="/wiki/Social_science" title="Social science">social sciences</a>, such as economics, psychology and sociology, which apply the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific method</a> to the study of human behavior and social patterns; the <a href="/wiki/Humanities" title="Humanities">humanities</a>, which use a critical or analytical approach to study the human condition; and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Formal_sciences" title="Formal sciences">formal sciences</a> such as mathematics and logic, which use an <a href="/wiki/A_priori_and_a_posteriori" title="A priori and a posteriori">a priori</a>, as opposed to empirical methodology to study formal systems.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sciences
<p><b>Science</b> is a systematic enterprise that builds and organizes <a href="/wiki/Knowledge" title="Knowledge">knowledge</a> in the form of testable explanations and predictions about the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wilson_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-wilson-2">2</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">3</a></sup> In an older and closely related meaning, "science" also refers to a body of knowledge itself, of the type that can be rationally explained and reliably applied. A practitioner of science is known as a <a href="/wiki/Scientist" title="Scientist">scientist</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Knowledge
<p><b>Knowledge</b> is a familiarity with someone or something, which can include <a href="/wiki/Fact" title="Fact">facts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Information" title="Information">information</a>, <a href="/wiki/Description" title="Description">descriptions</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Skills" title="Skills">skills</a> acquired through <a href="/wiki/Experience" title="Experience">experience</a> or <a href="/wiki/Education" title="Education">education</a>. It can refer to the theoretical or practical understanding of a subject. It can be implicit or explicit; it can be more or less formal or systematic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>, the study of knowledge is called <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">epistemology</a>; the philosopher <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> famously defined knowledge as "<a href="/wiki/Justified_true_belief" title="Justified true belief">justified true belief</a>." However, no single agreed upon definition of knowledge exists, though there are numerous theories to explain it.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fact
<p>A <b>fact</b> is something that has really occurred or is actually the case. The usual test for a statement of fact is verifiability, that is whether it can be <a href="/wiki/Proof_(truth)" title="Proof">proven</a> to correspond to <a href="/wiki/Experience" title="Experience">experience</a>. Standard reference works are often used to check facts. <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">Scientific</a> facts are verified by repeatable <a href="/wiki/Experiment" title="Experiment">experiments</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proof_(truth)
<p>A <b>proof</b> is <a href="/wiki/Necessity_and_sufficiency" title="Necessity and sufficiency">sufficient</a> <a href="/wiki/Evidence" title="Evidence">evidence</a> or an <a href="/wiki/Argument" title="Argument">argument</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Truth" title="Truth">truth</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Proposition" title="Proposition">proposition</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">2</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">3</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">4</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Necessity_and_sufficiency
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>, <b>necessity</b> and <b>sufficiency</b> are implicational relationships between <a href="/wiki/Statement_(logic)" title="Statement">statements</a>.<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[]</sup> The assertion that one statement is a  <a href="/wiki/Condition_(philosophy)" title="Condition">condition</a> of another means that the former statement is true <a href="/wiki/If_and_only_if" title="If and only if">if and only if</a> the latter is true. That is, the two statements must be either simultaneously true or simultaneously false. In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ordinary_English" title="Ordinary English">ordinary English</a>, ‘necessary’ and ‘sufficient’ indicate relations between <a href="/wiki/Condition_(philosophy)" title="Condition">conditions</a> or states of affairs, not statements. Being male sibling is a necessary and sufficient condition for being a brother. Fred’s being a male sibling is necessary and sufficient for the truth of the statement that Fred is a brother.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Logic
<p><b>Logic</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-argumentative_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-argumentative-1">1</a></sup> has two meanings: first, it describes the use of valid <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reasoning" title="Reasoning">reasoning</a> in some activity; second, it names the <a href="/wiki/Normative" title="Normative">normative</a> study of reasoning or a branch thereof.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PopkinStroll1993_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-PopkinStroll1993-2">2</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jacquette2002_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-jacquette2002-3">3</a></sup> In the latter sense, it features most prominently in the subjects of <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">computer science</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reasoning
<p><b>Reason</b> is the capacity for <a href="/wiki/Consciousness" title="Consciousness">consciously</a> making sense of things, applying <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>, for establishing and verifying <a href="/wiki/Fact" title="Fact">facts</a>, and changing or justifying practices, institutions, and <a href="/wiki/Belief" title="Belief">beliefs</a> based on new or existing information.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup> It is closely associated with such characteristically <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">human</a> activities as <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a>, <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">language</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Art" title="Art">art</a>, and is normally considered to be a definitive characteristic of <a href="/wiki/Human_nature" title="Human nature">human nature</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">2</a></sup> The concept of reason is sometimes referred to as <a href="/wiki/Rationality" title="Rationality">rationality</a> and sometimes as <b>discursive reason</b>, in opposition to <a href="/wiki/Intuition_(psychology)" title="Intuition">intuitive reason</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">3</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Consciousness
<p><b>Consciousness</b> is the <a href="/wiki/Quality_(philosophy)" title="Quality">quality</a> or state of being aware of an external object or something within oneself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-van_Gulick2004_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-van_Gulick2004-2">2</a></sup> It has been defined as: <a href="/wiki/Sentience" title="Sentience">sentience</a>, <a href="/wiki/Awareness" title="Awareness">awareness</a>, <a href="/wiki/Subjectivity" title="Subjectivity">subjectivity</a>, the ability to <a href="/wiki/Experience" title="Experience">experience</a> or to <a href="/wiki/Feeling" title="Feeling">feel</a>, wakefulness, having a sense of <a href="/wiki/Self" title="Self">selfhood</a>, and the executive control system of the mind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Farthing1992Psychology_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Farthing1992Psychology-3">3</a></sup> Despite the difficulty in definition, many philosophers believe that there is a broadly shared underlying intuition about what consciousness is.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">4</a></sup> As <a href="/wiki/Max_Velmans" title="Max Velmans">Max Velmans</a> and Susan Schneider wrote in : "Anything that we are aware of at a given moment forms part of our consciousness, making conscious experience at once the most familiar and most mysterious aspect of our lives."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">5</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quality_(philosophy)
<p>A <b>quality</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup> is an attribute or a <a href="/wiki/Property_(philosophy)" title="Property">property</a>. Attributes are ascribable, by a subject, whereas properties are possessible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cargile.2C_1995_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cargile.2C_1995-2">2</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[]</sup> In contemporary <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a> the idea of qualities, and especially how to distinguish certain kinds of qualities from one another, remains controversial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cargile.2C_1995_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cargile.2C_1995-2">2</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Property_(philosophy)
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Modern_philosophy" title="Modern philosophy">modern philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>, a <b>property</b> is an attribute of an <a href="/wiki/Object_(philosophy)" title="Object">object</a>; a red object is said to have the property of redness. The property may be considered a form of object in its own right, able to possess other properties. A property however differs from individual objects in that it may be instantiated, and often in more than one thing. It differs from the logical/mathematical concept of <a href="/wiki/Class_(set_theory)" title="Class">class</a> by not having any concept of <a href="/wiki/Extensionality" title="Extensionality">extensionality</a>, and from the philosophical concept of <a href="/wiki/Class_(philosophy)" title="Class">class</a> in that a property is considered to be distinct from the objects which possess it. Understanding how different individual entities can in some sense have some of the same properties is the basis of the <a href="/wiki/Problem_of_universals" title="Problem of universals">problem of universals</a>. The terms <b>attribute</b> and <b>quality</b> have similar meanings.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Modern_philosophy
<p><b>Modern philosophy</b> is a category of <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a> that originated in <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western Europe</a> in the 17th century, and is now common worldwide. It is not a specific doctrine or school, although there are certain assumptions common to much of it, which helps to distinguish it from earlier <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup></p>

http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gladys_Campbell
<p><b>Gladys Campbell</b> was a poet and teacher in <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a>. As a student she was an early member of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Chicago_Poetry_Club" title="University of Chicago Poetry Club">University of Chicago Poetry Club</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chicago
<p><b>Chicago</b> is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_cities_by_population" title="List of United States cities by population">third most populous</a> city in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, after <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a> and <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles" title="Los Angeles">Los Angeles</a>. With 2.7 million residents,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-population1_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-population1-1">1</a></sup> it is the most populous city in both the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">U.S.</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_state" title="United States state">state</a> of <a href="/wiki/Illinois" title="Illinois">Illinois</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/American_Midwest" title="American Midwest">American Midwest</a>. Its metropolitan area, sometimes called , is home to 9.5 million people and is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_metropolitan_areas_of_the_United_States" title="List of metropolitan areas of the United States">third-largest</a> in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-metro_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-metro-2">2</a></sup> Chicago is the <a href="/wiki/County_seat" title="County seat">seat</a> of <a href="/wiki/Cook_County,_Illinois" title="Cook County, Illinois">Cook County</a>, although a small part of the city extends into <a href="/wiki/DuPage_County,_Illinois" title="DuPage County, Illinois">DuPage County</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GR6_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-GR6-4">4</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_United_States_cities_by_population
<p>The following is a <b>list of the most populous <a href="/wiki/Municipal_corporation" title="Municipal corporation">incorporated places</a> of the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States of America</a></b>. As defined by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Census_Bureau" title="United States Census Bureau">United States Census Bureau</a>, an "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Incorporated_place" title="Incorporated place">incorporated place</a>" includes a variety of designations, including <a href="/wiki/City" title="City">city</a>, <a href="/wiki/Town" title="Town">town</a>, <a href="/wiki/Village" title="Village">village</a>, <a href="/wiki/Borough" title="Borough">borough</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Municipality" title="Municipality">municipality</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NewEngland_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-NewEngland-1">1</a></sup> A few exceptional <a href="/wiki/Census-designated_place" title="Census-designated place">Census Designated Places</a> are also included in the Census Bureau's listing of incorporated places.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HonoluluHI_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-HonoluluHI-2">2</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Consolidated_city%E2%80%93county" title="Consolidated city–county">Consolidated city-counties</a> represent a distinct type of government that includes the entire population of a county, or county equivalent. Some consolidated city-counties, however, include multiple incorporated places. This list presents only that portion of such consolidated city-counties that are not a part of another incorporated place.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Municipal_corporation
<p>A <b>municipal corporation</b> is the legal term for a <a href="/wiki/Local_government" title="Local government">local governing body</a>, including <a href="/wiki/City" title="City">cities</a>, <a href="/wiki/County" title="County">counties</a>, <a href="/wiki/Town" title="Town">towns</a>, <a href="/wiki/Township" title="Township">townships</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charter_township" title="Charter township">charter townships</a>, <a href="/wiki/Village" title="Village">villages</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Borough" title="Borough">boroughs</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Local_government
<p><b>Local government</b> is a form of <a href="/wiki/Public_administration" title="Public administration">public administration</a> which in a majority of contexts, exists as the lowest tier of administration within a given state. The term is used to contrast with offices at <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_state" title="Sovereign state">state</a> level, which are referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Central_government" title="Central government">central government</a>, <a href="/wiki/Central_government" title="Central government">national government</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Federal_government" title="Federal government">federal government</a> and also to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supranational" title="Supranational">supranational</a> government which deals with governing institutions between states. Local governments generally act within powers delegated to them by legislation or directives of the higher level of government. In <a href="/wiki/Federation" title="Federation">federal states</a>, local government generally comprises the third tier of government, whereas in <a href="/wiki/Unitary_state" title="Unitary state">unitary states</a>, local government usually occupies the second or third tier of government, often with greater powers than higher-level administrative divisions.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Public_administration
<p><b>Public administration</b> refers to two meanings: first, it is concerned with the implementation of government policy; second, it is an academic discipline that studies this implementation and prepares civil servants for working in the public service.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup> As a "field of inquiry with a diverse scope" its "fundamental goal... is to advance management and policies so that government can function."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">2</a></sup> Some of the various definitions which have been offered for the term are: "the management of public programs";<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">3</a></sup> the "translation of <a href="/wiki/Politics" title="Politics">politics</a> into the reality that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Citizen" title="Citizen">citizens</a> see every day";<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KettlDonald_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-KettlDonald-4">4</a></sup> and "the study of government decision making, the <a href="/wiki/Policy_analysis" title="Policy analysis">analysis of the policies</a> themselves, the various inputs that have produced them, and the inputs necessary to produce alternative policies."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">5</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Politics
<p><b>Politics</b> is the practice and theory of influencing other people on a civic or individual level. More narrowly, it refers to achieving and exercising positions of <a href="/wiki/Governance" title="Governance">governance</a> — organized control over a human community, particularly a <a href="/wiki/State_(polity)" title="State">state</a>. A variety of methods are employed in politics, which include promoting one's own political views among people, <a href="/wiki/Negotiation" title="Negotiation">negotiation</a> with other political subjects, making <a href="/wiki/Law" title="Law">laws</a>, and exercising <a href="/wiki/Force_(law)" title="Force">force</a>, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Warfare" title="Warfare">warfare</a> against adversaries. Politics is exercised on a wide range of social levels, from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clans" title="Clans">clans</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tribes" title="Tribes">tribes</a> of traditional societies, through modern <a href="/wiki/Local_government" title="Local government">local governments</a>, <a href="/wiki/Company" title="Company">companies</a> and institutions up to <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_state" title="Sovereign state">sovereign states</a>, to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_politics" title="International politics">international level</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Governance
<p><b>Governance</b> refers to "all processes of governing, whether undertaken by a government, market, or network, whether over a family, tribe, formal or informal organization, or territory, and whether through laws, norms, power, or language."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup> It relates to processes and decisions that seek to define actions, grant power, and verify performance.</p>
<p>In general terms, governance occurs in three broad ways:<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[]</sup></p>
<p>To distinguish the term  from govern: "governance" is the concrete activity that reproduces a formal or informal organization. If the organization is a formal one, governance is primarily about what the relevant "governing body" does. If the organization is an informal one, such as a market, governance is primarily about the rules and norms that guide the relevant activity. Whether the organization is a geo-political entity, a corporate entity, a socio-political entity, or an informal one, its governance is the way the rules and actions are produced, sustained, and regulated.</p>
