http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jan_Kiepura
<p><b>Jan Wiktor Kiepura</b> was an acclaimed <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Polish</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Singer" title="Singer">singer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Actor" title="Actor">actor</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Poland
<p><b>Poland</b> , officially the <b>Republic of Poland</b>, is a country in <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">Central Europe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Borders_of_Poland" title="Borders of Poland">bordered</a> by <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> to the west; the <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">Czech Republic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Slovakia" title="Slovakia">Slovakia</a> to the south; <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Belarus" title="Belarus">Belarus</a> to the east; and the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea" title="Baltic Sea">Baltic Sea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kaliningrad_Oblast" title="Kaliningrad Oblast">Kaliningrad Oblast</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lithuania" title="Lithuania">Lithuania</a> to the north. The total <a href="/wiki/Area_of_Poland" title="Area of Poland">area of Poland</a> is 312,679 square kilometres,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CSO_2008_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-CSO_2008-7">7</a></sup> making it the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_and_dependencies_by_area" title="List of countries and dependencies by area">69th largest country</a> in the world and the 9th largest in Europe. With a population of over 38.5 million people,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CSO_2008_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-CSO_2008-7">7</a></sup> Poland is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_population" title="List of countries by population">34th most populous country</a> in the world,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nationmaster_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-nationmaster-9">9</a></sup> the sixth most populous <a href="/wiki/Member_state_of_the_European_Union" title="Member state of the European Union">member of the European Union</a>, and the most populous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Post-communist" title="Post-communist">post-communist</a> member of the European Union. Poland is a <a href="/wiki/Unitary_state" title="Unitary state">unitary state</a> divided into 16 <a href="/wiki/Voivodeship" title="Voivodeship">administrative subdivisions</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Central_Europe
<p><b>Central Europe</b> or alternatively <b>Middle Europe</b> is a region of the <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">European</a> <a href="/wiki/Continent" title="Continent">continent</a> lying between the variously defined areas of <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">Eastern</a> and <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western Europe</a>. The term and widespread interest in the region itself came back into fashion<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Economist_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Economist-3">3</a></sup> by the end of the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>, which had divided <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> politically into East and West, splitting Central Europe in half.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">4</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">5</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Europe
<p><b>Europe</b> is, by convention, one of the world's seven <a href="/wiki/Continent" title="Continent">continents</a>. Comprising the westernmost <a href="/wiki/Peninsula" title="Peninsula">peninsula</a> of <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a>, Europe is generally <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Borders_of_the_continents#Europe_and_Asia" title="Borders of the continents">divided from Asia</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Drainage_divide" title="Drainage divide">watershed divides</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Ural_Mountains" title="Ural Mountains">Ural</a> and <a href="/wiki/Caucasus_Mountains" title="Caucasus Mountains">Caucasus</a> Mountains, the <a href="/wiki/Ural_River" title="Ural River">Ural River</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Caspian_Sea" title="Caspian Sea">Caspian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black</a> Seas, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bosporus" title="Bosporus">waterways</a> connecting the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aegean_Sea" title="Aegean Sea">Aegean</a> Seas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NatlGeoAtlas_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-NatlGeoAtlas-2">2</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Continent
<p>A <b>continent</b> is one of several very large <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Landmass" title="Landmass">landmasses</a> on <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>. They are generally identified by <a href="/wiki/Convention_(norm)" title="Convention">convention</a> rather than any strict criteria, with up to seven regions commonly regarded as continents. These are—from largest in size to smallest: <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a>, <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Australia_(continent)" title="Australia">Australia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NatlGeo_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-NatlGeo-1">1</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Landmass
<p><b>Land</b>, sometimes referred to as <b>dry land</b>, is the solid surface of the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>, that is not covered by <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup> The division between land and <a href="/wiki/Ocean" title="Ocean">ocean</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sea" title="Sea">sea</a>, or other bodies of water, is one of the most fundamental separations on the planet. The vast majority of human activity has historically occurred, and continues to occur, on land. For this reason, land, and particularly those areas of land that support <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hunting" title="Hunting">hunting</a>, and other human activities, may be invested with great emotional and cultural value. Furthermore, the life forms that exist primarily on land, including <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_plant" title="Terrestrial plant">terrestrial plants</a> and <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_animal" title="Terrestrial animal">terrestrial animals</a>, differ from those that exist primarily in large bodies of water. The demarcation between land and water varies. In some places, it is sharply defined by solid rock <a href="/wiki/Landform" title="Landform">landforms</a> coming directly to the edge of the water. In other places, land may slowly give way to marshes or swamps, with no clear point at which the land ends and a body of water has begun. In such places, the demarcation can further vary due to tides and weather, with the area where land and sea interact being called the "coastal zone".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">2</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth
<p><b>Earth</b> is the third <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planet</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>. It is the <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">densest</a> and fifth-largest of the eight planets in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>. It is also the largest of the Solar System's four <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_planet" title="Terrestrial planet">terrestrial planets</a>. It is sometimes referred to as the <a href="/wiki/World" title="World">world</a> or the Blue Planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-blueplanet_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-blueplanet-28">23</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet
<p>A <b>planet</b> is an <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_object" title="Astronomical object">astronomical object</a> <a href="/wiki/Orbit" title="Orbit">orbiting</a> a <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">star</a> or <a href="/wiki/Stellar_evolution#Stellar_remnants" title="Stellar evolution">stellar remnant</a> that is massive enough to be rounded by its own <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravity</a>, is not massive enough to cause <a href="/wiki/Thermonuclear_fusion" title="Thermonuclear fusion">thermonuclear fusion</a>, and has <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cleared_the_neighbourhood" title="Cleared the neighbourhood">cleared its neighbouring region</a> of <a href="/wiki/Planetesimal" title="Planetesimal">planetesimals</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-footnoteA_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-footnoteA-1">a</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAU_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-IAU-2">1</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WSGESP_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-WSGESP-3">2</a></sup> The term  is ancient, with ties to <a href="/wiki/History" title="History">history</a>, <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mythology" title="Mythology">mythology</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">religion</a>. The planets were originally seen by many early cultures as divine, or as emissaries of <a href="/wiki/Deity" title="Deity">deities</a>. As scientific knowledge advanced, human perception of the planets changed, incorporating a number of disparate objects. In 2006, the <a href="/wiki/International_Astronomical_Union" title="International Astronomical Union">International Astronomical Union</a> officially adopted a resolution <a href="/wiki/IAU_definition_of_planet" title="IAU definition of planet">defining planets</a> within the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>. This definition has been both praised and criticized and remains disputed by some scientists because it excludes many objects of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planemo" title="Planemo">planetary mass</a> based on where or what they orbit. Although eight of the planetary bodies discovered before 1950 remain "planets" under the modern definition, some celestial bodies, such as <a href="/wiki/Ceres_(dwarf_planet)" title="Ceres">Ceres</a>, <a href="/wiki/2_Pallas" title="2 Pallas">Pallas</a>, <a href="/wiki/3_Juno" title="3 Juno">Juno</a>, <a href="/wiki/4_Vesta" title="4 Vesta">Vesta</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a>, that were once considered planets by the scientific community are no longer viewed as such.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Astronomical_object
<p><b>Astronomical objects</b> or <b>celestial objects</b> are naturally occurring <a href="/wiki/Entity" title="Entity">physical entities</a>, associations or structures that current <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a> has demonstrated to exist in the <a href="/wiki/Observable_universe" title="Observable universe">observable universe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">2</a></sup> The term  is sometimes used interchangeably with . Typically, an astronomical body refers to a single, cohesive structure that is bound together by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravity</a>. Examples include the <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroids</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moons" title="Moons">moons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planets</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a>. Astronomical objects are gravitationally bound structures that are associated with a position in space, but may consist of multiple independent astronomical bodies or objects. These objects range from single planets to <a href="/wiki/Star_cluster" title="Star cluster">star clusters</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nebula" title="Nebula">nebulae</a> or entire galaxies. A <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">comet</a> may be described as a body, in reference to the frozen nucleus of ice and dust, or as an object, when describing the nucleus with its diffuse coma and tail.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Entity
<p>An <b>entity</b> is something that exists by itself, although it need not be of material existence. In particular, <a href="/wiki/Abstraction" title="Abstraction">abstractions</a> and <a href="/wiki/Legal_fiction" title="Legal fiction">legal fictions</a> are usually regarded as entities. In general, there is also no presumption that an entity is <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">animate</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abstraction
<p><b>Abstraction</b> is a process by which concepts are derived from the usage and classification of literal concepts, <a href="/wiki/First_principle" title="First principle">first principles</a>, or other methods. "An abstraction" is the product of this process—a concept that acts as a super-categorical noun for all subordinate concepts, and connects any related concepts as a  or .<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Langer_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Langer-1">1</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/First_principle
<p><b>In <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a></b>, first principles are referred to as <a href="/wiki/Axiom" title="Axiom">axioms</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Postulate" title="Postulate">postulates</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics
<p><b>Mathematics</b> is the abstract study of topics such as <a href="/wiki/Quantity" title="Quantity">quantity</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-OED-2">2</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Structure" title="Structure">structure</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kneebone_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kneebone-3">3</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Space" title="Space">space</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-OED-2">2</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Calculus" title="Calculus">change</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LaTorre_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-LaTorre-4">4</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ramana_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ramana-5">5</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ziegler_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ziegler-6">6</a></sup> There is a range of views among mathematicians and philosophers as to the exact scope and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Definition_of_mathematics" title="Definition of mathematics">definition of mathematics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mura_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mura-7">7</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Runge_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Runge-8">8</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantity
<p><b>Quantity</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Property_(philosophy)" title="Property">property</a> that can exist as a <a href="/wiki/Magnitude_(mathematics)" title="Magnitude">magnitude</a> or <a href="/wiki/Counting" title="Counting">multitude</a>. Quantities can be compared in terms of "more", "less" or "equal", or by assigning a numerical value in terms of a unit of measurement. Quantity is among the basic <a href="/wiki/Class_(philosophy)" title="Class">classes</a> of things along with <a href="/wiki/Quality_(philosophy)" title="Quality">quality</a>, <a href="/wiki/Substance_theory" title="Substance theory">substance</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Change_(mathematics)" title="Change">change</a>, and relation. Being a fundamental term, quantity is used to refer to any type of quantitative properties or attributes of things. Some quantities are such by their inner nature, while others are functioning as states of things such as heavy and light, long and short, broad and narrow, small and great, or much and little. A small quantity is sometimes referred to as a <b>quantulum</b>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Property_(philosophy)
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Modern_philosophy" title="Modern philosophy">modern philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>, a <b>property</b> is an attribute of an <a href="/wiki/Object_(philosophy)" title="Object">object</a>; a red object is said to have the property of redness. The property may be considered a form of object in its own right, able to possess other properties. A property however differs from individual objects in that it may be instantiated, and often in more than one thing. It differs from the logical/mathematical concept of <a href="/wiki/Class_(set_theory)" title="Class">class</a> by not having any concept of <a href="/wiki/Extensionality" title="Extensionality">extensionality</a>, and from the philosophical concept of <a href="/wiki/Class_(philosophy)" title="Class">class</a> in that a property is considered to be distinct from the objects which possess it. Understanding how different individual entities can in some sense have some of the same properties is the basis of the <a href="/wiki/Problem_of_universals" title="Problem of universals">problem of universals</a>. The terms <b>attribute</b> and <b>quality</b> have similar meanings.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Modern_philosophy
<p><b>Modern philosophy</b> is a category of <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a> that originated in <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western Europe</a> in the 17th century, and is now common worldwide. It is not a specific doctrine or school, although there are certain assumptions common to much of it, which helps to distinguish it from earlier <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup></p>

<p><b>Čeplje</b> is a settlement in the <a href="/wiki/Municipality_of_Ko%C4%8Devje" title="Municipality of Kočevje">Municipality of Kočevje</a> in southern <a href="/wiki/Slovenia" title="Slovenia">Slovenia</a>. The area is part of the traditional region of <a href="/wiki/Lower_Carniola" title="Lower Carniola">Lower Carniola</a> and is now included in the <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Slovenia_Statistical_Region" title="Southeast Slovenia Statistical Region">Southeast Slovenia Statistical Region</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">3</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Municipality_of_Ko%C4%8Devje
<p>The <b>Municipality of Kočevje</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Municipalities_of_Slovenia" title="Municipalities of Slovenia">municipality</a> in southern <a href="/wiki/Slovenia" title="Slovenia">Slovenia</a>. The seat of the municipality is the city of <a href="/wiki/Ko%C4%8Devje" title="Kočevje">Kočevje</a>. Today it is part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jugovzhodna_Slovenija_statistical_region" title="Jugovzhodna Slovenija statistical region">Southeast Slovenia statistical region</a>. In terms of area, it is the largest municipality in Slovenia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SORS-MUN-KOCEVJE_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-SORS-MUN-KOCEVJE-1">1</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Municipalities_of_Slovenia
<p><b><a href="/wiki/Slovenia" title="Slovenia">Slovenia</a></b> is divided into 212 <a href="/wiki/Municipality" title="Municipality">municipalities</a>, of which 11 have urban status. Municipalities are further divided into local communities and districts.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Slovenia
<p><b>Slovenia</b>, officially the <b>Republic of Slovenia</b>, is a <a href="/wiki/Nation_state" title="Nation state">nation state</a> in southern <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">Central Europe</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Armstrong2007_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Armstrong2007-16">12</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">Note 2</a></sup> at the crossroads of main European cultural and trade routes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">17</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">18</a></sup> It borders <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a> to the west, <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a> to the north, <a href="/wiki/Croatia" title="Croatia">Croatia</a> to the south and southeast, and <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">Hungary</a> to the northeast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">19</a></sup> It covers 20,273 square kilometers and has a population of 2.05 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">20</a></sup> It is a <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_republic" title="Parliamentary republic">parliamentary republic</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">21</a></sup> and a member of the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> and <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">22</a></sup> Its capital and largest city is <a href="/wiki/Ljubljana" title="Ljubljana">Ljubljana</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">23</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nation_state
<p>The <b>nation state</b> is a <a href="/wiki/State_(polity)" title="State">state</a> that self-identifies as deriving its <a href="/wiki/Legitimacy_(political)" title="Legitimacy">political legitimacy</a> from serving as a <a href="/wiki/Sovereignty" title="Sovereignty">sovereign</a> entity for a <a href="/wiki/Nation" title="Nation">nation</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_state" title="Sovereign state">sovereign territorial unit</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup> The state is a <a href="/wiki/Politics" title="Politics">political</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geopolitics" title="Geopolitics">geopolitical</a> entity, while the nation is a <a href="/wiki/Culture" title="Culture">cultural</a> and/or <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_group" title="Ethnic group">ethnic</a> one; the term "nation state" implies that the two coincide geographically. Nation state formation took place at different times in different parts of the world, but has become the dominant form of state organization.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/State_(polity)
<p>A <b>state</b> is an organized <a href="/wiki/Community" title="Community">community</a> living under one <a href="/wiki/Government" title="Government">government</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oxford-state_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-oxford-state-1">1</a></sup> States may be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sovereign" title="Sovereign">sovereign</a>. The denomination state is also employed to <a href="/wiki/Federated_state" title="Federated state">federated states</a> that are members of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Federal_union" title="Federal union">federal union</a>, which is the <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_state" title="Sovereign state">sovereign state</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oxford-state_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-oxford-state-1">1</a></sup> Some states are subject to external <a href="/wiki/Sovereignty" title="Sovereignty">sovereignty</a> or <a href="/wiki/Hegemony" title="Hegemony">hegemony</a> where ultimate sovereignty lies in another state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">2</a></sup> The state can also be used to refer to the secular branches of government within a state, often as a manner of contrasting them with churches and <a href="/wiki/Civil_society" title="Civil society">civilian institutions</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Community
<p>The term <b>community</b> has two distinct meanings: 1) Community can refer to a usually small, <a href="/wiki/Level_of_analysis#Meso-level" title="Level of analysis">social unit</a> of any size that shares common <a href="/wiki/Value_(personal_and_cultural)" title="Value">values</a>. The term can also refer to the <a href="/wiki/Nation" title="Nation">national community</a> or <a href="/wiki/International_community" title="International community">international community</a>, and 2) in <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biology</a>, a community is a group of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interacting" title="Interacting">interacting</a> <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">living</a> <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">organisms</a> sharing a populated <a href="/wiki/Environment_(biophysical)" title="Environment">environment</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Level_of_analysis#Meso-level
<p>The term "<b>level of analysis</b>" is a social sciences term that points to the location, size, or scale of a research target. "Level of analysis" is distinct from the term "<a href="/wiki/Unit_of_observation" title="Unit of observation">unit of observation</a>" in that the former refers to a more or less integrated set of relationships while the latter refers to the distinct unit from which data have been or will be gathered. Together, the <a href="/wiki/Unit_of_observation" title="Unit of observation">unit of observation</a> and the level of analysis help define the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sample_population" title="Sample population">population</a> of a research enterprise.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Unit_of_observation
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Statistics" title="Statistics">statistics</a>, a <b>unit of observation</b> is the unit described by the <a href="/wiki/Data" title="Data">data</a> that one analyzes. For example, in a study of the <a href="/wiki/Demand_for_money" title="Demand for money">demand for money</a>, the unit of observation might be chosen as the individual, with different observations for a given point in time differing as to which individual they refer to; or the unit of observation might be the country, with different observations differing only in regard to the country they refer to. A study may have a differing unit of observation and <a href="/wiki/Unit_of_analysis" title="Unit of analysis">unit of analysis</a>: for example, in <a href="/wiki/Community" title="Community">community</a> research, the <a href="/wiki/Research_design" title="Research design">research design</a> may collect data at the individual level of observation but the <a href="/wiki/Unit_of_analysis" title="Unit of analysis">level of analysis</a> might be at the neighborhood level, drawing conclusions on neighborhood characteristics from data collected from individuals. Together, the unit of observation and the <a href="/wiki/Unit_of_analysis" title="Unit of analysis">level of analysis</a> define the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sample_population" title="Sample population">population</a> of a research enterprise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Statistics
<p><b>Statistics</b> is the study of the collection, organization, analysis, interpretation and presentation of <a href="/wiki/Data" title="Data">data</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dodge_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dodge-1">1</a></sup> It deals with all aspects of data including the planning of data collection in terms of the design of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Statistical_survey" title="Statistical survey">surveys</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Experimental_design" title="Experimental design">experiments</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dodge_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dodge-1">1</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Data
<p><b>Data</b> is a set of <a href="/wiki/Value_(computer_science)" title="Value">values</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qualitative_data" title="Qualitative data">qualitative</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quantitative_data" title="Quantitative data">quantitative</a> <a href="/wiki/Variable_and_attribute_(research)" title="Variable and attribute">variables</a>; restated, data are individual pieces of <a href="/wiki/Information" title="Information">information</a>. Data in <a href="/wiki/Computing" title="Computing">computing</a> are represented in a <a href="/wiki/Structure" title="Structure">structure</a> that is often <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tabular" title="Tabular">tabular</a>" title="Row">rows</a> and <a href="/wiki/Column_(database)" title="Column">columns</a>), a <a href="/wiki/Tree_structure" title="Tree structure">tree</a>" title="Set">set</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Node_(graph_theory)" title="Node">nodes</a> with <a href="/wiki/Parent" title="Parent">parent</a>-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Children" title="Children">children</a> <a href="/wiki/Relation_(database)" title="Relation">relationship</a>), or a <a href="/wiki/Graph_(mathematics)" title="Graph">graph</a>. Data are typically the results of <a href="/wiki/Measurement" title="Measurement">measurements</a> and can be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Data_visualisation" title="Data visualisation">visualised</a> using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Graph_(data_structure)" title="Graph">graphs</a> or <a href="/wiki/Image" title="Image">images</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Value_(computer_science)
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">computer science</a>, a <b>value</b> is an <a href="/wiki/Expression_(computer_science)" title="Expression">expression</a> which cannot be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evaluation_(computer_science)" title="Evaluation">evaluated</a> any further" title="Normal form">normal form</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMitchell199692_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMitchell199692-1">1</a></sup> The members of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Type_(computer_science)" title="Type">type</a> are the values of that type.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMitchell19969_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMitchell19969-2">2</a></sup> For example, the expression "1 + 2" is not a value as it can be <a href="/wiki/Reduction_(mathematics)" title="Reduction">reduced</a> to the expression "3". This expression cannot be reduced any further and therefore is a value.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Computer_science
<p><b>Computer science</b> is the <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">scientific</a> and practical approach to <a href="/wiki/Computation" title="Computation">computation</a> and its applications. It is the systematic study of the feasibility, structure, expression, and mechanization of the methodical processes that underlie the acquisition, representation, processing, storage, communication of, and access to information, whether such information is encoded in bits and bytes in a computer memory or transcribed engines and protein structures in a human cell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Computer_scientist" title="Computer scientist">computer scientist</a> specializes in the theory of computation and the design of computational systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">2</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Science
<p><b>Science</b> is a systematic enterprise that builds and organizes <a href="/wiki/Knowledge" title="Knowledge">knowledge</a> in the form of testable explanations and predictions about the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wilson_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-wilson-2">2</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">3</a></sup> In an older and closely related meaning, "science" also refers to a body of knowledge itself, of the type that can be rationally explained and reliably applied. A practitioner of science is known as a <a href="/wiki/Scientist" title="Scientist">scientist</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Knowledge
<p><b>Knowledge</b> is a familiarity with someone or something, which can include <a href="/wiki/Fact" title="Fact">facts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Information" title="Information">information</a>, <a href="/wiki/Description" title="Description">descriptions</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Skills" title="Skills">skills</a> acquired through <a href="/wiki/Experience" title="Experience">experience</a> or <a href="/wiki/Education" title="Education">education</a>. It can refer to the theoretical or practical understanding of a subject. It can be implicit or explicit; it can be more or less formal or systematic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>, the study of knowledge is called <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">epistemology</a>; the philosopher <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> famously defined knowledge as "<a href="/wiki/Justified_true_belief" title="Justified true belief">justified true belief</a>." However, no single agreed upon definition of knowledge exists, though there are numerous theories to explain it.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fact
<p>A <b>fact</b> is something that has really occurred or is actually the case. The usual test for a statement of fact is verifiability, that is whether it can be <a href="/wiki/Proof_(truth)" title="Proof">proven</a> to correspond to <a href="/wiki/Experience" title="Experience">experience</a>. Standard reference works are often used to check facts. <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">Scientific</a> facts are verified by repeatable <a href="/wiki/Experiment" title="Experiment">experiments</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proof_(truth)
<p>A <b>proof</b> is <a href="/wiki/Necessity_and_sufficiency" title="Necessity and sufficiency">sufficient</a> <a href="/wiki/Evidence" title="Evidence">evidence</a> or an <a href="/wiki/Argument" title="Argument">argument</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Truth" title="Truth">truth</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Proposition" title="Proposition">proposition</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">2</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">3</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">4</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Necessity_and_sufficiency
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>, <b>necessity</b> and <b>sufficiency</b> are implicational relationships between <a href="/wiki/Statement_(logic)" title="Statement">statements</a>.<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[]</sup> The assertion that one statement is a  <a href="/wiki/Condition_(philosophy)" title="Condition">condition</a> of another means that the former statement is true <a href="/wiki/If_and_only_if" title="If and only if">if and only if</a> the latter is true. That is, the two statements must be either simultaneously true or simultaneously false. In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ordinary_English" title="Ordinary English">ordinary English</a>, ‘necessary’ and ‘sufficient’ indicate relations between <a href="/wiki/Condition_(philosophy)" title="Condition">conditions</a> or states of affairs, not statements. Being male sibling is a necessary and sufficient condition for being a brother. Fred’s being a male sibling is necessary and sufficient for the truth of the statement that Fred is a brother.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Logic
<p><b>Logic</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-argumentative_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-argumentative-1">1</a></sup> has two meanings: first, it describes the use of valid <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reasoning" title="Reasoning">reasoning</a> in some activity; second, it names the <a href="/wiki/Normative" title="Normative">normative</a> study of reasoning or a branch thereof.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PopkinStroll1993_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-PopkinStroll1993-2">2</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jacquette2002_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-jacquette2002-3">3</a></sup> In the latter sense, it features most prominently in the subjects of <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">computer science</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reasoning
<p><b>Reason</b> is the capacity for <a href="/wiki/Consciousness" title="Consciousness">consciously</a> making sense of things, applying <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>, for establishing and verifying <a href="/wiki/Fact" title="Fact">facts</a>, and changing or justifying practices, institutions, and <a href="/wiki/Belief" title="Belief">beliefs</a> based on new or existing information.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup> It is closely associated with such characteristically <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">human</a> activities as <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a>, <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">language</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Art" title="Art">art</a>, and is normally considered to be a definitive characteristic of <a href="/wiki/Human_nature" title="Human nature">human nature</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">2</a></sup> The concept of reason is sometimes referred to as <a href="/wiki/Rationality" title="Rationality">rationality</a> and sometimes as <b>discursive reason</b>, in opposition to <a href="/wiki/Intuition_(psychology)" title="Intuition">intuitive reason</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">3</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Consciousness
<p><b>Consciousness</b> is the <a href="/wiki/Quality_(philosophy)" title="Quality">quality</a> or state of being aware of an external object or something within oneself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-van_Gulick2004_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-van_Gulick2004-2">2</a></sup> It has been defined as: <a href="/wiki/Sentience" title="Sentience">sentience</a>, <a href="/wiki/Awareness" title="Awareness">awareness</a>, <a href="/wiki/Subjectivity" title="Subjectivity">subjectivity</a>, the ability to <a href="/wiki/Experience" title="Experience">experience</a> or to <a href="/wiki/Feeling" title="Feeling">feel</a>, wakefulness, having a sense of <a href="/wiki/Self" title="Self">selfhood</a>, and the executive control system of the mind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Farthing1992Psychology_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Farthing1992Psychology-3">3</a></sup> Despite the difficulty in definition, many philosophers believe that there is a broadly shared underlying intuition about what consciousness is.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">4</a></sup> As <a href="/wiki/Max_Velmans" title="Max Velmans">Max Velmans</a> and Susan Schneider wrote in : "Anything that we are aware of at a given moment forms part of our consciousness, making conscious experience at once the most familiar and most mysterious aspect of our lives."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">5</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quality_(philosophy)
<p>A <b>quality</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup> is an attribute or a <a href="/wiki/Property_(philosophy)" title="Property">property</a>. Attributes are ascribable, by a subject, whereas properties are possessible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cargile.2C_1995_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cargile.2C_1995-2">2</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[]</sup> In contemporary <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a> the idea of qualities, and especially how to distinguish certain kinds of qualities from one another, remains controversial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cargile.2C_1995_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cargile.2C_1995-2">2</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Property_(philosophy)
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Modern_philosophy" title="Modern philosophy">modern philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>, a <b>property</b> is an attribute of an <a href="/wiki/Object_(philosophy)" title="Object">object</a>; a red object is said to have the property of redness. The property may be considered a form of object in its own right, able to possess other properties. A property however differs from individual objects in that it may be instantiated, and often in more than one thing. It differs from the logical/mathematical concept of <a href="/wiki/Class_(set_theory)" title="Class">class</a> by not having any concept of <a href="/wiki/Extensionality" title="Extensionality">extensionality</a>, and from the philosophical concept of <a href="/wiki/Class_(philosophy)" title="Class">class</a> in that a property is considered to be distinct from the objects which possess it. Understanding how different individual entities can in some sense have some of the same properties is the basis of the <a href="/wiki/Problem_of_universals" title="Problem of universals">problem of universals</a>. The terms <b>attribute</b> and <b>quality</b> have similar meanings.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Modern_philosophy
<p><b>Modern philosophy</b> is a category of <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a> that originated in <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western Europe</a> in the 17th century, and is now common worldwide. It is not a specific doctrine or school, although there are certain assumptions common to much of it, which helps to distinguish it from earlier <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Philosophy

http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Assembly_of_Manitoba_Chiefs
<p>The <b>Assembly of Manitoba Chiefs</b> is an organization of <a href="/wiki/First_Nations" title="First Nations">First Nations</a> leaders in <a href="/wiki/Manitoba" title="Manitoba">Manitoba</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>. The previous Grand Chief was <a href="/wiki/Ron_Evans_(politician)" title="Ron Evans">Ron Evans</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Norway_House_Cree_Nation" title="Norway House Cree Nation">Norway House Cree Nation</a>. The current Grand Chief is <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Derek_Nepinak&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Derek Nepinak">Derek Nepinak</a> of <a href="/wiki/Pine_Creek_First_Nation" title="Pine Creek First Nation">Pine Creek First Nation</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/First_Nations
<p>The <b>First Nations</b> are the various <a href="/wiki/Aboriginal_peoples_in_Canada" title="Aboriginal peoples in Canada">Aboriginal peoples in Canada</a> who are neither <a href="/wiki/Inuit" title="Inuit">Inuit</a> nor <a href="/wiki/M%C3%A9tis_people_(Canada)" title="Métis people">Métis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">2</a></sup> There are currently over 630<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">3</a></sup> recognized <a href="/wiki/List_of_First_Nations_governments" title="List of First Nations governments">First Nations governments or bands</a> spread across <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>, roughly half of which are in the provinces of <a href="/wiki/Ontario" title="Ontario">Ontario</a> and <a href="/wiki/British_Columbia" title="British Columbia">British Columbia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-one_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-one-4">4</a></sup> The total population is nearly 700,000 people. Under the <a href="/wiki/Employment_equity_(Canada)" title="Employment equity">Employment Equity Act</a>, First Nations are a "designated group", along with women, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Visible_minorities" title="Visible minorities">visible minorities</a>, and people with physical or mental disabilities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">5</a></sup> First Nations are not defined as a <a href="/wiki/Visible_minority" title="Visible minority">visible minority</a> under the Act or by the criteria of <a href="/wiki/Statistics_Canada" title="Statistics Canada">Statistics Canada</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">6</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aboriginal_peoples_in_Canada
<p><b>Aboriginal People in Canada</b> are the <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">indigenous peoples in North America</a> within the boundaries of present-day <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>. They comprise the <a href="/wiki/First_Nations" title="First Nations">First Nations</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-First_Nations_Culture_Areas_Index_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-First_Nations_Culture_Areas_Index-2">2</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Inuit" title="Inuit">Inuit</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ICCcharter_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-ICCcharter-3">3</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/M%C3%A9tis_people_(Canada)" title="Métis people">Métis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ToddThornton2001_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-ToddThornton2001-4">4</a></sup> The descriptors "Indian" and "<a href="/wiki/Eskimo" title="Eskimo">Eskimo</a>" have largely fallen into disuse in Canada and are commonly considered <a href="/wiki/Racism" title="Racism">pejorative</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">5</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-indian_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-indian-6">6</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kaplan_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-kaplan-7">7</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas
<p>The <b>indigenous peoples of the Americas</b> are the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pre-Columbian" title="Pre-Columbian">pre-Columbian</a> inhabitants of <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indigenous_people_of_South_America" title="Indigenous people of South America">South America</a> and their descendants.  is a common term in <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish-speaking</a> countries.  is used in <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a>, whereas "Amerindian" is used in <a href="/wiki/Guyana" title="Guyana">Guyana</a> but not commonly in other countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">22</a></sup> Indigenous peoples are commonly known in <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a> as <a href="/wiki/Aboriginal_peoples_in_Canada" title="Aboriginal peoples in Canada">Aboriginal peoples</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Canada_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Canada-23">23</a></sup> which include <a href="/wiki/First_Nations" title="First Nations">First Nations</a>, <a href="/wiki/Inuit" title="Inuit">Inuit</a>, and <a href="/wiki/M%C3%A9tis_people_(Canada)" title="Métis people">Métis</a> peoples. Indigenous peoples of the United States are commonly known as <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">Native Americans</a> or American Indians, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alaskan_Natives" title="Alaskan Natives">Alaskan Natives</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Office_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Office-24">24</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pre-Columbian
<p>The <b>pre-Columbian era</b> incorporates all <a href="/wiki/Archaeology_of_the_Americas" title="Archaeology of the Americas">period subdivisions</a> in the <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Americas" title="History of the Americas">history and prehistory of the Americas</a> before the appearance of significant European influences on the American continents, spanning the time of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Migration_to_the_New_World" title="Migration to the New World">original settlement</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Upper_Paleolithic" title="Upper Paleolithic">Upper Paleolithic</a> period to <a href="/wiki/European_colonization_of_the_Americas" title="European colonization of the Americas">European colonization</a> during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_Modern_period" title="Early Modern period">Early Modern period</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaeology_of_the_Americas
<p>The <b>archaeology of the Americas</b> is the study of the <a href="/wiki/Archaeology" title="Archaeology">archaeology</a> of <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>, <a href="/wiki/Central_America" title="Central America">Central America</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">Caribbean</a>. This includes the study of pre-historic/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pre-Columbian" title="Pre-Columbian">Pre-Columbian</a> and historic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indigenous_Peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous Peoples of the Americas">indigenous American peoples</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaeology
<p><b>Archaeology</b>, or <b>archeology</b>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Society_for_American_Archaeology_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Society_for_American_Archaeology-1">1</a></sup> is the study of <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">human</a> activity in the past, primarily through the recovery and <a href="/wiki/Analysis" title="Analysis">analysis</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Material_culture" title="Material culture">material culture</a> and environmental data that they have left behind, which includes <a href="/wiki/Artifact_(archaeology)" title="Artifact">artifacts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Architecture" title="Architecture">architecture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biofact_(archaeology)" title="Biofact">biofacts</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cultural_landscapes" title="Cultural landscapes">cultural landscapes</a>. Because archaeology employs a wide range of different procedures, it can be considered to be both a <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Humanities" title="Humanities">humanity</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Renfrew_Bahn1991_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Renfrew_Bahn1991-2">2</a></sup> and in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> it is thought of as a branch of <a href="/wiki/Anthropology" title="Anthropology">anthropology</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Haviland_et_al_2005_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Haviland_et_al_2005-3">3</a></sup> although in <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> it is viewed as a separate discipline.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human
<p><b>Humans</b> are <a href="/wiki/Primate" title="Primate">primates</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Family_(biology)" title="Family">family</a> <a href="/wiki/Hominidae" title="Hominidae">Hominidae</a>, and the only <a class="extiw" href="//en.wiktionary.org/wiki/extant" title="wikt:extant">extant</a> <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genus</a> .<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">2</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">3</a></sup> Humans are distinguished from other primates by their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bipedal" title="Bipedal">bipedal</a> locomotion, and especially by their relatively <a href="/wiki/Encephalization" title="Encephalization">larger</a> <a href="/wiki/Human_brain" title="Human brain">brain</a> with its particularly well developed <a href="/wiki/Neocortex" title="Neocortex">neocortex</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prefrontal_cortex" title="Prefrontal cortex">prefrontal cortex</a> and <a href="/wiki/Temporal_lobe" title="Temporal lobe">temporal lobes</a>, which enable high levels of abstract <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reasoning" title="Reasoning">reasoning</a>, <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">language</a>, <a href="/wiki/Problem_solving" title="Problem solving">problem solving</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Culture" title="Culture">culture</a> through social learning. Humans use <a href="/wiki/Tool" title="Tool">tools</a> to a much higher degree than any other animal, and are the only extant species known to build <a href="/wiki/Fire" title="Fire">fires</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cooking" title="Cooking">cook their food</a>, as well as the only known species to <a href="/wiki/Clothing" title="Clothing">clothe</a> themselves and create and use numerous other <a href="/wiki/Technology" title="Technology">technologies</a> and <a href="/wiki/Art" title="Art">arts</a>. The scientific study of humans is the discipline of <a href="/wiki/Anthropology" title="Anthropology">anthropology</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Primate
<p>A <b>primate</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammal</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Order_(biology)" title="Order">order</a> <b>Primates</b>,.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">2</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">3</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Taxonomy_(biology)" title="Taxonomy">taxonomy</a>, primates include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prosimians" title="Prosimians">prosimians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Simian" title="Simian">simians</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goodman2_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goodman2-4">4</a></sup> Primates arose from ancestors that lived in the trees of tropical forests; many primate characteristics represent adaptations to life in this challenging three-dimensional environment. Most primate species remain at least partly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arboreal" title="Arboreal">arboreal</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mammal
<p><b>Mammals</b>" title="Class">class</a> <b>Mammalia</b> ) are a <a href="/wiki/Clade" title="Clade">clade</a> of <a href="/wiki/Endotherm" title="Endotherm">endothermic</a> <a href="/wiki/Amniote" title="Amniote">amniotes</a> distinguished from the <a href="/wiki/Reptile" title="Reptile">reptiles</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">birds</a> by the possession of <a href="/wiki/Hair" title="Hair">hair</a>, three <a href="/wiki/Ossicles" title="Ossicles">middle ear bones</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mammary_gland" title="Mammary gland">mammary glands</a> in females, and a <a href="/wiki/Neocortex" title="Neocortex">neocortex</a>. The mammalian brain regulates body temperature and the <a href="/wiki/Circulatory_system" title="Circulatory system">circulatory system</a>, including the four-chambered <a href="/wiki/Heart" title="Heart">heart</a>. The mammals include the largest animals on the planet, the <a href="/wiki/Rorqual" title="Rorqual">rorquals</a> and some other whales, as well as some of the most intelligent, such as <a href="/wiki/Elephant" title="Elephant">elephants</a>, some <a href="/wiki/Primate" title="Primate">primates</a> and some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cetacean" title="Cetacean">cetaceans</a>. The basic body type is a four-legged land-borne animal, but some mammals are adapted for <a href="/wiki/Marine_mammal" title="Marine mammal">life at sea</a>, in the air, in the trees, or on two legs. The largest group of mammals, the <a href="/wiki/Eutheria" title="Eutheria">placentals</a>, have a <a href="/wiki/Placenta" title="Placenta">placenta</a> which feeds the offspring during pregnancy. Mammals range in size from the 30–40 mm <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bumblebee_bat" title="Bumblebee bat">bumblebee bat</a> to the 33-meter <a href="/wiki/Blue_whale" title="Blue whale">blue whale</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clade
<p>A <b>clade</b> or <b>monophylum</b> is a group consisting of an ancestor and all its descendants, a single "branch" on the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tree_of_life_(science)" title="Tree of life">tree of life</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dupuis_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dupuis-1">1</a></sup> The ancestor may be an individual, a population or even a <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a>. Many familiar groups, <a href="/wiki/Rodent" title="Rodent">rodents</a> and <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a> for example, are clades; others, like <a href="/wiki/Lizard" title="Lizard">lizards</a> and <a href="/wiki/Monkey" title="Monkey">monkeys</a>, are not.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tree_of_life_(science)
<p>The <b>tree of life</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Metaphor" title="Metaphor">metaphor</a> used to describe the relationships between organisms, both living and extinct. Its use dates back to at least the early 1800s. It was employed by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Darwin" title="Charles Darwin">Charles Darwin</a> to express the concept of the branching divergence of varieties and then species in a process of <a href="/wiki/Common_descent" title="Common descent">common descent</a> from ancestors. <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Haeckel" title="Ernst Haeckel">Ernst Haeckel</a> coined the term <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phylogeny" title="Phylogeny">phylogeny</a> for the evolutionary relationships of species through time, and went further than Darwin in proposing phylogenic histories of life. The modern development of this idea is called the <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetic_tree" title="Phylogenetic tree">phylogenetic tree</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metaphor
<p>A <b>metaphor</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Figure_of_speech" title="Figure of speech">figure of speech</a> that describes a subject by asserting that it is, on some point of comparison, the same as another otherwise unrelated object. Metaphor is a type of <a href="/wiki/Analogy" title="Analogy">analogy</a> and is closely related to other <a href="/wiki/Rhetoric" title="Rhetoric">rhetorical</a> <a href="/wiki/Literal_and_figurative_language" title="Literal and figurative language">figures of speech</a> that achieve their effects via association, comparison or resemblance including <a href="/wiki/Allegory" title="Allegory">allegory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hyperbole" title="Hyperbole">hyperbole</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Simile" title="Simile">simile</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Figure_of_speech
<p>A <b>figure of speech</b> is the use of a <a href="/wiki/Word" title="Word">word</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Phrase" title="Phrase">phrase</a>, which transcends its literal interpretation. It can be a special repetition, arrangement or omission of words with literal meaning, or a <a href="/wiki/Phrase" title="Phrase">phrase</a> with a specialized meaning not based on the literal meaning of the words in it, as in <a href="/wiki/Idiom" title="Idiom">idiom</a>, <a href="/wiki/Metaphor" title="Metaphor">metaphor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Simile" title="Simile">simile</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hyperbole" title="Hyperbole">hyperbole</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Personification" title="Personification">personification</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Synecdoche" title="Synecdoche">synecdoche</a>. Figures of speech often provide emphasis, freshness of expression, or clarity. However, clarity may also suffer from their use, as any figure of speech introduces an ambiguity between literal and figurative interpretation. A figure of speech is sometimes called a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rhetorical_figure" title="Rhetorical figure">rhetorical figure</a> or a locution.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Word
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Linguistics" title="Linguistics">linguistics</a>, a <b>word</b> is the smallest element that may be uttered in isolation with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semantic" title="Semantic">semantic</a> or <a href="/wiki/Pragmatics" title="Pragmatics">pragmatic</a> content" title="Meaning">meaning</a>). This contrasts with a <a href="/wiki/Morpheme" title="Morpheme">morpheme</a>, which is the smallest unit of meaning but will not necessarily stand on its own. A word may consist of a single morpheme, or several, whereas a morpheme may not be able to stand on its own as a word. A complex word will typically include a <a href="/wiki/Root_(linguistics)" title="Root">root</a> and one or more <a href="/wiki/Affix" title="Affix">affixes</a>, or more than one root in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Compound_word" title="Compound word">compound</a>. Words can be put together to build larger elements of <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">language</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Phrase" title="Phrase">phrases</a>, <a href="/wiki/Clause" title="Clause">clauses</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sentence_(linguistics)" title="Sentence">sentences</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Linguistics
<p><b>Linguistics</b> is the <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">scientific</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup> study of <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">language</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">2</a></sup> There are broadly three aspects to the study, which include language form, language meaning, and language in context.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">3</a></sup> The earliest known activities in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Descriptive_linguistics" title="Descriptive linguistics">description of language</a> have been attributed to <a href="/wiki/P%C4%81%E1%B9%87ini" title="Pāṇini">Pāṇini</a> around 500 BCE, with his analysis of <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> in .<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">4</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Science
<p><b>Science</b> is a systematic enterprise that builds and organizes <a href="/wiki/Knowledge" title="Knowledge">knowledge</a> in the form of testable explanations and predictions about the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wilson_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-wilson-2">2</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">3</a></sup> In an older and closely related meaning, "science" also refers to a body of knowledge itself, of the type that can be rationally explained and reliably applied. A practitioner of science is known as a <a href="/wiki/Scientist" title="Scientist">scientist</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Knowledge
<p><b>Knowledge</b> is a familiarity with someone or something, which can include <a href="/wiki/Fact" title="Fact">facts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Information" title="Information">information</a>, <a href="/wiki/Description" title="Description">descriptions</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Skills" title="Skills">skills</a> acquired through <a href="/wiki/Experience" title="Experience">experience</a> or <a href="/wiki/Education" title="Education">education</a>. It can refer to the theoretical or practical understanding of a subject. It can be implicit or explicit; it can be more or less formal or systematic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>, the study of knowledge is called <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">epistemology</a>; the philosopher <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> famously defined knowledge as "<a href="/wiki/Justified_true_belief" title="Justified true belief">justified true belief</a>." However, no single agreed upon definition of knowledge exists, though there are numerous theories to explain it.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fact
<p>A <b>fact</b> is something that has really occurred or is actually the case. The usual test for a statement of fact is verifiability, that is whether it can be <a href="/wiki/Proof_(truth)" title="Proof">proven</a> to correspond to <a href="/wiki/Experience" title="Experience">experience</a>. Standard reference works are often used to check facts. <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">Scientific</a> facts are verified by repeatable <a href="/wiki/Experiment" title="Experiment">experiments</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proof_(truth)
<p>A <b>proof</b> is <a href="/wiki/Necessity_and_sufficiency" title="Necessity and sufficiency">sufficient</a> <a href="/wiki/Evidence" title="Evidence">evidence</a> or an <a href="/wiki/Argument" title="Argument">argument</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Truth" title="Truth">truth</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Proposition" title="Proposition">proposition</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">2</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">3</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">4</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Necessity_and_sufficiency
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>, <b>necessity</b> and <b>sufficiency</b> are implicational relationships between <a href="/wiki/Statement_(logic)" title="Statement">statements</a>.<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[]</sup> The assertion that one statement is a  <a href="/wiki/Condition_(philosophy)" title="Condition">condition</a> of another means that the former statement is true <a href="/wiki/If_and_only_if" title="If and only if">if and only if</a> the latter is true. That is, the two statements must be either simultaneously true or simultaneously false. In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ordinary_English" title="Ordinary English">ordinary English</a>, ‘necessary’ and ‘sufficient’ indicate relations between <a href="/wiki/Condition_(philosophy)" title="Condition">conditions</a> or states of affairs, not statements. Being male sibling is a necessary and sufficient condition for being a brother. Fred’s being a male sibling is necessary and sufficient for the truth of the statement that Fred is a brother.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Logic
<p><b>Logic</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-argumentative_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-argumentative-1">1</a></sup> has two meanings: first, it describes the use of valid <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reasoning" title="Reasoning">reasoning</a> in some activity; second, it names the <a href="/wiki/Normative" title="Normative">normative</a> study of reasoning or a branch thereof.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PopkinStroll1993_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-PopkinStroll1993-2">2</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jacquette2002_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-jacquette2002-3">3</a></sup> In the latter sense, it features most prominently in the subjects of <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">computer science</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reasoning
<p><b>Reason</b> is the capacity for <a href="/wiki/Consciousness" title="Consciousness">consciously</a> making sense of things, applying <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>, for establishing and verifying <a href="/wiki/Fact" title="Fact">facts</a>, and changing or justifying practices, institutions, and <a href="/wiki/Belief" title="Belief">beliefs</a> based on new or existing information.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup> It is closely associated with such characteristically <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">human</a> activities as <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a>, <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">language</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Art" title="Art">art</a>, and is normally considered to be a definitive characteristic of <a href="/wiki/Human_nature" title="Human nature">human nature</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">2</a></sup> The concept of reason is sometimes referred to as <a href="/wiki/Rationality" title="Rationality">rationality</a> and sometimes as <b>discursive reason</b>, in opposition to <a href="/wiki/Intuition_(psychology)" title="Intuition">intuitive reason</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">3</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Consciousness
<p><b>Consciousness</b> is the <a href="/wiki/Quality_(philosophy)" title="Quality">quality</a> or state of being aware of an external object or something within oneself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-van_Gulick2004_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-van_Gulick2004-2">2</a></sup> It has been defined as: <a href="/wiki/Sentience" title="Sentience">sentience</a>, <a href="/wiki/Awareness" title="Awareness">awareness</a>, <a href="/wiki/Subjectivity" title="Subjectivity">subjectivity</a>, the ability to <a href="/wiki/Experience" title="Experience">experience</a> or to <a href="/wiki/Feeling" title="Feeling">feel</a>, wakefulness, having a sense of <a href="/wiki/Self" title="Self">selfhood</a>, and the executive control system of the mind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Farthing1992Psychology_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Farthing1992Psychology-3">3</a></sup> Despite the difficulty in definition, many philosophers believe that there is a broadly shared underlying intuition about what consciousness is.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">4</a></sup> As <a href="/wiki/Max_Velmans" title="Max Velmans">Max Velmans</a> and Susan Schneider wrote in : "Anything that we are aware of at a given moment forms part of our consciousness, making conscious experience at once the most familiar and most mysterious aspect of our lives."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">5</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quality_(philosophy)
<p>A <b>quality</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup> is an attribute or a <a href="/wiki/Property_(philosophy)" title="Property">property</a>. Attributes are ascribable, by a subject, whereas properties are possessible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cargile.2C_1995_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cargile.2C_1995-2">2</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[]</sup> In contemporary <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a> the idea of qualities, and especially how to distinguish certain kinds of qualities from one another, remains controversial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cargile.2C_1995_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cargile.2C_1995-2">2</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Property_(philosophy)
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Modern_philosophy" title="Modern philosophy">modern philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>, a <b>property</b> is an attribute of an <a href="/wiki/Object_(philosophy)" title="Object">object</a>; a red object is said to have the property of redness. The property may be considered a form of object in its own right, able to possess other properties. A property however differs from individual objects in that it may be instantiated, and often in more than one thing. It differs from the logical/mathematical concept of <a href="/wiki/Class_(set_theory)" title="Class">class</a> by not having any concept of <a href="/wiki/Extensionality" title="Extensionality">extensionality</a>, and from the philosophical concept of <a href="/wiki/Class_(philosophy)" title="Class">class</a> in that a property is considered to be distinct from the objects which possess it. Understanding how different individual entities can in some sense have some of the same properties is the basis of the <a href="/wiki/Problem_of_universals" title="Problem of universals">problem of universals</a>. The terms <b>attribute</b> and <b>quality</b> have similar meanings.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Modern_philosophy
<p><b>Modern philosophy</b> is a category of <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a> that originated in <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western Europe</a> in the 17th century, and is now common worldwide. It is not a specific doctrine or school, although there are certain assumptions common to much of it, which helps to distinguish it from earlier <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Philosophy
