Test 16
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Z
<p><b>Z</b> is the twenty-sixth and final <a href="/wiki/Letter_(alphabet)" title="Letter">letter</a> of the <a href="/wiki/ISO_basic_Latin_alphabet" title="ISO basic Latin alphabet">ISO basic Latin alphabet</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Letter_(alphabet)
<p>A <b>letter</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Grapheme" title="Grapheme">grapheme</a> in an <a href="/wiki/Alphabet" title="Alphabet">alphabetic</a> system of <a href="/wiki/Writing" title="Writing">writing</a>, such as the <a href="/wiki/Greek_alphabet" title="Greek alphabet">Greek alphabet</a> and its descendants. Letters compose <a href="/wiki/Phoneme" title="Phoneme">phonemes</a> and each phoneme represents a <a href="/wiki/Phone_(phonetics)" title="Phone">phone</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Spoken_language" title="Spoken language">spoken form of the language</a>. Letters also appear in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abjads" title="Abjads">abjads</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Grapheme
<p>A <b>grapheme</b> is the smallest semantically distinguishing unit in a <a href="/wiki/Writing" title="Writing">written language</a>, analogous to the <a href="/wiki/Phoneme" title="Phoneme">phonemes</a> of spoken languages. A grapheme may or may not carry meaning by itself, and may or may not correspond to a single phoneme. Graphemes include <a href="/wiki/Letter_(alphabet)" title="Letter">alphabetic letters</a>, <a href="/wiki/Typographic_ligature" title="Typographic ligature">typographic ligatures</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chinese_characters" title="Chinese characters">Chinese characters</a>, <a href="/wiki/Numerical_digit" title="Numerical digit">numerical digits</a>, <a href="/wiki/Punctuation" title="Punctuation">punctuation</a> marks, and other individual symbols of any of the world's <a href="/wiki/Writing_system" title="Writing system">writing systems</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Writing
<p><b>Writing</b> is a medium of <a href="/wiki/Communication" title="Communication">communication</a> that represents <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">language</a> through the inscription of signs and symbols. In most languages, writing is a complement to speech or spoken language. Within a language system, writing relies on many of the same structures as speech, such as <a href="/wiki/Vocabulary" title="Vocabulary">vocabulary</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grammar" title="Grammar">grammar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Semantics" title="Semantics">semantics</a>, with the added dependency of a system of signs or symbols, usually in the form of a formal <a href="/wiki/Alphabet" title="Alphabet">alphabet</a>. The result of writing is generally called , and the recipient of text is called a reader. Motivations for writing include <a href="/wiki/Publication" title="Publication">publication</a>, <a href="/wiki/Storytelling" title="Storytelling">storytelling</a>, <a href="/wiki/Correspondence_chess" title="Correspondence chess">correspondence</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diary" title="Diary">diary</a>. Writing has been instrumental in keeping <a href="/wiki/History" title="History">history</a>, dissemination of knowledge through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_media" title="The media">the media</a> and the formation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Legal_systems" title="Legal systems">legal systems</a>. Under the influence of technologies such as data storage and computer networks, the pace of correspondence and potential for collaboration increased.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Communication
<p><b>Communication</b> is the activity of conveying <a href="/wiki/Information" title="Information">information</a> through the exchange of thoughts, messages, or information, as by speech, visuals, signals, writing, or behavior. It is the meaningful exchange of information between two or more living creatures.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Information
<p><b>Information</b>, in its most restricted technical sense, is a <a href="/wiki/Sequence" title="Sequence">sequence</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Symbols" title="Symbols">symbols</a> that can be interpreted as a <a href="/wiki/Message" title="Message">message</a>. Information can be recorded as <a href="/wiki/Sign_(semiotics)" title="Sign">signs</a>, or transmitted as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Signal_(electronics)" title="Signal">signals</a>. Information is any kind of <a class="extiw" href="//en.wiktionary.org/wiki/event" title="wikt:event">event</a> that affects the <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamic_state" title="Thermodynamic state">state</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Dynamical_system" title="Dynamical system">dynamic system</a> that can interpret the information.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sequence
<p>A <b>sequence</b> is an ordered list. Like a <a href="/wiki/Set_(mathematics)" title="Set">set</a>, it contains <a href="/wiki/Element_(mathematics)" title="Element">members</a>. The number of ordered elements is called the  of the sequence. Unlike a set, order matters, and exactly the same elements can appear multiple times at different positions in the sequence. Most precisely, a sequence can be defined as a <a href="/wiki/Function_(mathematics)" title="Function">function</a> whose domain is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Countable" title="Countable">countable</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Totally_ordered" title="Totally ordered">totally ordered</a> set, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_numbers" title="Natural numbers">natural numbers</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Set_(mathematics)
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, a <b>set</b> is a collection of distinct objects, considered as an <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_object" title="Mathematical object">object</a> in its own right. For example, the numbers 2, 4, and 6 are distinct objects when considered separately, but when they are considered collectively they form a single set of size three, written {2,4,6}. Sets are one of the most fundamental concepts in mathematics. Developed at the end of the 19th century, set theory is now a ubiquitous part of mathematics, and can be used as a foundation from which nearly all of mathematics can be derived. In <a href="/wiki/Mathematics_education" title="Mathematics education">mathematics education</a>, elementary topics such as <a href="/wiki/Venn_diagram" title="Venn diagram">Venn diagrams</a> are taught at a young age, while more advanced concepts are taught as part of a university degree. The term itself was coined by Bolzano in his work <a href="/wiki/The_Paradoxes_of_the_Infinite" title="The Paradoxes of the Infinite">The Paradoxes of the Infinite</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics
<p><b>Mathematics</b> is the abstract study of topics such as <a href="/wiki/Quantity" title="Quantity">quantity</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-OED-2">2</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Structure" title="Structure">structure</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kneebone_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kneebone-3">3</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Space" title="Space">space</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-OED-2">2</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Calculus" title="Calculus">change</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LaTorre_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-LaTorre-4">4</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ramana_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ramana-5">5</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ziegler_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ziegler-6">6</a></sup> There is a range of views among mathematicians and philosophers as to the exact scope and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Definition_of_mathematics" title="Definition of mathematics">definition of mathematics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mura_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mura-7">7</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Runge_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Runge-8">8</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantity
<p><b>Quantity</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Property_(philosophy)" title="Property">property</a> that can exist as a <a href="/wiki/Magnitude_(mathematics)" title="Magnitude">magnitude</a> or <a href="/wiki/Counting" title="Counting">multitude</a>. Quantities can be compared in terms of "more", "less" or "equal", or by assigning a numerical value in terms of a unit of measurement. Quantity is among the basic <a href="/wiki/Class_(philosophy)" title="Class">classes</a> of things along with <a href="/wiki/Quality_(philosophy)" title="Quality">quality</a>, <a href="/wiki/Substance_theory" title="Substance theory">substance</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Change_(mathematics)" title="Change">change</a>, and relation. Being a fundamental term, quantity is used to refer to any type of quantitative properties or attributes of things. Some quantities are such by their inner nature, while others are functioning as states of things such as heavy and light, long and short, broad and narrow, small and great, or much and little. A small quantity is sometimes referred to as a <b>quantulum</b>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Property_(philosophy)
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Modern_philosophy" title="Modern philosophy">modern philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>, a <b>property</b> is an attribute of an <a href="/wiki/Object_(philosophy)" title="Object">object</a>; a red object is said to have the property of redness. The property may be considered a form of object in its own right, able to possess other properties. A property however differs from individual objects in that it may be instantiated, and often in more than one thing. It differs from the logical/mathematical concept of <a href="/wiki/Class_(set_theory)" title="Class">class</a> by not having any concept of <a href="/wiki/Extensionality" title="Extensionality">extensionality</a>, and from the philosophical concept of <a href="/wiki/Class_(philosophy)" title="Class">class</a> in that a property is considered to be distinct from the objects which possess it. Understanding how different individual entities can in some sense have some of the same properties is the basis of the <a href="/wiki/Problem_of_universals" title="Problem of universals">problem of universals</a>. The terms <b>attribute</b> and <b>quality</b> have similar meanings.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Modern_philosophy
<p><b>Modern philosophy</b> is a category of <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a> that originated in <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western Europe</a> in the 17th century, and is now common worldwide. It is not a specific doctrine or school, although there are certain assumptions common to much of it, which helps to distinguish it from earlier <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Philosophy

Test 17
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrogen_fuel
<p><b>Hydrogen fuel</b> is a zero-emission <a href="/wiki/Fuel" title="Fuel">fuel</a> which uses <a href="/wiki/Electrochemical_cell" title="Electrochemical cell">electrochemical cells</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Combustion" title="Combustion">combustion</a> in internal engines, to power vehicles and electric devices. It is also used in the propulsion of spacecraft and can potentially be mass-produced and commercialized for passenger vehicles and aircraft.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fuel
<p><b>Fuels</b> are any materials that store <a href="/wiki/Potential_energy" title="Potential energy">potential energy</a> in forms that can be practicably released and used as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heat_energy" title="Heat energy">heat energy</a>. The concept originally applied solely to those materials storing energy in the form of <a href="/wiki/Chemical_energy" title="Chemical energy">chemical energy</a> that could be released through <a href="/wiki/Combustion" title="Combustion">combustion</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup> but the concept has since been also applied to other sources of heat energy such as <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power" title="Nuclear power">nuclear energy</a>, as well as releases of chemical energy released through non-combustion <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxidation" title="Oxidation">oxidation</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Potential_energy
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a>, <b>potential energy</b> is <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a> stored in a system of forcefully interacting physical entities .<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/International_System_of_Units" title="International System of Units">SI unit</a> for measuring work and energy is the <a href="/wiki/Joule" title="Joule">joule</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Physics
<p><b>Physics</b> is the <a href="/wiki/Natural_science" title="Natural science">natural science</a> that involves the study of <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">matter</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-feynman_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-feynman-6">6</a></sup> and its <a href="/wiki/Motion_(physics)" title="Motion">motion</a> through space and time, along with related concepts such as <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Force" title="Force">force</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">7</a></sup> More broadly, it is the general analysis of <a href="/wiki/Nature" title="Nature">nature</a>, conducted in order to understand how the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a> behaves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">8</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">9</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">10</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Natural_science
<p>The <b>natural sciences</b> are the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sciences" title="Sciences">sciences</a> that seek to elucidate the rules that govern the natural world through scientific methods, the cornerstone of which is <a href="/wiki/Measurement" title="Measurement">measured</a> by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Numerical_data" title="Numerical data">quantitative</a> data.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELedoux200234_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELedoux200234-1">1</a></sup> Based on <a href="/wiki/Formal_science" title="Formal science">formal sciences</a>, they also attempt to provide mathematical <a href="/wiki/Scientific_modelling" title="Scientific modelling">models</a> of natural processes. The term "natural science" is used to distinguish the subject from the <a href="/wiki/Social_science" title="Social science">social sciences</a>, such as economics, psychology and sociology, which apply the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific method</a> to the study of human behavior and social patterns; the <a href="/wiki/Humanities" title="Humanities">humanities</a>, which use a critical or analytical approach to study the human condition; and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Formal_sciences" title="Formal sciences">formal sciences</a> such as mathematics and logic, which use an <a href="/wiki/A_priori_and_a_posteriori" title="A priori and a posteriori">a priori</a>, as opposed to empirical methodology to study formal systems.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sciences
<p><b>Science</b> is a systematic enterprise that builds and organizes <a href="/wiki/Knowledge" title="Knowledge">knowledge</a> in the form of testable explanations and predictions about the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wilson_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-wilson-2">2</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">3</a></sup> In an older and closely related meaning, "science" also refers to a body of knowledge itself, of the type that can be rationally explained and reliably applied. A practitioner of science is known as a <a href="/wiki/Scientist" title="Scientist">scientist</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Knowledge
<p><b>Knowledge</b> is a familiarity with someone or something, which can include <a href="/wiki/Fact" title="Fact">facts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Information" title="Information">information</a>, <a href="/wiki/Description" title="Description">descriptions</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Skills" title="Skills">skills</a> acquired through <a href="/wiki/Experience" title="Experience">experience</a> or <a href="/wiki/Education" title="Education">education</a>. It can refer to the theoretical or practical understanding of a subject. It can be implicit or explicit; it can be more or less formal or systematic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>, the study of knowledge is called <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">epistemology</a>; the philosopher <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> famously defined knowledge as "<a href="/wiki/Justified_true_belief" title="Justified true belief">justified true belief</a>." However, no single agreed upon definition of knowledge exists, though there are numerous theories to explain it.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fact
<p>A <b>fact</b> is something that has really occurred or is actually the case. The usual test for a statement of fact is verifiability, that is whether it can be <a href="/wiki/Proof_(truth)" title="Proof">proven</a> to correspond to <a href="/wiki/Experience" title="Experience">experience</a>. Standard reference works are often used to check facts. <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">Scientific</a> facts are verified by repeatable <a href="/wiki/Experiment" title="Experiment">experiments</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proof_(truth)
<p>A <b>proof</b> is <a href="/wiki/Necessity_and_sufficiency" title="Necessity and sufficiency">sufficient</a> <a href="/wiki/Evidence" title="Evidence">evidence</a> or an <a href="/wiki/Argument" title="Argument">argument</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Truth" title="Truth">truth</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Proposition" title="Proposition">proposition</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">2</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">3</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">4</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Necessity_and_sufficiency
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>, <b>necessity</b> and <b>sufficiency</b> are implicational relationships between <a href="/wiki/Statement_(logic)" title="Statement">statements</a>.<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[]</sup> The assertion that one statement is a  <a href="/wiki/Condition_(philosophy)" title="Condition">condition</a> of another means that the former statement is true <a href="/wiki/If_and_only_if" title="If and only if">if and only if</a> the latter is true. That is, the two statements must be either simultaneously true or simultaneously false. In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ordinary_English" title="Ordinary English">ordinary English</a>, ‘necessary’ and ‘sufficient’ indicate relations between <a href="/wiki/Condition_(philosophy)" title="Condition">conditions</a> or states of affairs, not statements. Being male sibling is a necessary and sufficient condition for being a brother. Fred’s being a male sibling is necessary and sufficient for the truth of the statement that Fred is a brother.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Logic
<p><b>Logic</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-argumentative_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-argumentative-1">1</a></sup> has two meanings: first, it describes the use of valid <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reasoning" title="Reasoning">reasoning</a> in some activity; second, it names the <a href="/wiki/Normative" title="Normative">normative</a> study of reasoning or a branch thereof.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PopkinStroll1993_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-PopkinStroll1993-2">2</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jacquette2002_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-jacquette2002-3">3</a></sup> In the latter sense, it features most prominently in the subjects of <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">computer science</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reasoning
<p><b>Reason</b> is the capacity for <a href="/wiki/Consciousness" title="Consciousness">consciously</a> making sense of things, applying <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>, for establishing and verifying <a href="/wiki/Fact" title="Fact">facts</a>, and changing or justifying practices, institutions, and <a href="/wiki/Belief" title="Belief">beliefs</a> based on new or existing information.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup> It is closely associated with such characteristically <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">human</a> activities as <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a>, <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">language</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Art" title="Art">art</a>, and is normally considered to be a definitive characteristic of <a href="/wiki/Human_nature" title="Human nature">human nature</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">2</a></sup> The concept of reason is sometimes referred to as <a href="/wiki/Rationality" title="Rationality">rationality</a> and sometimes as <b>discursive reason</b>, in opposition to <a href="/wiki/Intuition_(psychology)" title="Intuition">intuitive reason</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">3</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Consciousness
<p><b>Consciousness</b> is the <a href="/wiki/Quality_(philosophy)" title="Quality">quality</a> or state of being aware of an external object or something within oneself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-van_Gulick2004_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-van_Gulick2004-2">2</a></sup> It has been defined as: <a href="/wiki/Sentience" title="Sentience">sentience</a>, <a href="/wiki/Awareness" title="Awareness">awareness</a>, <a href="/wiki/Subjectivity" title="Subjectivity">subjectivity</a>, the ability to <a href="/wiki/Experience" title="Experience">experience</a> or to <a href="/wiki/Feeling" title="Feeling">feel</a>, wakefulness, having a sense of <a href="/wiki/Self" title="Self">selfhood</a>, and the executive control system of the mind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Farthing1992Psychology_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Farthing1992Psychology-3">3</a></sup> Despite the difficulty in definition, many philosophers believe that there is a broadly shared underlying intuition about what consciousness is.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">4</a></sup> As <a href="/wiki/Max_Velmans" title="Max Velmans">Max Velmans</a> and Susan Schneider wrote in : "Anything that we are aware of at a given moment forms part of our consciousness, making conscious experience at once the most familiar and most mysterious aspect of our lives."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">5</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quality_(philosophy)
<p>A <b>quality</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup> is an attribute or a <a href="/wiki/Property_(philosophy)" title="Property">property</a>. Attributes are ascribable, by a subject, whereas properties are possessible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cargile.2C_1995_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cargile.2C_1995-2">2</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[]</sup> In contemporary <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a> the idea of qualities, and especially how to distinguish certain kinds of qualities from one another, remains controversial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cargile.2C_1995_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cargile.2C_1995-2">2</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Property_(philosophy)
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Modern_philosophy" title="Modern philosophy">modern philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>, a <b>property</b> is an attribute of an <a href="/wiki/Object_(philosophy)" title="Object">object</a>; a red object is said to have the property of redness. The property may be considered a form of object in its own right, able to possess other properties. A property however differs from individual objects in that it may be instantiated, and often in more than one thing. It differs from the logical/mathematical concept of <a href="/wiki/Class_(set_theory)" title="Class">class</a> by not having any concept of <a href="/wiki/Extensionality" title="Extensionality">extensionality</a>, and from the philosophical concept of <a href="/wiki/Class_(philosophy)" title="Class">class</a> in that a property is considered to be distinct from the objects which possess it. Understanding how different individual entities can in some sense have some of the same properties is the basis of the <a href="/wiki/Problem_of_universals" title="Problem of universals">problem of universals</a>. The terms <b>attribute</b> and <b>quality</b> have similar meanings.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Modern_philosophy
<p><b>Modern philosophy</b> is a category of <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a> that originated in <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western Europe</a> in the 17th century, and is now common worldwide. It is not a specific doctrine or school, although there are certain assumptions common to much of it, which helps to distinguish it from earlier <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup></p>

http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nadim_Sabagh
<p><b>Nadim Sabagh</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Syrian_people" title="Syrian people">Syrian</a> <a href="/wiki/Association_football" title="Association football">footballer</a>. He currently plays for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arbil_SC" title="Arbil SC">Arbil</a>, which competes in the <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_Premier_League" title="Iraqi Premier League">Iraqi Premier League</a> the top division in <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>. He plays as a <a href="/wiki/Defender_(association_football)" title="Defender">defender</a>, wearing the number 12 jersey for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arbil_SC" title="Arbil SC">Arbil</a> and for the <a href="/wiki/Syria_national_football_team" title="Syria national football team">Syrian national football team</a> he wears the number 13 shirt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Syrian_people
<p>The <b>Syrian people</b> are the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inhabitants" title="Inhabitants">inhabitants</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Citizen" title="Citizen">citizens</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syrian_Arab_Republic" title="Syrian Arab Republic">Syrian Arab Republic</a>. Most modern-day Syrians are commonly described as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arab" title="Arab">Arabs</a> due to some Arab ancestry, language, and bonds to <a href="/wiki/Arab_culture" title="Arab culture">Arab culture</a> and history. However, there are <a href="/wiki/Kurdish_people" title="Kurdish people">Kurds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syrian_Turks" title="Syrian Turks">Turkmens</a>, <a href="/wiki/Circassians_in_Syria" title="Circassians in Syria">Circassians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Christianity" title="Syriac Christianity">Syriacs</a>/<a href="/wiki/Assyrian_people" title="Assyrian people">Assyrians</a> and other minorities, who also reside in Syria and express the Syrian National identity. Many Syrians live outside of Syria, and they stay connected to their cultural roots by visiting their homeland, forming and participating in Arab and Syrian communities in their new countries, listening to <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Syria" title="Music of Syria">Syrian music</a>, watching <a href="/wiki/Television_in_Syria" title="Television in Syria">Syrian Television</a>, and preparing <a href="/wiki/Syrian_cuisine" title="Syrian cuisine">Syrian cuisine</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inhabitants
<p><b>Residency</b> is the act of establishing or maintaining a <a href="/wiki/House" title="House">residence</a> in a given place. Residency is a concept which heavily affects a the legal rights and responsibilities that are available to a person, including eligibility to vote, eligibility to stand for political office, eligibility to access government services, responsibility to pay <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taxation" title="Taxation">taxes</a>, and on and so forth.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/House
<p>A <b>house</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Building" title="Building">building</a> that functions as a <a href="/wiki/Home" title="Home">home</a> for <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">humans</a> or other creatures,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">2</a></sup> including simple <a href="/wiki/Dwelling" title="Dwelling">dwellings</a> ranging from rudimentary huts of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nomadic_tribes" title="Nomadic tribes">nomadic tribes</a> and complex structures composed of many systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schoenauer_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-schoenauer-3">3</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">4</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Building
<p>A <b>building</b> is a man-made <a href="/wiki/Structure#Physical_structure" title="Structure">structure</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Roof" title="Roof">roof</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wall" title="Wall">walls</a> standing more or less permanently in one place. Buildings come in a variety of shapes, sizes and functions, and have been adapted throughout history for a wide number of factors, from building materials available, to weather conditions, to land prices, ground conditions, specific uses and aesthetic reasons. To better understand the term  compare the list of <a href="/wiki/Nonbuilding_structure" title="Nonbuilding structure">nonbuilding structures</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Structure#Physical_structure
<p><b>Structure</b> is a fundamental, tangible or intangible notion referring to the <a href="/wiki/Pattern_recognition" title="Pattern recognition">recognition</a>, <a href="/wiki/Observation" title="Observation">observation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nature_(philosophy)" title="Nature">nature</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Object_permanence" title="Object permanence">permanence</a> of <a href="/wiki/Pattern" title="Pattern">patterns</a> and <a class="extiw" href="//en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Relationship" title="wiktionary:Relationship">relationships</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Entities" title="Entities">entities</a>. This notion may itself be an object, such as a built structure, or an attribute, such as the structure of society. From a child's verbal description of a <a href="/wiki/Snow" title="Snow">snowflake</a>, to the detailed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scientific_analysis" title="Scientific analysis">scientific analysis</a> of the properties of <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic fields</a>, the concept of structure is now often an essential foundation of nearly every mode of inquiry and discovery in <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Art" title="Art">art</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pullan000_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pullan000-1">1</a></sup> In early 20th-century and earlier thought, <a class="extiw" href="//en.wiktionary.org/wiki/form" title="wikt:form">form</a> often plays a role comparable to that of structure in contemporary thought. The <a href="/wiki/Neo-Kantianism" title="Neo-Kantianism">neo-Kantianism</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Cassirer" title="Ernst Cassirer">Ernst Cassirer</a> is sometimes regarded as a precursor of the later shift to <a href="/wiki/Structuralism" title="Structuralism">structuralism</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Poststructuralism" title="Poststructuralism">poststructuralism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">2</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pattern_recognition
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Machine_learning" title="Machine learning">machine learning</a>, <b>pattern recognition</b> is the assignment of a label to a given input value. An example of pattern recognition is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Classification_(machine_learning)" title="Classification">classification</a>, which attempts to assign each input value to one of a given set of . However, pattern recognition is a more general problem that encompasses other types of output as well. Other examples are <a href="/wiki/Regression_analysis" title="Regression analysis">regression</a>, which assigns a real-valued output to each input; <a href="/wiki/Sequence_labeling" title="Sequence labeling">sequence labeling</a>, which assigns a class to each member of a sequence of values; and <a href="/wiki/Parsing" title="Parsing">parsing</a>, which assigns a <a href="/wiki/Parse_tree" title="Parse tree">parse tree</a> to an input sentence, describing the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syntactic_structure" title="Syntactic structure">syntactic structure</a> of the sentence.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Machine_learning
<p><b>Machine learning</b>, a branch of <a href="/wiki/Artificial_intelligence" title="Artificial intelligence">artificial intelligence</a>, concerns the construction and study of systems that can <a href="/wiki/Learning" title="Learning">learn</a> from data. For example, a machine learning system could be trained on email messages to learn to distinguish between spam and non-spam messages. After learning, it can then be used to classify new email messages into spam and non-spam folders.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_intelligence
<p><b>Artificial intelligence</b> is the intelligence exhibited by machines or software, and the branch of <a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">computer science</a> that develops machines and software with intelligence. Major AI researchers and textbooks define the field as "the study and design of intelligent agents",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Definition_of_AI_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Definition_of_AI-1">1</a></sup> where an <a href="/wiki/Intelligent_agent" title="Intelligent agent">intelligent agent</a> is a system that perceives its environment and takes actions that maximize its chances of success.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Intelligent_agents_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Intelligent_agents-2">2</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_McCarthy_(computer_scientist)" title="John McCarthy">John McCarthy</a>, who coined the term in 1955,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coining_of_the_term_AI_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Coining_of_the_term_AI-3">3</a></sup> defines it as "the science and engineering of making intelligent machines".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McCarthy.27s_definition_of_AI_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-McCarthy.27s_definition_of_AI-4">4</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Computer_science
<p><b>Computer science</b> is the <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">scientific</a> and practical approach to <a href="/wiki/Computation" title="Computation">computation</a> and its applications. It is the systematic study of the feasibility, structure, expression, and mechanization of the methodical processes that underlie the acquisition, representation, processing, storage, communication of, and access to information, whether such information is encoded in bits and bytes in a computer memory or transcribed engines and protein structures in a human cell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Computer_scientist" title="Computer scientist">computer scientist</a> specializes in the theory of computation and the design of computational systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">2</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Science
<p><b>Science</b> is a systematic enterprise that builds and organizes <a href="/wiki/Knowledge" title="Knowledge">knowledge</a> in the form of testable explanations and predictions about the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wilson_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-wilson-2">2</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">3</a></sup> In an older and closely related meaning, "science" also refers to a body of knowledge itself, of the type that can be rationally explained and reliably applied. A practitioner of science is known as a <a href="/wiki/Scientist" title="Scientist">scientist</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Knowledge
<p><b>Knowledge</b> is a familiarity with someone or something, which can include <a href="/wiki/Fact" title="Fact">facts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Information" title="Information">information</a>, <a href="/wiki/Description" title="Description">descriptions</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Skills" title="Skills">skills</a> acquired through <a href="/wiki/Experience" title="Experience">experience</a> or <a href="/wiki/Education" title="Education">education</a>. It can refer to the theoretical or practical understanding of a subject. It can be implicit or explicit; it can be more or less formal or systematic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>, the study of knowledge is called <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">epistemology</a>; the philosopher <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> famously defined knowledge as "<a href="/wiki/Justified_true_belief" title="Justified true belief">justified true belief</a>." However, no single agreed upon definition of knowledge exists, though there are numerous theories to explain it.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fact
<p>A <b>fact</b> is something that has really occurred or is actually the case. The usual test for a statement of fact is verifiability, that is whether it can be <a href="/wiki/Proof_(truth)" title="Proof">proven</a> to correspond to <a href="/wiki/Experience" title="Experience">experience</a>. Standard reference works are often used to check facts. <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">Scientific</a> facts are verified by repeatable <a href="/wiki/Experiment" title="Experiment">experiments</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proof_(truth)
<p>A <b>proof</b> is <a href="/wiki/Necessity_and_sufficiency" title="Necessity and sufficiency">sufficient</a> <a href="/wiki/Evidence" title="Evidence">evidence</a> or an <a href="/wiki/Argument" title="Argument">argument</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Truth" title="Truth">truth</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Proposition" title="Proposition">proposition</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">2</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">3</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">4</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Necessity_and_sufficiency
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>, <b>necessity</b> and <b>sufficiency</b> are implicational relationships between <a href="/wiki/Statement_(logic)" title="Statement">statements</a>.<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[]</sup> The assertion that one statement is a  <a href="/wiki/Condition_(philosophy)" title="Condition">condition</a> of another means that the former statement is true <a href="/wiki/If_and_only_if" title="If and only if">if and only if</a> the latter is true. That is, the two statements must be either simultaneously true or simultaneously false. In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ordinary_English" title="Ordinary English">ordinary English</a>, ‘necessary’ and ‘sufficient’ indicate relations between <a href="/wiki/Condition_(philosophy)" title="Condition">conditions</a> or states of affairs, not statements. Being male sibling is a necessary and sufficient condition for being a brother. Fred’s being a male sibling is necessary and sufficient for the truth of the statement that Fred is a brother.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Logic
<p><b>Logic</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-argumentative_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-argumentative-1">1</a></sup> has two meanings: first, it describes the use of valid <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reasoning" title="Reasoning">reasoning</a> in some activity; second, it names the <a href="/wiki/Normative" title="Normative">normative</a> study of reasoning or a branch thereof.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PopkinStroll1993_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-PopkinStroll1993-2">2</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jacquette2002_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-jacquette2002-3">3</a></sup> In the latter sense, it features most prominently in the subjects of <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">computer science</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reasoning
<p><b>Reason</b> is the capacity for <a href="/wiki/Consciousness" title="Consciousness">consciously</a> making sense of things, applying <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>, for establishing and verifying <a href="/wiki/Fact" title="Fact">facts</a>, and changing or justifying practices, institutions, and <a href="/wiki/Belief" title="Belief">beliefs</a> based on new or existing information.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup> It is closely associated with such characteristically <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">human</a> activities as <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a>, <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">language</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Art" title="Art">art</a>, and is normally considered to be a definitive characteristic of <a href="/wiki/Human_nature" title="Human nature">human nature</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">2</a></sup> The concept of reason is sometimes referred to as <a href="/wiki/Rationality" title="Rationality">rationality</a> and sometimes as <b>discursive reason</b>, in opposition to <a href="/wiki/Intuition_(psychology)" title="Intuition">intuitive reason</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">3</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Consciousness
<p><b>Consciousness</b> is the <a href="/wiki/Quality_(philosophy)" title="Quality">quality</a> or state of being aware of an external object or something within oneself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-van_Gulick2004_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-van_Gulick2004-2">2</a></sup> It has been defined as: <a href="/wiki/Sentience" title="Sentience">sentience</a>, <a href="/wiki/Awareness" title="Awareness">awareness</a>, <a href="/wiki/Subjectivity" title="Subjectivity">subjectivity</a>, the ability to <a href="/wiki/Experience" title="Experience">experience</a> or to <a href="/wiki/Feeling" title="Feeling">feel</a>, wakefulness, having a sense of <a href="/wiki/Self" title="Self">selfhood</a>, and the executive control system of the mind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Farthing1992Psychology_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Farthing1992Psychology-3">3</a></sup> Despite the difficulty in definition, many philosophers believe that there is a broadly shared underlying intuition about what consciousness is.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">4</a></sup> As <a href="/wiki/Max_Velmans" title="Max Velmans">Max Velmans</a> and Susan Schneider wrote in : "Anything that we are aware of at a given moment forms part of our consciousness, making conscious experience at once the most familiar and most mysterious aspect of our lives."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">5</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quality_(philosophy)
<p>A <b>quality</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup> is an attribute or a <a href="/wiki/Property_(philosophy)" title="Property">property</a>. Attributes are ascribable, by a subject, whereas properties are possessible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cargile.2C_1995_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cargile.2C_1995-2">2</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[]</sup> In contemporary <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a> the idea of qualities, and especially how to distinguish certain kinds of qualities from one another, remains controversial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cargile.2C_1995_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cargile.2C_1995-2">2</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Property_(philosophy)
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Modern_philosophy" title="Modern philosophy">modern philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>, a <b>property</b> is an attribute of an <a href="/wiki/Object_(philosophy)" title="Object">object</a>; a red object is said to have the property of redness. The property may be considered a form of object in its own right, able to possess other properties. A property however differs from individual objects in that it may be instantiated, and often in more than one thing. It differs from the logical/mathematical concept of <a href="/wiki/Class_(set_theory)" title="Class">class</a> by not having any concept of <a href="/wiki/Extensionality" title="Extensionality">extensionality</a>, and from the philosophical concept of <a href="/wiki/Class_(philosophy)" title="Class">class</a> in that a property is considered to be distinct from the objects which possess it. Understanding how different individual entities can in some sense have some of the same properties is the basis of the <a href="/wiki/Problem_of_universals" title="Problem of universals">problem of universals</a>. The terms <b>attribute</b> and <b>quality</b> have similar meanings.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Modern_philosophy
<p><b>Modern philosophy</b> is a category of <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a> that originated in <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western Europe</a> in the 17th century, and is now common worldwide. It is not a specific doctrine or school, although there are certain assumptions common to much of it, which helps to distinguish it from earlier <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup></p>

http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kongahawela
<p><b>Kongahawela</b> is a village in <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a>. It is located within <a href="/wiki/Central_Province,_Sri_Lanka" title="Central Province, Sri Lanka">Central Province</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sri_Lanka
<p><b>Sri Lanka</b>, officially the <b>Democratic Socialist Republic of Sri Lanka</b>, is an island country in the northern <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Ocean</a> off the southern coast of the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a> in <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South Asia</a>; known until 1972 as <b>Ceylon</b>, Sri Lanka has maritime borders with <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> to the northwest and the <a href="/wiki/Maldives" title="Maldives">Maldives</a> to the southwest.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indian_Ocean
<p>The <b>Indian Ocean</b> is the third largest of the world's <a href="/wiki/Ocean" title="Ocean">oceanic</a> divisions, covering approximately 20% of the <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>'s surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rasul_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rasul-1">1</a></sup> It is bounded by <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a>—including <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, after which the ocean is named<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">2</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">3</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">4</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">5</a></sup>—on the north, on the west by <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>, on the east by <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, and on the south by the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Ocean" title="Southern Ocean">Southern Ocean</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">6</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ocean
<p>An <b>ocean</b> is a body of <a href="/wiki/Saline_water" title="Saline water">saline water</a> that composes much of a <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planet</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Hydrosphere" title="Hydrosphere">hydrosphere</a>, occupying more than two-thirds of Earth's surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">2</a></sup> On <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>, an ocean is one or all of the major divisions of the planet's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_Ocean" title="World Ocean">World Ocean</a> – which are, in descending order of area, the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific</a>, <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Southern_Ocean" title="Southern Ocean">Southern</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Arctic_Ocean" title="Arctic Ocean">Arctic</a> Oceans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">3</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">4</a></sup> The word  is often used interchangeably with "ocean" in <a href="/wiki/American_English" title="American English">American English</a> but, strictly speaking, a sea is a body of saline water that land partly or fully encloses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">5</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saline_water
<p><b>Saline water</b> is a general term for <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a> that contains a significant concentration of <a href="/wiki/Solvation" title="Solvation">dissolved</a> <a href="/wiki/Salt" title="Salt">salts</a>. The salt concentration is usually expressed in parts per thousand or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parts_per_million" title="Parts per million">parts per million</a>. The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Geological_Survey" title="United States Geological Survey">United States Geological Survey</a> classifies saline water in three salinity categories. Salt concentration in slightly saline water is around 1,000 to 3,000 ppm, in moderately saline water 3,000 to 10,000 ppm and in highly saline water 10,000 to 35,000 ppm. <a href="/wiki/Seawater" title="Seawater">Seawater</a> has a <a href="/wiki/Salinity" title="Salinity">salinity</a> of roughly 35,000 ppm, equivalent to 35 grams of salt per one liter of water. The saturation level is dependent on the temperature of the water. At freezing one mililiter of water can dissolve about 357 milligrams of salt; a concentration of 35.7%. At boiling the amount that can be dissolved in a mililiter of water increases to about 390 milligrams or 39% saline solution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Water
<p><b>Water</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_compound" title="Chemical compound">chemical compound</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Chemical_formula" title="Chemical formula">chemical formula</a> <b></b>. A <a href="/wiki/Properties_of_water" title="Properties of water">water molecule</a> contains one <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> and two <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atoms</a> that are connected by <a href="/wiki/Covalent_bond" title="Covalent bond">covalent bonds</a>. Water is a <a href="/wiki/Liquid" title="Liquid">liquid</a> at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_ambient_temperature_and_pressure" title="Standard ambient temperature and pressure">standard ambient temperature and pressure</a>, but it often co-exists on <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> with its <a href="/wiki/Solid" title="Solid">solid</a> state, <a href="/wiki/Ice" title="Ice">ice</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gas" title="Gas">gaseous</a> state, <a href="/wiki/Steam" title="Steam">steam</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chemical_compound
<p>A <b>chemical compound</b> is a pure <a href="/wiki/Chemical_substance" title="Chemical substance">chemical substance</a> consisting of two or more different <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical elements</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown_p.6_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brown_p.6-1">1</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hill_p.6_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hill_p.6-2">2</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Whitten_p.15_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Whitten_p.15-3">3</a></sup> that can be separated into simpler substances by <a href="/wiki/Chemical_reaction" title="Chemical reaction">chemical reactions</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wilbraham_p.36_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wilbraham_p.36-4">4</a></sup> Chemical compounds have a unique and defined <a href="/wiki/Chemical_structure" title="Chemical structure">chemical structure</a>; they consist of a fixed ratio of atoms<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Whitten_p.15_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Whitten_p.15-3">3</a></sup> that are held together in a defined spatial arrangement by <a href="/wiki/Chemical_bond" title="Chemical bond">chemical bonds</a>. Chemical compounds can be <a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">molecular</a> compounds held together by <a href="/wiki/Covalent_bond" title="Covalent bond">covalent bonds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Salt_(chemistry)" title="Salt">salts</a> held together by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ionic_bond" title="Ionic bond">ionic bonds</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intermetallic_compounds" title="Intermetallic compounds">intermetallic compounds</a> held together by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metallic_bond" title="Metallic bond">metallic bonds</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Complex_(chemistry)" title="Complex">complexes</a> held together by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coordinate_covalent_bond" title="Coordinate covalent bond">coordinate covalent bonds</a>. Pure <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical elements</a> are not considered chemical compounds, even if they consist of molecules that contain only multiple atoms of a single element,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Halal_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Halal-5">5</a></sup> which are called <a href="/wiki/Diatomic_molecule" title="Diatomic molecule">diatomic molecules</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polyatomic_molecule" title="Polyatomic molecule">polyatomic molecules</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chemical_substance
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Chemistry" title="Chemistry">chemistry</a>, a <b>chemical substance</b> is a form of <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">matter</a> that has constant <a href="/wiki/Chemical_compound" title="Chemical compound">chemical composition</a> and characteristic properties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup> It cannot be separated into components by physical separation methods, i.e. without breaking chemical bonds. It can be solid, liquid, gas, or plasma.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chemistry
<p><b>Chemistry</b>, a branch of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physical_science" title="Physical science">physical science</a>, is the study of the composition, structure, properties and change of <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">matter</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-definition_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-definition-1">1</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">2</a></sup> Chemistry is chiefly concerned with <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atoms</a> and their interactions with other atoms - for example, the properties of the <a href="/wiki/Chemical_bond" title="Chemical bond">chemical bonds</a> formed between atoms to create <a href="/wiki/Chemical_compound" title="Chemical compound">chemical compounds</a>. As well as this, interactions including atoms and other phenomena—<a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a> and various forms of <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a>—are considered, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photochemical_reaction" title="Photochemical reaction">photochemical reactions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">oxidation-reduction reactions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phase_transition" title="Phase transition">changes in phases of matter</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Separation_process" title="Separation process">separation of mixtures</a>. Finally, properties of matter such as <a href="/wiki/Alloy" title="Alloy">alloys</a> or <a href="/wiki/Polymer" title="Polymer">polymers</a> are considered.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Physical_science
<p><b>Physical science</b> is the study of <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chemistry" title="Chemistry">chemistry</a> of nature.<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[]</sup> From the materialist and functionalist viewpoints it overlaps the life sciences where ecology studies the evidences of historical facts or evolution. <a href="/wiki/Natural_science" title="Natural science">Natural sciences</a> bridge the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phenomena" title="Phenomena">phenomena</a> in the physical sciences to the <a href="/wiki/Noumenon" title="Noumenon">noumenon</a> in the life sciences. The following is presented as an overview and topical guide of these physical sciences. </p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Physics
<p><b>Physics</b> is the <a href="/wiki/Natural_science" title="Natural science">natural science</a> that involves the study of <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">matter</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-feynman_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-feynman-6">6</a></sup> and its <a href="/wiki/Motion_(physics)" title="Motion">motion</a> through space and time, along with related concepts such as <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Force" title="Force">force</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">7</a></sup> More broadly, it is the general analysis of <a href="/wiki/Nature" title="Nature">nature</a>, conducted in order to understand how the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a> behaves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">8</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">9</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">10</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Natural_science
<p>The <b>natural sciences</b> are the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sciences" title="Sciences">sciences</a> that seek to elucidate the rules that govern the natural world through scientific methods, the cornerstone of which is <a href="/wiki/Measurement" title="Measurement">measured</a> by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Numerical_data" title="Numerical data">quantitative</a> data.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELedoux200234_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELedoux200234-1">1</a></sup> Based on <a href="/wiki/Formal_science" title="Formal science">formal sciences</a>, they also attempt to provide mathematical <a href="/wiki/Scientific_modelling" title="Scientific modelling">models</a> of natural processes. The term "natural science" is used to distinguish the subject from the <a href="/wiki/Social_science" title="Social science">social sciences</a>, such as economics, psychology and sociology, which apply the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific method</a> to the study of human behavior and social patterns; the <a href="/wiki/Humanities" title="Humanities">humanities</a>, which use a critical or analytical approach to study the human condition; and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Formal_sciences" title="Formal sciences">formal sciences</a> such as mathematics and logic, which use an <a href="/wiki/A_priori_and_a_posteriori" title="A priori and a posteriori">a priori</a>, as opposed to empirical methodology to study formal systems.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sciences
<p><b>Science</b> is a systematic enterprise that builds and organizes <a href="/wiki/Knowledge" title="Knowledge">knowledge</a> in the form of testable explanations and predictions about the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wilson_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-wilson-2">2</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">3</a></sup> In an older and closely related meaning, "science" also refers to a body of knowledge itself, of the type that can be rationally explained and reliably applied. A practitioner of science is known as a <a href="/wiki/Scientist" title="Scientist">scientist</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Knowledge
<p><b>Knowledge</b> is a familiarity with someone or something, which can include <a href="/wiki/Fact" title="Fact">facts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Information" title="Information">information</a>, <a href="/wiki/Description" title="Description">descriptions</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Skills" title="Skills">skills</a> acquired through <a href="/wiki/Experience" title="Experience">experience</a> or <a href="/wiki/Education" title="Education">education</a>. It can refer to the theoretical or practical understanding of a subject. It can be implicit or explicit; it can be more or less formal or systematic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>, the study of knowledge is called <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">epistemology</a>; the philosopher <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> famously defined knowledge as "<a href="/wiki/Justified_true_belief" title="Justified true belief">justified true belief</a>." However, no single agreed upon definition of knowledge exists, though there are numerous theories to explain it.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fact
<p>A <b>fact</b> is something that has really occurred or is actually the case. The usual test for a statement of fact is verifiability, that is whether it can be <a href="/wiki/Proof_(truth)" title="Proof">proven</a> to correspond to <a href="/wiki/Experience" title="Experience">experience</a>. Standard reference works are often used to check facts. <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">Scientific</a> facts are verified by repeatable <a href="/wiki/Experiment" title="Experiment">experiments</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proof_(truth)
<p>A <b>proof</b> is <a href="/wiki/Necessity_and_sufficiency" title="Necessity and sufficiency">sufficient</a> <a href="/wiki/Evidence" title="Evidence">evidence</a> or an <a href="/wiki/Argument" title="Argument">argument</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Truth" title="Truth">truth</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Proposition" title="Proposition">proposition</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">2</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">3</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">4</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Necessity_and_sufficiency
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>, <b>necessity</b> and <b>sufficiency</b> are implicational relationships between <a href="/wiki/Statement_(logic)" title="Statement">statements</a>.<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[]</sup> The assertion that one statement is a  <a href="/wiki/Condition_(philosophy)" title="Condition">condition</a> of another means that the former statement is true <a href="/wiki/If_and_only_if" title="If and only if">if and only if</a> the latter is true. That is, the two statements must be either simultaneously true or simultaneously false. In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ordinary_English" title="Ordinary English">ordinary English</a>, ‘necessary’ and ‘sufficient’ indicate relations between <a href="/wiki/Condition_(philosophy)" title="Condition">conditions</a> or states of affairs, not statements. Being male sibling is a necessary and sufficient condition for being a brother. Fred’s being a male sibling is necessary and sufficient for the truth of the statement that Fred is a brother.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Logic
<p><b>Logic</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-argumentative_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-argumentative-1">1</a></sup> has two meanings: first, it describes the use of valid <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reasoning" title="Reasoning">reasoning</a> in some activity; second, it names the <a href="/wiki/Normative" title="Normative">normative</a> study of reasoning or a branch thereof.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PopkinStroll1993_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-PopkinStroll1993-2">2</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jacquette2002_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-jacquette2002-3">3</a></sup> In the latter sense, it features most prominently in the subjects of <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">computer science</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reasoning
<p><b>Reason</b> is the capacity for <a href="/wiki/Consciousness" title="Consciousness">consciously</a> making sense of things, applying <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>, for establishing and verifying <a href="/wiki/Fact" title="Fact">facts</a>, and changing or justifying practices, institutions, and <a href="/wiki/Belief" title="Belief">beliefs</a> based on new or existing information.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup> It is closely associated with such characteristically <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">human</a> activities as <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a>, <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">language</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Art" title="Art">art</a>, and is normally considered to be a definitive characteristic of <a href="/wiki/Human_nature" title="Human nature">human nature</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">2</a></sup> The concept of reason is sometimes referred to as <a href="/wiki/Rationality" title="Rationality">rationality</a> and sometimes as <b>discursive reason</b>, in opposition to <a href="/wiki/Intuition_(psychology)" title="Intuition">intuitive reason</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">3</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Consciousness
<p><b>Consciousness</b> is the <a href="/wiki/Quality_(philosophy)" title="Quality">quality</a> or state of being aware of an external object or something within oneself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-van_Gulick2004_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-van_Gulick2004-2">2</a></sup> It has been defined as: <a href="/wiki/Sentience" title="Sentience">sentience</a>, <a href="/wiki/Awareness" title="Awareness">awareness</a>, <a href="/wiki/Subjectivity" title="Subjectivity">subjectivity</a>, the ability to <a href="/wiki/Experience" title="Experience">experience</a> or to <a href="/wiki/Feeling" title="Feeling">feel</a>, wakefulness, having a sense of <a href="/wiki/Self" title="Self">selfhood</a>, and the executive control system of the mind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Farthing1992Psychology_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Farthing1992Psychology-3">3</a></sup> Despite the difficulty in definition, many philosophers believe that there is a broadly shared underlying intuition about what consciousness is.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">4</a></sup> As <a href="/wiki/Max_Velmans" title="Max Velmans">Max Velmans</a> and Susan Schneider wrote in : "Anything that we are aware of at a given moment forms part of our consciousness, making conscious experience at once the most familiar and most mysterious aspect of our lives."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">5</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quality_(philosophy)
<p>A <b>quality</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup> is an attribute or a <a href="/wiki/Property_(philosophy)" title="Property">property</a>. Attributes are ascribable, by a subject, whereas properties are possessible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cargile.2C_1995_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cargile.2C_1995-2">2</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[]</sup> In contemporary <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a> the idea of qualities, and especially how to distinguish certain kinds of qualities from one another, remains controversial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cargile.2C_1995_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cargile.2C_1995-2">2</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Property_(philosophy)
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Modern_philosophy" title="Modern philosophy">modern philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>, a <b>property</b> is an attribute of an <a href="/wiki/Object_(philosophy)" title="Object">object</a>; a red object is said to have the property of redness. The property may be considered a form of object in its own right, able to possess other properties. A property however differs from individual objects in that it may be instantiated, and often in more than one thing. It differs from the logical/mathematical concept of <a href="/wiki/Class_(set_theory)" title="Class">class</a> by not having any concept of <a href="/wiki/Extensionality" title="Extensionality">extensionality</a>, and from the philosophical concept of <a href="/wiki/Class_(philosophy)" title="Class">class</a> in that a property is considered to be distinct from the objects which possess it. Understanding how different individual entities can in some sense have some of the same properties is the basis of the <a href="/wiki/Problem_of_universals" title="Problem of universals">problem of universals</a>. The terms <b>attribute</b> and <b>quality</b> have similar meanings.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Modern_philosophy
<p><b>Modern philosophy</b> is a category of <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a> that originated in <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western Europe</a> in the 17th century, and is now common worldwide. It is not a specific doctrine or school, although there are certain assumptions common to much of it, which helps to distinguish it from earlier <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup></p>
