http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Latirus_alboapicatus
<p> is a <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a> of <a href="/wiki/Sea_snail" title="Sea snail">sea snail</a>, a marine <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gastropod" title="Gastropod">gastropod</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mollusk" title="Mollusk">mollusk</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Family_(biology)" title="Family">family</a> <a href="/wiki/Fasciolariidae" title="Fasciolariidae">Fasciolariidae</a>, the spindle snails, the tulip snails and their allies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WoRMS_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-WoRMS-1">1</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Species
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biology</a>, a <b>species</b> is one of the basic units of <a href="/wiki/Biological_classification" title="Biological classification">biological classification</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Taxonomic_rank" title="Taxonomic rank">taxonomic rank</a>. A species is often defined as the largest group of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organisms" title="Organisms">organisms</a> capable of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interbreeding" title="Interbreeding">interbreeding</a> and <a href="/wiki/Reproduction" title="Reproduction">producing</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fertile" title="Fertile">fertile</a> <a href="/wiki/Offspring" title="Offspring">offspring</a>. While in many cases this definition is adequate, the difficulty of defining species is known as the <a href="/wiki/Species_problem" title="Species problem">species problem</a>. Differing measures are often used, such as similarity of DNA, morphology, or ecological niche. Presence of specific locally adapted traits may further subdivide species into "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Infraspecific_taxa" title="Infraspecific taxa">infraspecific taxa</a>" such as <a href="/wiki/Subspecies" title="Subspecies">subspecies</a>" title="Variety">varieties</a>, subvarieties, and <a href="/wiki/Form_(botany)" title="Form">formae</a>).</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biology
<p><b>Biology</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Natural_science" title="Natural science">natural science</a> concerned with the study of <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">life</a> and <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">living organisms</a>, including their structure, function, growth, <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolution</a>, distribution, and <a href="/wiki/Taxonomy_(biology)" title="Taxonomy">taxonomy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aquarenagloss_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-aquarenagloss-1">1</a></sup> Modern biology is a vast and eclectic field, composed of many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_biology_disciplines#Branches_of_biology" title="List of biology disciplines">branches and subdisciplines</a>. However, despite the broad scope of biology, there are certain general and unifying concepts within it that govern all study and research, consolidating it into single, coherent field. In general, biology recognizes the <a href="/wiki/Cell_(biology)" title="Cell">cell</a> as the basic unit of life, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genes" title="Genes">genes</a> as the basic unit of <a href="/wiki/Heredity" title="Heredity">heredity</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolution</a> as the engine that propels the synthesis and creation of new <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a>. It is also understood today that all organisms survive by consuming and transforming <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a> and by <a href="/wiki/Homeostasis" title="Homeostasis">regulating</a> their internal environment to maintain a stable and vital condition.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Natural_science
<p>The <b>natural sciences</b> are the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sciences" title="Sciences">sciences</a> that seek to elucidate the rules that govern the natural world through scientific methods, the cornerstone of which is <a href="/wiki/Measurement" title="Measurement">measured</a> by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Numerical_data" title="Numerical data">quantitative</a> data.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELedoux200234_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELedoux200234-1">1</a></sup> Based on <a href="/wiki/Formal_science" title="Formal science">formal sciences</a>, they also attempt to provide mathematical <a href="/wiki/Scientific_modelling" title="Scientific modelling">models</a> of natural processes. The term "natural science" is used to distinguish the subject from the <a href="/wiki/Social_science" title="Social science">social sciences</a>, such as economics, psychology and sociology, which apply the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific method</a> to the study of human behavior and social patterns; the <a href="/wiki/Humanities" title="Humanities">humanities</a>, which use a critical or analytical approach to study the human condition; and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Formal_sciences" title="Formal sciences">formal sciences</a> such as mathematics and logic, which use an <a href="/wiki/A_priori_and_a_posteriori" title="A priori and a posteriori">a priori</a>, as opposed to empirical methodology to study formal systems.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sciences
<p><b>Science</b> is a systematic enterprise that builds and organizes <a href="/wiki/Knowledge" title="Knowledge">knowledge</a> in the form of testable explanations and predictions about the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wilson_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-wilson-2">2</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">3</a></sup> In an older and closely related meaning, "science" also refers to a body of knowledge itself, of the type that can be rationally explained and reliably applied. A practitioner of science is known as a <a href="/wiki/Scientist" title="Scientist">scientist</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Knowledge
<p><b>Knowledge</b> is a familiarity with someone or something, which can include <a href="/wiki/Fact" title="Fact">facts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Information" title="Information">information</a>, <a href="/wiki/Description" title="Description">descriptions</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Skills" title="Skills">skills</a> acquired through <a href="/wiki/Experience" title="Experience">experience</a> or <a href="/wiki/Education" title="Education">education</a>. It can refer to the theoretical or practical understanding of a subject. It can be implicit or explicit; it can be more or less formal or systematic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>, the study of knowledge is called <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">epistemology</a>; the philosopher <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> famously defined knowledge as "<a href="/wiki/Justified_true_belief" title="Justified true belief">justified true belief</a>." However, no single agreed upon definition of knowledge exists, though there are numerous theories to explain it.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fact
<p>A <b>fact</b> is something that has really occurred or is actually the case. The usual test for a statement of fact is verifiability, that is whether it can be <a href="/wiki/Proof_(truth)" title="Proof">proven</a> to correspond to <a href="/wiki/Experience" title="Experience">experience</a>. Standard reference works are often used to check facts. <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">Scientific</a> facts are verified by repeatable <a href="/wiki/Experiment" title="Experiment">experiments</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proof_(truth)
<p>A <b>proof</b> is <a href="/wiki/Necessity_and_sufficiency" title="Necessity and sufficiency">sufficient</a> <a href="/wiki/Evidence" title="Evidence">evidence</a> or an <a href="/wiki/Argument" title="Argument">argument</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Truth" title="Truth">truth</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Proposition" title="Proposition">proposition</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">2</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">3</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">4</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Necessity_and_sufficiency
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>, <b>necessity</b> and <b>sufficiency</b> are implicational relationships between <a href="/wiki/Statement_(logic)" title="Statement">statements</a>.<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[]</sup> The assertion that one statement is a  <a href="/wiki/Condition_(philosophy)" title="Condition">condition</a> of another means that the former statement is true <a href="/wiki/If_and_only_if" title="If and only if">if and only if</a> the latter is true. That is, the two statements must be either simultaneously true or simultaneously false. In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ordinary_English" title="Ordinary English">ordinary English</a>, ‘necessary’ and ‘sufficient’ indicate relations between <a href="/wiki/Condition_(philosophy)" title="Condition">conditions</a> or states of affairs, not statements. Being male sibling is a necessary and sufficient condition for being a brother. Fred’s being a male sibling is necessary and sufficient for the truth of the statement that Fred is a brother.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Logic
<p><b>Logic</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-argumentative_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-argumentative-1">1</a></sup> has two meanings: first, it describes the use of valid <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reasoning" title="Reasoning">reasoning</a> in some activity; second, it names the <a href="/wiki/Normative" title="Normative">normative</a> study of reasoning or a branch thereof.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PopkinStroll1993_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-PopkinStroll1993-2">2</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jacquette2002_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-jacquette2002-3">3</a></sup> In the latter sense, it features most prominently in the subjects of <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">computer science</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reasoning
<p><b>Reason</b> is the capacity for <a href="/wiki/Consciousness" title="Consciousness">consciously</a> making sense of things, applying <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>, for establishing and verifying <a href="/wiki/Fact" title="Fact">facts</a>, and changing or justifying practices, institutions, and <a href="/wiki/Belief" title="Belief">beliefs</a> based on new or existing information.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup> It is closely associated with such characteristically <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">human</a> activities as <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a>, <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">language</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Art" title="Art">art</a>, and is normally considered to be a definitive characteristic of <a href="/wiki/Human_nature" title="Human nature">human nature</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">2</a></sup> The concept of reason is sometimes referred to as <a href="/wiki/Rationality" title="Rationality">rationality</a> and sometimes as <b>discursive reason</b>, in opposition to <a href="/wiki/Intuition_(psychology)" title="Intuition">intuitive reason</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">3</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Consciousness
<p><b>Consciousness</b> is the <a href="/wiki/Quality_(philosophy)" title="Quality">quality</a> or state of being aware of an external object or something within oneself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-van_Gulick2004_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-van_Gulick2004-2">2</a></sup> It has been defined as: <a href="/wiki/Sentience" title="Sentience">sentience</a>, <a href="/wiki/Awareness" title="Awareness">awareness</a>, <a href="/wiki/Subjectivity" title="Subjectivity">subjectivity</a>, the ability to <a href="/wiki/Experience" title="Experience">experience</a> or to <a href="/wiki/Feeling" title="Feeling">feel</a>, wakefulness, having a sense of <a href="/wiki/Self" title="Self">selfhood</a>, and the executive control system of the mind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Farthing1992Psychology_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Farthing1992Psychology-3">3</a></sup> Despite the difficulty in definition, many philosophers believe that there is a broadly shared underlying intuition about what consciousness is.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">4</a></sup> As <a href="/wiki/Max_Velmans" title="Max Velmans">Max Velmans</a> and Susan Schneider wrote in : "Anything that we are aware of at a given moment forms part of our consciousness, making conscious experience at once the most familiar and most mysterious aspect of our lives."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">5</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quality_(philosophy)
<p>A <b>quality</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup> is an attribute or a <a href="/wiki/Property_(philosophy)" title="Property">property</a>. Attributes are ascribable, by a subject, whereas properties are possessible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cargile.2C_1995_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cargile.2C_1995-2">2</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[]</sup> In contemporary <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a> the idea of qualities, and especially how to distinguish certain kinds of qualities from one another, remains controversial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cargile.2C_1995_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cargile.2C_1995-2">2</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Property_(philosophy)
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Modern_philosophy" title="Modern philosophy">modern philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>, a <b>property</b> is an attribute of an <a href="/wiki/Object_(philosophy)" title="Object">object</a>; a red object is said to have the property of redness. The property may be considered a form of object in its own right, able to possess other properties. A property however differs from individual objects in that it may be instantiated, and often in more than one thing. It differs from the logical/mathematical concept of <a href="/wiki/Class_(set_theory)" title="Class">class</a> by not having any concept of <a href="/wiki/Extensionality" title="Extensionality">extensionality</a>, and from the philosophical concept of <a href="/wiki/Class_(philosophy)" title="Class">class</a> in that a property is considered to be distinct from the objects which possess it. Understanding how different individual entities can in some sense have some of the same properties is the basis of the <a href="/wiki/Problem_of_universals" title="Problem of universals">problem of universals</a>. The terms <b>attribute</b> and <b>quality</b> have similar meanings.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Modern_philosophy
<p><b>Modern philosophy</b> is a category of <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a> that originated in <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western Europe</a> in the 17th century, and is now common worldwide. It is not a specific doctrine or school, although there are certain assumptions common to much of it, which helps to distinguish it from earlier <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Philosophy

http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tolka_Row
<p> is an Irish <a href="/wiki/Soap_opera" title="Soap opera">soap opera</a> set in a fictional <a href="/wiki/Housing_estate" title="Housing estate">housing estate</a> on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northside_(Dublin)" title="Northside">northside</a> of <a href="/wiki/Dublin" title="Dublin">Dublin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup> Based on <a href="/wiki/Maura_Laverty" title="Maura Laverty">Maura Laverty</a>'s play of the same name,  was first broadcast on 3 January 1964 and aired weekly for five series until it ended on 31 May 1968.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-indo_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-indo-2">2</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soap_opera
<p>A <b>soap opera</b>, often referred to simply as a <b>soap</b>, is a serial drama, on television or radio, that features related story lines dealing with the lives of multiple characters. The stories in these series typically focus heavily on emotional relationships to the point of <a href="/wiki/Melodrama" title="Melodrama">melodrama</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup> The name  stems from the fact that many of the sponsors and producers of the original dramatic serials' broadcast on radio were soap manufacturers, such as <a href="/wiki/Dial_Corporation" title="Dial Corporation">Dial Corporation</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Procter_and_Gamble#Productions" title="Procter and Gamble">Procter &amp; Gamble</a>, <a href="/wiki/Colgate-Palmolive" title="Colgate-Palmolive">Colgate-Palmolive</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lever_Brothers" title="Lever Brothers">Lever Brothers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowles-118_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bowles-118-2">2</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">3</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Melodrama
<p>The term  refers to a <a href="/wiki/Drama" title="Drama">dramatic</a> work that puts characters in a lot of danger in order to appeal to the emotions. It may also refer to the <a href="/wiki/Genre" title="Genre">genre</a> which includes such works, or to language, behavior, or events which resemble them. It is based around having the same character in every scene, often a hero, damsel in distress, a villain. It is also used in scholarly and historical musical contexts to refer to dramas of the 18th and 19th centuries in which orchestral music or song was used to accompany the action. The term originated from the early 19th-century <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> word , which is derived from <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> , music, and French , drama.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">2</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">3</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Drama
<p><b>Drama</b> is the specific <a href="/wiki/Mode_(literature)" title="Mode">mode</a> of <a href="/wiki/Fiction" title="Fiction">fiction</a> <a href="/wiki/Mimesis" title="Mimesis">represented</a> in <a href="/wiki/Performance" title="Performance">performance</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-elam98_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-elam98-1">1</a></sup> The term comes from a <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Greek</a> word meaning "<a href="/wiki/Action_(philosophy)" title="Action">action</a>", which is derived from the verb meaning "to do" or "to act". The enactment of drama in <a href="/wiki/Theatre" title="Theatre">theatre</a>, performed by <a href="/wiki/Actor" title="Actor">actors</a> on a <a href="/wiki/Stage_(theatre)" title="Stage">stage</a> before an <a href="/wiki/Audience" title="Audience">audience</a>, presupposes <a href="/wiki/Collaboration" title="Collaboration">collaborative</a> modes of production and a <a href="/wiki/Collective" title="Collective">collective</a> form of reception. The <a href="/wiki/Dramatic_structure" title="Dramatic structure">structure of dramatic texts</a>, unlike other forms of <a href="/wiki/Literature" title="Literature">literature</a>, is directly influenced by this collaborative production and collective reception.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">2</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/English_Renaissance_theatre" title="English Renaissance theatre">early modern</a> <a href="/wiki/Tragedy" title="Tragedy">tragedy</a>  by <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">Shakespeare</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Theatre_of_ancient_Greece" title="Theatre of ancient Greece">classical Athenian</a> tragedy  by <a href="/wiki/Sophocles" title="Sophocles">Sophocles</a> are among the masterpieces of the art of drama.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">3</a></sup> A modern example is  by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eugene_O%E2%80%99Neill" title="Eugene O’Neill">Eugene O’Neill</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">4</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mode_(literature)
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Literature" title="Literature">literature</a>, a <b>mode</b> is an employed method or approach, identifiable within a written work. As descriptive terms, <a class="extiw" href="//en.wiktionary.org/wiki/form" title="wikt:form">form</a> and <a href="/wiki/Genre" title="Genre">genre</a> are often used inaccurately instead of <b>mode</b>; for example, the <a href="/wiki/Pastoral" title="Pastoral">pastoral</a> mode is often mistakenly identified as a <a href="/wiki/Genre" title="Genre">genre</a>. The Writers Web site feature, , defines <b>mode</b> thus:</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Literature
<p><b>Literature</b>; letter) is the art of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Written" title="Written">written</a> work. The word literature literally means: "things made from letters". Literature is commonly classified as having two major forms—fiction and non-fiction—and two major techniques—poetry and prose.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Written
<p><b>Writing</b> is a medium of <a href="/wiki/Communication" title="Communication">communication</a> that represents <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">language</a> through the inscription of signs and symbols. In most languages, writing is a complement to speech or spoken language. Within a language system, writing relies on many of the same structures as speech, such as <a href="/wiki/Vocabulary" title="Vocabulary">vocabulary</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grammar" title="Grammar">grammar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Semantics" title="Semantics">semantics</a>, with the added dependency of a system of signs or symbols, usually in the form of a formal <a href="/wiki/Alphabet" title="Alphabet">alphabet</a>. The result of writing is generally called , and the recipient of text is called a reader. Motivations for writing include <a href="/wiki/Publication" title="Publication">publication</a>, <a href="/wiki/Storytelling" title="Storytelling">storytelling</a>, <a href="/wiki/Correspondence_chess" title="Correspondence chess">correspondence</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diary" title="Diary">diary</a>. Writing has been instrumental in keeping <a href="/wiki/History" title="History">history</a>, dissemination of knowledge through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_media" title="The media">the media</a> and the formation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Legal_systems" title="Legal systems">legal systems</a>. Under the influence of technologies such as data storage and computer networks, the pace of correspondence and potential for collaboration increased.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Communication
<p><b>Communication</b> is the activity of conveying <a href="/wiki/Information" title="Information">information</a> through the exchange of thoughts, messages, or information, as by speech, visuals, signals, writing, or behavior. It is the meaningful exchange of information between two or more living creatures.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Information
<p><b>Information</b>, in its most restricted technical sense, is a <a href="/wiki/Sequence" title="Sequence">sequence</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Symbols" title="Symbols">symbols</a> that can be interpreted as a <a href="/wiki/Message" title="Message">message</a>. Information can be recorded as <a href="/wiki/Sign_(semiotics)" title="Sign">signs</a>, or transmitted as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Signal_(electronics)" title="Signal">signals</a>. Information is any kind of <a class="extiw" href="//en.wiktionary.org/wiki/event" title="wikt:event">event</a> that affects the <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamic_state" title="Thermodynamic state">state</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Dynamical_system" title="Dynamical system">dynamic system</a> that can interpret the information.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sequence
<p>A <b>sequence</b> is an ordered list. Like a <a href="/wiki/Set_(mathematics)" title="Set">set</a>, it contains <a href="/wiki/Element_(mathematics)" title="Element">members</a>. The number of ordered elements is called the  of the sequence. Unlike a set, order matters, and exactly the same elements can appear multiple times at different positions in the sequence. Most precisely, a sequence can be defined as a <a href="/wiki/Function_(mathematics)" title="Function">function</a> whose domain is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Countable" title="Countable">countable</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Totally_ordered" title="Totally ordered">totally ordered</a> set, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_numbers" title="Natural numbers">natural numbers</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Set_(mathematics)
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, a <b>set</b> is a collection of distinct objects, considered as an <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_object" title="Mathematical object">object</a> in its own right. For example, the numbers 2, 4, and 6 are distinct objects when considered separately, but when they are considered collectively they form a single set of size three, written {2,4,6}. Sets are one of the most fundamental concepts in mathematics. Developed at the end of the 19th century, set theory is now a ubiquitous part of mathematics, and can be used as a foundation from which nearly all of mathematics can be derived. In <a href="/wiki/Mathematics_education" title="Mathematics education">mathematics education</a>, elementary topics such as <a href="/wiki/Venn_diagram" title="Venn diagram">Venn diagrams</a> are taught at a young age, while more advanced concepts are taught as part of a university degree. The term itself was coined by Bolzano in his work <a href="/wiki/The_Paradoxes_of_the_Infinite" title="The Paradoxes of the Infinite">The Paradoxes of the Infinite</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics
<p><b>Mathematics</b> is the abstract study of topics such as <a href="/wiki/Quantity" title="Quantity">quantity</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-OED-2">2</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Structure" title="Structure">structure</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kneebone_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kneebone-3">3</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Space" title="Space">space</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-OED-2">2</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Calculus" title="Calculus">change</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LaTorre_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-LaTorre-4">4</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ramana_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ramana-5">5</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ziegler_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ziegler-6">6</a></sup> There is a range of views among mathematicians and philosophers as to the exact scope and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Definition_of_mathematics" title="Definition of mathematics">definition of mathematics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mura_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mura-7">7</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Runge_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Runge-8">8</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantity
<p><b>Quantity</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Property_(philosophy)" title="Property">property</a> that can exist as a <a href="/wiki/Magnitude_(mathematics)" title="Magnitude">magnitude</a> or <a href="/wiki/Counting" title="Counting">multitude</a>. Quantities can be compared in terms of "more", "less" or "equal", or by assigning a numerical value in terms of a unit of measurement. Quantity is among the basic <a href="/wiki/Class_(philosophy)" title="Class">classes</a> of things along with <a href="/wiki/Quality_(philosophy)" title="Quality">quality</a>, <a href="/wiki/Substance_theory" title="Substance theory">substance</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Change_(mathematics)" title="Change">change</a>, and relation. Being a fundamental term, quantity is used to refer to any type of quantitative properties or attributes of things. Some quantities are such by their inner nature, while others are functioning as states of things such as heavy and light, long and short, broad and narrow, small and great, or much and little. A small quantity is sometimes referred to as a <b>quantulum</b>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Property_(philosophy)
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Modern_philosophy" title="Modern philosophy">modern philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>, a <b>property</b> is an attribute of an <a href="/wiki/Object_(philosophy)" title="Object">object</a>; a red object is said to have the property of redness. The property may be considered a form of object in its own right, able to possess other properties. A property however differs from individual objects in that it may be instantiated, and often in more than one thing. It differs from the logical/mathematical concept of <a href="/wiki/Class_(set_theory)" title="Class">class</a> by not having any concept of <a href="/wiki/Extensionality" title="Extensionality">extensionality</a>, and from the philosophical concept of <a href="/wiki/Class_(philosophy)" title="Class">class</a> in that a property is considered to be distinct from the objects which possess it. Understanding how different individual entities can in some sense have some of the same properties is the basis of the <a href="/wiki/Problem_of_universals" title="Problem of universals">problem of universals</a>. The terms <b>attribute</b> and <b>quality</b> have similar meanings.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Modern_philosophy
<p><b>Modern philosophy</b> is a category of <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a> that originated in <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western Europe</a> in the 17th century, and is now common worldwide. It is not a specific doctrine or school, although there are certain assumptions common to much of it, which helps to distinguish it from earlier <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup></p>

<p><b>Dennis Praet</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgian</a> <a href="/wiki/Association_football" title="Association football">footballer</a> who plays as a <a href="/wiki/Midfielder" title="Midfielder">midfielder</a> for <a href="/wiki/R.S.C._Anderlecht" title="R.S.C. Anderlecht">Anderlecht</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Belgian_Pro_League" title="Belgian Pro League">Belgian Pro League</a>. http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Belgium
<p><b>Belgium</b>, officially the <b>Kingdom of Belgium</b>, is a <a href="/wiki/Federation" title="Federation">federal</a> <a href="/wiki/Monarchy" title="Monarchy">monarchy</a> in <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western Europe</a>. It is a founding member of the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> and hosts the EU's headquarters as well as those of several other major international organisations such as <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Note-1_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Note-1-6">nb 1</a></sup> Belgium covers an area of 30,528 square kilometres, and it has a population of about 11 million people.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Federation
<p>A <b>federation</b>, also known as a <b>federal state</b>, is a political entity characterized by a <a href="/wiki/Political_union" title="Political union">union</a> of partially <a href="/wiki/Federated_state" title="Federated state">self-governing states</a> or regions under a central government. In a federation, the self-governing status of the component states, as well as the division of power between them and the central government, are typically constitutionally entrenched and may not be altered by a unilateral decision of either party, the states or the federal political body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Political_union
<p>A <b>political union</b> is a type of <a href="/wiki/State_(polity)" title="State">state</a> which is composed of or created out of smaller <a href="/wiki/Federated_state" title="Federated state">states</a>. The process is called <b>unification</b>. Unifications of states that used to be together and are reuniting is referred to as <b>reunification</b>. Unlike a <a href="/wiki/Personal_union" title="Personal union">personal union</a>, the individual states share a <a href="/wiki/Central_government" title="Central government">central government</a> and the union is <a href="/wiki/Diplomatic_recognition" title="Diplomatic recognition">recognized internationally</a> as a single political entity. A political union may also be called a legislative union or state union.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/State_(polity)
<p>A <b>state</b> is an organized <a href="/wiki/Community" title="Community">community</a> living under one <a href="/wiki/Government" title="Government">government</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oxford-state_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-oxford-state-1">1</a></sup> States may be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sovereign" title="Sovereign">sovereign</a>. The denomination state is also employed to <a href="/wiki/Federated_state" title="Federated state">federated states</a> that are members of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Federal_union" title="Federal union">federal union</a>, which is the <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_state" title="Sovereign state">sovereign state</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oxford-state_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-oxford-state-1">1</a></sup> Some states are subject to external <a href="/wiki/Sovereignty" title="Sovereignty">sovereignty</a> or <a href="/wiki/Hegemony" title="Hegemony">hegemony</a> where ultimate sovereignty lies in another state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">2</a></sup> The state can also be used to refer to the secular branches of government within a state, often as a manner of contrasting them with churches and <a href="/wiki/Civil_society" title="Civil society">civilian institutions</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Community
<p>The term <b>community</b> has two distinct meanings: 1) Community can refer to a usually small, <a href="/wiki/Level_of_analysis#Meso-level" title="Level of analysis">social unit</a> of any size that shares common <a href="/wiki/Value_(personal_and_cultural)" title="Value">values</a>. The term can also refer to the <a href="/wiki/Nation" title="Nation">national community</a> or <a href="/wiki/International_community" title="International community">international community</a>, and 2) in <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biology</a>, a community is a group of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interacting" title="Interacting">interacting</a> <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">living</a> <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">organisms</a> sharing a populated <a href="/wiki/Environment_(biophysical)" title="Environment">environment</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Level_of_analysis#Meso-level
<p>The term "<b>level of analysis</b>" is a social sciences term that points to the location, size, or scale of a research target. "Level of analysis" is distinct from the term "<a href="/wiki/Unit_of_observation" title="Unit of observation">unit of observation</a>" in that the former refers to a more or less integrated set of relationships while the latter refers to the distinct unit from which data have been or will be gathered. Together, the <a href="/wiki/Unit_of_observation" title="Unit of observation">unit of observation</a> and the level of analysis help define the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sample_population" title="Sample population">population</a> of a research enterprise.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Unit_of_observation
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Statistics" title="Statistics">statistics</a>, a <b>unit of observation</b> is the unit described by the <a href="/wiki/Data" title="Data">data</a> that one analyzes. For example, in a study of the <a href="/wiki/Demand_for_money" title="Demand for money">demand for money</a>, the unit of observation might be chosen as the individual, with different observations for a given point in time differing as to which individual they refer to; or the unit of observation might be the country, with different observations differing only in regard to the country they refer to. A study may have a differing unit of observation and <a href="/wiki/Unit_of_analysis" title="Unit of analysis">unit of analysis</a>: for example, in <a href="/wiki/Community" title="Community">community</a> research, the <a href="/wiki/Research_design" title="Research design">research design</a> may collect data at the individual level of observation but the <a href="/wiki/Unit_of_analysis" title="Unit of analysis">level of analysis</a> might be at the neighborhood level, drawing conclusions on neighborhood characteristics from data collected from individuals. Together, the unit of observation and the <a href="/wiki/Unit_of_analysis" title="Unit of analysis">level of analysis</a> define the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sample_population" title="Sample population">population</a> of a research enterprise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Statistics
<p><b>Statistics</b> is the study of the collection, organization, analysis, interpretation and presentation of <a href="/wiki/Data" title="Data">data</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dodge_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dodge-1">1</a></sup> It deals with all aspects of data including the planning of data collection in terms of the design of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Statistical_survey" title="Statistical survey">surveys</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Experimental_design" title="Experimental design">experiments</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dodge_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dodge-1">1</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Data
<p><b>Data</b> is a set of <a href="/wiki/Value_(computer_science)" title="Value">values</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qualitative_data" title="Qualitative data">qualitative</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quantitative_data" title="Quantitative data">quantitative</a> <a href="/wiki/Variable_and_attribute_(research)" title="Variable and attribute">variables</a>; restated, data are individual pieces of <a href="/wiki/Information" title="Information">information</a>. Data in <a href="/wiki/Computing" title="Computing">computing</a> are represented in a <a href="/wiki/Structure" title="Structure">structure</a> that is often <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tabular" title="Tabular">tabular</a>" title="Row">rows</a> and <a href="/wiki/Column_(database)" title="Column">columns</a>), a <a href="/wiki/Tree_structure" title="Tree structure">tree</a>" title="Set">set</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Node_(graph_theory)" title="Node">nodes</a> with <a href="/wiki/Parent" title="Parent">parent</a>-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Children" title="Children">children</a> <a href="/wiki/Relation_(database)" title="Relation">relationship</a>), or a <a href="/wiki/Graph_(mathematics)" title="Graph">graph</a>. Data are typically the results of <a href="/wiki/Measurement" title="Measurement">measurements</a> and can be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Data_visualisation" title="Data visualisation">visualised</a> using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Graph_(data_structure)" title="Graph">graphs</a> or <a href="/wiki/Image" title="Image">images</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Value_(computer_science)
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">computer science</a>, a <b>value</b> is an <a href="/wiki/Expression_(computer_science)" title="Expression">expression</a> which cannot be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evaluation_(computer_science)" title="Evaluation">evaluated</a> any further" title="Normal form">normal form</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMitchell199692_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMitchell199692-1">1</a></sup> The members of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Type_(computer_science)" title="Type">type</a> are the values of that type.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMitchell19969_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMitchell19969-2">2</a></sup> For example, the expression "1 + 2" is not a value as it can be <a href="/wiki/Reduction_(mathematics)" title="Reduction">reduced</a> to the expression "3". This expression cannot be reduced any further and therefore is a value.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Computer_science
<p><b>Computer science</b> is the <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">scientific</a> and practical approach to <a href="/wiki/Computation" title="Computation">computation</a> and its applications. It is the systematic study of the feasibility, structure, expression, and mechanization of the methodical processes that underlie the acquisition, representation, processing, storage, communication of, and access to information, whether such information is encoded in bits and bytes in a computer memory or transcribed engines and protein structures in a human cell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Computer_scientist" title="Computer scientist">computer scientist</a> specializes in the theory of computation and the design of computational systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">2</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Science
<p><b>Science</b> is a systematic enterprise that builds and organizes <a href="/wiki/Knowledge" title="Knowledge">knowledge</a> in the form of testable explanations and predictions about the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wilson_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-wilson-2">2</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">3</a></sup> In an older and closely related meaning, "science" also refers to a body of knowledge itself, of the type that can be rationally explained and reliably applied. A practitioner of science is known as a <a href="/wiki/Scientist" title="Scientist">scientist</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Knowledge
<p><b>Knowledge</b> is a familiarity with someone or something, which can include <a href="/wiki/Fact" title="Fact">facts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Information" title="Information">information</a>, <a href="/wiki/Description" title="Description">descriptions</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Skills" title="Skills">skills</a> acquired through <a href="/wiki/Experience" title="Experience">experience</a> or <a href="/wiki/Education" title="Education">education</a>. It can refer to the theoretical or practical understanding of a subject. It can be implicit or explicit; it can be more or less formal or systematic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>, the study of knowledge is called <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">epistemology</a>; the philosopher <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> famously defined knowledge as "<a href="/wiki/Justified_true_belief" title="Justified true belief">justified true belief</a>." However, no single agreed upon definition of knowledge exists, though there are numerous theories to explain it.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fact
<p>A <b>fact</b> is something that has really occurred or is actually the case. The usual test for a statement of fact is verifiability, that is whether it can be <a href="/wiki/Proof_(truth)" title="Proof">proven</a> to correspond to <a href="/wiki/Experience" title="Experience">experience</a>. Standard reference works are often used to check facts. <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">Scientific</a> facts are verified by repeatable <a href="/wiki/Experiment" title="Experiment">experiments</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proof_(truth)
<p>A <b>proof</b> is <a href="/wiki/Necessity_and_sufficiency" title="Necessity and sufficiency">sufficient</a> <a href="/wiki/Evidence" title="Evidence">evidence</a> or an <a href="/wiki/Argument" title="Argument">argument</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Truth" title="Truth">truth</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Proposition" title="Proposition">proposition</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">2</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">3</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">4</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Necessity_and_sufficiency
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>, <b>necessity</b> and <b>sufficiency</b> are implicational relationships between <a href="/wiki/Statement_(logic)" title="Statement">statements</a>.<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[]</sup> The assertion that one statement is a  <a href="/wiki/Condition_(philosophy)" title="Condition">condition</a> of another means that the former statement is true <a href="/wiki/If_and_only_if" title="If and only if">if and only if</a> the latter is true. That is, the two statements must be either simultaneously true or simultaneously false. In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ordinary_English" title="Ordinary English">ordinary English</a>, ‘necessary’ and ‘sufficient’ indicate relations between <a href="/wiki/Condition_(philosophy)" title="Condition">conditions</a> or states of affairs, not statements. Being male sibling is a necessary and sufficient condition for being a brother. Fred’s being a male sibling is necessary and sufficient for the truth of the statement that Fred is a brother.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Logic
<p><b>Logic</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-argumentative_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-argumentative-1">1</a></sup> has two meanings: first, it describes the use of valid <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reasoning" title="Reasoning">reasoning</a> in some activity; second, it names the <a href="/wiki/Normative" title="Normative">normative</a> study of reasoning or a branch thereof.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PopkinStroll1993_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-PopkinStroll1993-2">2</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jacquette2002_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-jacquette2002-3">3</a></sup> In the latter sense, it features most prominently in the subjects of <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">computer science</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reasoning
<p><b>Reason</b> is the capacity for <a href="/wiki/Consciousness" title="Consciousness">consciously</a> making sense of things, applying <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>, for establishing and verifying <a href="/wiki/Fact" title="Fact">facts</a>, and changing or justifying practices, institutions, and <a href="/wiki/Belief" title="Belief">beliefs</a> based on new or existing information.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup> It is closely associated with such characteristically <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">human</a> activities as <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a>, <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">language</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Art" title="Art">art</a>, and is normally considered to be a definitive characteristic of <a href="/wiki/Human_nature" title="Human nature">human nature</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">2</a></sup> The concept of reason is sometimes referred to as <a href="/wiki/Rationality" title="Rationality">rationality</a> and sometimes as <b>discursive reason</b>, in opposition to <a href="/wiki/Intuition_(psychology)" title="Intuition">intuitive reason</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">3</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Consciousness
<p><b>Consciousness</b> is the <a href="/wiki/Quality_(philosophy)" title="Quality">quality</a> or state of being aware of an external object or something within oneself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-van_Gulick2004_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-van_Gulick2004-2">2</a></sup> It has been defined as: <a href="/wiki/Sentience" title="Sentience">sentience</a>, <a href="/wiki/Awareness" title="Awareness">awareness</a>, <a href="/wiki/Subjectivity" title="Subjectivity">subjectivity</a>, the ability to <a href="/wiki/Experience" title="Experience">experience</a> or to <a href="/wiki/Feeling" title="Feeling">feel</a>, wakefulness, having a sense of <a href="/wiki/Self" title="Self">selfhood</a>, and the executive control system of the mind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Farthing1992Psychology_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Farthing1992Psychology-3">3</a></sup> Despite the difficulty in definition, many philosophers believe that there is a broadly shared underlying intuition about what consciousness is.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">4</a></sup> As <a href="/wiki/Max_Velmans" title="Max Velmans">Max Velmans</a> and Susan Schneider wrote in : "Anything that we are aware of at a given moment forms part of our consciousness, making conscious experience at once the most familiar and most mysterious aspect of our lives."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">5</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quality_(philosophy)
<p>A <b>quality</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup> is an attribute or a <a href="/wiki/Property_(philosophy)" title="Property">property</a>. Attributes are ascribable, by a subject, whereas properties are possessible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cargile.2C_1995_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cargile.2C_1995-2">2</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[]</sup> In contemporary <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a> the idea of qualities, and especially how to distinguish certain kinds of qualities from one another, remains controversial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cargile.2C_1995_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cargile.2C_1995-2">2</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Property_(philosophy)
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Modern_philosophy" title="Modern philosophy">modern philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>, a <b>property</b> is an attribute of an <a href="/wiki/Object_(philosophy)" title="Object">object</a>; a red object is said to have the property of redness. The property may be considered a form of object in its own right, able to possess other properties. A property however differs from individual objects in that it may be instantiated, and often in more than one thing. It differs from the logical/mathematical concept of <a href="/wiki/Class_(set_theory)" title="Class">class</a> by not having any concept of <a href="/wiki/Extensionality" title="Extensionality">extensionality</a>, and from the philosophical concept of <a href="/wiki/Class_(philosophy)" title="Class">class</a> in that a property is considered to be distinct from the objects which possess it. Understanding how different individual entities can in some sense have some of the same properties is the basis of the <a href="/wiki/Problem_of_universals" title="Problem of universals">problem of universals</a>. The terms <b>attribute</b> and <b>quality</b> have similar meanings.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Modern_philosophy
<p><b>Modern philosophy</b> is a category of <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a> that originated in <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western Europe</a> in the 17th century, and is now common worldwide. It is not a specific doctrine or school, although there are certain assumptions common to much of it, which helps to distinguish it from earlier <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup></p>

<p><br/></p>
<p>"<b>Sunday in the Park with George</b>" is the 21st episode of the <a href="/wiki/American_Broadcasting_Company" title="American Broadcasting Company">ABC</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Television_series" title="Television series">television series</a>, . The episode was the 21st episode for the show's first season. The episode was written by <a href="/wiki/Katie_Ford_(screenwriter)" title="Katie Ford">Katie Ford</a> and was directed by <a href="/wiki/Larry_Shaw_(director)" title="Larry Shaw">Larry Shaw</a>. It originally aired on Sunday, May 8, 2005.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_Broadcasting_Company
<p>The <b>American Broadcasting Company</b> is an <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">American</a> media company that owns a <a href="/wiki/Commercial_broadcasting" title="Commercial broadcasting">commercial broadcast</a> <a href="/wiki/Television_network" title="Television network">television network</a>, a number of specialty channels, and various independent production and distribution companies. Throughout its history and to support its financial operations, ABC has diversified into the press, the publishing industry, the operation of theaters, and filmmaking. Many of the company's assets in these fields have been sold to other companies, and since 2007, when <a href="/wiki/Cumulus_Media_Networks" title="Cumulus Media Networks">ABC Radio</a> was sold to <a href="/wiki/Citadel_Broadcasting" title="Citadel Broadcasting">Citadel Broadcasting</a>, ABC has reduced its operations almost exclusively to television.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States
<p>The <b>United States of America</b>, commonly referred to as the <b>United States</b>, <b>America</b>, or simply <b>the States</b>, is a <a href="/wiki/Federal_republic" title="Federal republic">federal republic</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">10</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">11</a></sup> consisting of 50 <a href="/wiki/U.S._state" title="U.S. state">states</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">federal district</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Contiguous_United_States" title="Contiguous United States">48 contiguous states</a> and the federal district of <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a>, are in central <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a> between <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>. The state of <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a> is the northwestern part of North America and the state of <a href="/wiki/Hawaii" title="Hawaii">Hawaii</a> is an <a href="/wiki/Archipelago" title="Archipelago">archipelago</a> in the mid-<a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific</a>. The country also has five populated and nine unpopulated <a href="/wiki/Territories_of_the_United_States" title="Territories of the United States">territories</a> in the Pacific and the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">Caribbean</a>. At 3.79 million square miles in total and with around 317 million people, the United States is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_and_dependencies_by_area" title="List of countries and dependencies by area">fourth-largest country by total area</a> and third largest by <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_population" title="List of countries by population">population</a>. It is one of the world's most <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multiethnic_society" title="Multiethnic society">ethnically diverse</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multicultural" title="Multicultural">multicultural</a> nations, the product of large-scale <a href="/wiki/Immigration_to_the_United_States" title="Immigration to the United States">immigration from many countries</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DD_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-DD-12">12</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_the_United_States" title="Geography of the United States">geography</a> and <a href="/wiki/Climate_of_the_United_States" title="Climate of the United States">climate</a> of the United States is also extremely diverse, and it is home to a wide variety of wildlife.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Federal_republic
<p>A <b>federal republic</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Federation" title="Federation">federation</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State_(administrative_division)" title="State">states</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Republic" title="Republic">republican</a> form of government. Usage of the term  is inconsistent<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[]</sup> but, as a minimum, it means a state or federation of states that does not have a <a href="/wiki/Monarch" title="Monarch">monarch</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Federation
<p>A <b>federation</b>, also known as a <b>federal state</b>, is a political entity characterized by a <a href="/wiki/Political_union" title="Political union">union</a> of partially <a href="/wiki/Federated_state" title="Federated state">self-governing states</a> or regions under a central government. In a federation, the self-governing status of the component states, as well as the division of power between them and the central government, are typically constitutionally entrenched and may not be altered by a unilateral decision of either party, the states or the federal political body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Political_union
<p>A <b>political union</b> is a type of <a href="/wiki/State_(polity)" title="State">state</a> which is composed of or created out of smaller <a href="/wiki/Federated_state" title="Federated state">states</a>. The process is called <b>unification</b>. Unifications of states that used to be together and are reuniting is referred to as <b>reunification</b>. Unlike a <a href="/wiki/Personal_union" title="Personal union">personal union</a>, the individual states share a <a href="/wiki/Central_government" title="Central government">central government</a> and the union is <a href="/wiki/Diplomatic_recognition" title="Diplomatic recognition">recognized internationally</a> as a single political entity. A political union may also be called a legislative union or state union.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/State_(polity)
<p>A <b>state</b> is an organized <a href="/wiki/Community" title="Community">community</a> living under one <a href="/wiki/Government" title="Government">government</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oxford-state_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-oxford-state-1">1</a></sup> States may be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sovereign" title="Sovereign">sovereign</a>. The denomination state is also employed to <a href="/wiki/Federated_state" title="Federated state">federated states</a> that are members of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Federal_union" title="Federal union">federal union</a>, which is the <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_state" title="Sovereign state">sovereign state</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oxford-state_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-oxford-state-1">1</a></sup> Some states are subject to external <a href="/wiki/Sovereignty" title="Sovereignty">sovereignty</a> or <a href="/wiki/Hegemony" title="Hegemony">hegemony</a> where ultimate sovereignty lies in another state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">2</a></sup> The state can also be used to refer to the secular branches of government within a state, often as a manner of contrasting them with churches and <a href="/wiki/Civil_society" title="Civil society">civilian institutions</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Community
<p>The term <b>community</b> has two distinct meanings: 1) Community can refer to a usually small, <a href="/wiki/Level_of_analysis#Meso-level" title="Level of analysis">social unit</a> of any size that shares common <a href="/wiki/Value_(personal_and_cultural)" title="Value">values</a>. The term can also refer to the <a href="/wiki/Nation" title="Nation">national community</a> or <a href="/wiki/International_community" title="International community">international community</a>, and 2) in <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biology</a>, a community is a group of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interacting" title="Interacting">interacting</a> <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">living</a> <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">organisms</a> sharing a populated <a href="/wiki/Environment_(biophysical)" title="Environment">environment</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Level_of_analysis#Meso-level
<p>The term "<b>level of analysis</b>" is a social sciences term that points to the location, size, or scale of a research target. "Level of analysis" is distinct from the term "<a href="/wiki/Unit_of_observation" title="Unit of observation">unit of observation</a>" in that the former refers to a more or less integrated set of relationships while the latter refers to the distinct unit from which data have been or will be gathered. Together, the <a href="/wiki/Unit_of_observation" title="Unit of observation">unit of observation</a> and the level of analysis help define the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sample_population" title="Sample population">population</a> of a research enterprise.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Unit_of_observation
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Statistics" title="Statistics">statistics</a>, a <b>unit of observation</b> is the unit described by the <a href="/wiki/Data" title="Data">data</a> that one analyzes. For example, in a study of the <a href="/wiki/Demand_for_money" title="Demand for money">demand for money</a>, the unit of observation might be chosen as the individual, with different observations for a given point in time differing as to which individual they refer to; or the unit of observation might be the country, with different observations differing only in regard to the country they refer to. A study may have a differing unit of observation and <a href="/wiki/Unit_of_analysis" title="Unit of analysis">unit of analysis</a>: for example, in <a href="/wiki/Community" title="Community">community</a> research, the <a href="/wiki/Research_design" title="Research design">research design</a> may collect data at the individual level of observation but the <a href="/wiki/Unit_of_analysis" title="Unit of analysis">level of analysis</a> might be at the neighborhood level, drawing conclusions on neighborhood characteristics from data collected from individuals. Together, the unit of observation and the <a href="/wiki/Unit_of_analysis" title="Unit of analysis">level of analysis</a> define the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sample_population" title="Sample population">population</a> of a research enterprise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Statistics
<p><b>Statistics</b> is the study of the collection, organization, analysis, interpretation and presentation of <a href="/wiki/Data" title="Data">data</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dodge_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dodge-1">1</a></sup> It deals with all aspects of data including the planning of data collection in terms of the design of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Statistical_survey" title="Statistical survey">surveys</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Experimental_design" title="Experimental design">experiments</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dodge_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dodge-1">1</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Data
<p><b>Data</b> is a set of <a href="/wiki/Value_(computer_science)" title="Value">values</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qualitative_data" title="Qualitative data">qualitative</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quantitative_data" title="Quantitative data">quantitative</a> <a href="/wiki/Variable_and_attribute_(research)" title="Variable and attribute">variables</a>; restated, data are individual pieces of <a href="/wiki/Information" title="Information">information</a>. Data in <a href="/wiki/Computing" title="Computing">computing</a> are represented in a <a href="/wiki/Structure" title="Structure">structure</a> that is often <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tabular" title="Tabular">tabular</a>" title="Row">rows</a> and <a href="/wiki/Column_(database)" title="Column">columns</a>), a <a href="/wiki/Tree_structure" title="Tree structure">tree</a>" title="Set">set</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Node_(graph_theory)" title="Node">nodes</a> with <a href="/wiki/Parent" title="Parent">parent</a>-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Children" title="Children">children</a> <a href="/wiki/Relation_(database)" title="Relation">relationship</a>), or a <a href="/wiki/Graph_(mathematics)" title="Graph">graph</a>. Data are typically the results of <a href="/wiki/Measurement" title="Measurement">measurements</a> and can be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Data_visualisation" title="Data visualisation">visualised</a> using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Graph_(data_structure)" title="Graph">graphs</a> or <a href="/wiki/Image" title="Image">images</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Value_(computer_science)
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">computer science</a>, a <b>value</b> is an <a href="/wiki/Expression_(computer_science)" title="Expression">expression</a> which cannot be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evaluation_(computer_science)" title="Evaluation">evaluated</a> any further" title="Normal form">normal form</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMitchell199692_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMitchell199692-1">1</a></sup> The members of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Type_(computer_science)" title="Type">type</a> are the values of that type.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMitchell19969_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMitchell19969-2">2</a></sup> For example, the expression "1 + 2" is not a value as it can be <a href="/wiki/Reduction_(mathematics)" title="Reduction">reduced</a> to the expression "3". This expression cannot be reduced any further and therefore is a value.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Computer_science
<p><b>Computer science</b> is the <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">scientific</a> and practical approach to <a href="/wiki/Computation" title="Computation">computation</a> and its applications. It is the systematic study of the feasibility, structure, expression, and mechanization of the methodical processes that underlie the acquisition, representation, processing, storage, communication of, and access to information, whether such information is encoded in bits and bytes in a computer memory or transcribed engines and protein structures in a human cell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Computer_scientist" title="Computer scientist">computer scientist</a> specializes in the theory of computation and the design of computational systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">2</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Science
<p><b>Science</b> is a systematic enterprise that builds and organizes <a href="/wiki/Knowledge" title="Knowledge">knowledge</a> in the form of testable explanations and predictions about the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wilson_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-wilson-2">2</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">3</a></sup> In an older and closely related meaning, "science" also refers to a body of knowledge itself, of the type that can be rationally explained and reliably applied. A practitioner of science is known as a <a href="/wiki/Scientist" title="Scientist">scientist</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Knowledge
<p><b>Knowledge</b> is a familiarity with someone or something, which can include <a href="/wiki/Fact" title="Fact">facts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Information" title="Information">information</a>, <a href="/wiki/Description" title="Description">descriptions</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Skills" title="Skills">skills</a> acquired through <a href="/wiki/Experience" title="Experience">experience</a> or <a href="/wiki/Education" title="Education">education</a>. It can refer to the theoretical or practical understanding of a subject. It can be implicit or explicit; it can be more or less formal or systematic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>, the study of knowledge is called <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">epistemology</a>; the philosopher <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> famously defined knowledge as "<a href="/wiki/Justified_true_belief" title="Justified true belief">justified true belief</a>." However, no single agreed upon definition of knowledge exists, though there are numerous theories to explain it.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fact
<p>A <b>fact</b> is something that has really occurred or is actually the case. The usual test for a statement of fact is verifiability, that is whether it can be <a href="/wiki/Proof_(truth)" title="Proof">proven</a> to correspond to <a href="/wiki/Experience" title="Experience">experience</a>. Standard reference works are often used to check facts. <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">Scientific</a> facts are verified by repeatable <a href="/wiki/Experiment" title="Experiment">experiments</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proof_(truth)
<p>A <b>proof</b> is <a href="/wiki/Necessity_and_sufficiency" title="Necessity and sufficiency">sufficient</a> <a href="/wiki/Evidence" title="Evidence">evidence</a> or an <a href="/wiki/Argument" title="Argument">argument</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Truth" title="Truth">truth</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Proposition" title="Proposition">proposition</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">2</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">3</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">4</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Necessity_and_sufficiency
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>, <b>necessity</b> and <b>sufficiency</b> are implicational relationships between <a href="/wiki/Statement_(logic)" title="Statement">statements</a>.<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[]</sup> The assertion that one statement is a  <a href="/wiki/Condition_(philosophy)" title="Condition">condition</a> of another means that the former statement is true <a href="/wiki/If_and_only_if" title="If and only if">if and only if</a> the latter is true. That is, the two statements must be either simultaneously true or simultaneously false. In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ordinary_English" title="Ordinary English">ordinary English</a>, ‘necessary’ and ‘sufficient’ indicate relations between <a href="/wiki/Condition_(philosophy)" title="Condition">conditions</a> or states of affairs, not statements. Being male sibling is a necessary and sufficient condition for being a brother. Fred’s being a male sibling is necessary and sufficient for the truth of the statement that Fred is a brother.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Logic
<p><b>Logic</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-argumentative_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-argumentative-1">1</a></sup> has two meanings: first, it describes the use of valid <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reasoning" title="Reasoning">reasoning</a> in some activity; second, it names the <a href="/wiki/Normative" title="Normative">normative</a> study of reasoning or a branch thereof.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PopkinStroll1993_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-PopkinStroll1993-2">2</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jacquette2002_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-jacquette2002-3">3</a></sup> In the latter sense, it features most prominently in the subjects of <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">computer science</a>.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reasoning
<p><b>Reason</b> is the capacity for <a href="/wiki/Consciousness" title="Consciousness">consciously</a> making sense of things, applying <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>, for establishing and verifying <a href="/wiki/Fact" title="Fact">facts</a>, and changing or justifying practices, institutions, and <a href="/wiki/Belief" title="Belief">beliefs</a> based on new or existing information.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup> It is closely associated with such characteristically <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">human</a> activities as <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a>, <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">language</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Art" title="Art">art</a>, and is normally considered to be a definitive characteristic of <a href="/wiki/Human_nature" title="Human nature">human nature</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">2</a></sup> The concept of reason is sometimes referred to as <a href="/wiki/Rationality" title="Rationality">rationality</a> and sometimes as <b>discursive reason</b>, in opposition to <a href="/wiki/Intuition_(psychology)" title="Intuition">intuitive reason</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">3</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Consciousness
<p><b>Consciousness</b> is the <a href="/wiki/Quality_(philosophy)" title="Quality">quality</a> or state of being aware of an external object or something within oneself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-van_Gulick2004_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-van_Gulick2004-2">2</a></sup> It has been defined as: <a href="/wiki/Sentience" title="Sentience">sentience</a>, <a href="/wiki/Awareness" title="Awareness">awareness</a>, <a href="/wiki/Subjectivity" title="Subjectivity">subjectivity</a>, the ability to <a href="/wiki/Experience" title="Experience">experience</a> or to <a href="/wiki/Feeling" title="Feeling">feel</a>, wakefulness, having a sense of <a href="/wiki/Self" title="Self">selfhood</a>, and the executive control system of the mind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Farthing1992Psychology_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Farthing1992Psychology-3">3</a></sup> Despite the difficulty in definition, many philosophers believe that there is a broadly shared underlying intuition about what consciousness is.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">4</a></sup> As <a href="/wiki/Max_Velmans" title="Max Velmans">Max Velmans</a> and Susan Schneider wrote in : "Anything that we are aware of at a given moment forms part of our consciousness, making conscious experience at once the most familiar and most mysterious aspect of our lives."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">5</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quality_(philosophy)
<p>A <b>quality</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup> is an attribute or a <a href="/wiki/Property_(philosophy)" title="Property">property</a>. Attributes are ascribable, by a subject, whereas properties are possessible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cargile.2C_1995_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cargile.2C_1995-2">2</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[]</sup> In contemporary <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a> the idea of qualities, and especially how to distinguish certain kinds of qualities from one another, remains controversial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cargile.2C_1995_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cargile.2C_1995-2">2</a></sup></p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Property_(philosophy)
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Modern_philosophy" title="Modern philosophy">modern philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>, a <b>property</b> is an attribute of an <a href="/wiki/Object_(philosophy)" title="Object">object</a>; a red object is said to have the property of redness. The property may be considered a form of object in its own right, able to possess other properties. A property however differs from individual objects in that it may be instantiated, and often in more than one thing. It differs from the logical/mathematical concept of <a href="/wiki/Class_(set_theory)" title="Class">class</a> by not having any concept of <a href="/wiki/Extensionality" title="Extensionality">extensionality</a>, and from the philosophical concept of <a href="/wiki/Class_(philosophy)" title="Class">class</a> in that a property is considered to be distinct from the objects which possess it. Understanding how different individual entities can in some sense have some of the same properties is the basis of the <a href="/wiki/Problem_of_universals" title="Problem of universals">problem of universals</a>. The terms <b>attribute</b> and <b>quality</b> have similar meanings.</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Modern_philosophy
<p><b>Modern philosophy</b> is a category of <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a> that originated in <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western Europe</a> in the 17th century, and is now common worldwide. It is not a specific doctrine or school, although there are certain assumptions common to much of it, which helps to distinguish it from earlier <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">1</a></sup></p>

http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Canadians
<p><b>Canadians</b> are the people who are identified with the country of <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>. This connection may be residential, legal, historical, and/or cultural. For most Canadians, several of those types of connections exist and are the source(s) of their being considered .</p>
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Canada
<p><b>Canada</b>  is a country in North America consisting of <a href="/wiki/Provinces_and_territories_of_Canada" title="Provinces and territories of Canada">10 provinces and 3 territories</a>. Located in the northern part of the continent, it extends from the A
